Crew member dies of H1N1
Edmonton Journal
90 words
27 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
C6
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

WELCOME, N.C. / A crew member for Richard Childress Racing has died of complications from the H1N1 virus.

Donald "DJ" Richardson died late Friday after a lengthy hospitalization for acute respiratory distress syndrome. Richardson contracted H1N1 during a Thanksgiving visit to his home state of Massachusetts, and team officials say he had been hospitalized at Health Alliance Hospital in Leominster the entire time.

The 37-year-old Richardson spent last season as a tire changer on Kevin Harvick's crew.

Sunday Reader
Top 10, worst 10 -- Lists and more lists; Italian author Umberto Eco thinks they help us escape death
Peggy Curran
Canwest News Service
2371 words
27 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
E4
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

It was the best of times, it was the worst of times, it was the age of wisdom, it was the age of foolishness, it was the epoch of belief, it was the epoch of incredulity, it was the season of Light, it was the season of Darkness, it was the spring of hope, it was the winter of despair, we had everything before us, we had nothing before us, we were all going direct to heaven, we were all going direct the other way -- in short, the period was so far like the present period, that some of its noisiest authorities insisted on its being received, for good or for evil, in the superlative degree of comparison only.

Charles Dickens, A Tale of Two Cities

Natural disasters are down, but dizzyingly sordid sex scandals are up. Economic forecasts for Canada are cheery, if only because 12 months ago they were unspeakably grim. We have hacked and barked through two flu seasons without calamitous consequences, awaiting the H1N1 fallout of holiday hugs and hand clasps with stoicism, paper masks and antiseptic hand sanitizer.

As the year, and indeed, this decade we've yet to name -- the Oh-Ohs? the Noughties? the GOOgles? -- draw to a close, we look back, trying to understand the great mystery of dissolving time.

For any who doubts that much really changes in that fraction of a light year it takes the Earth to turn on its axis 3,638 times, be reminded that at this time 10 years ago Jean Chretien was still prime minister, George W. Bush was not yet in the White House, and people actually cared that Scary Spice was getting a divorce. There was (sort of) peace on Earth, and we waited with trepidation for the dreaded Y2K bug.

(In Edmonton, Bill Smith was the mayor, the Anthony Henday was still on drawing board and the Oilers were, well, you know, about the same as they are now.)

Small wonder that as year and decade slip away, we often ask ourselves where we've been and where we're going, what we did right or monumentally wrong and what monsters and delights might lurk around the corner.

And so we make lists, chiselling out a rough draft of history in bite-size portions.

The best of this, the worst of that.

The 10 best books of 2009, the 50 worst movies, lists of top newsmakers, famous people who died, rogues who stole our savings, crimes against fashion and rising indie stars in rehab.

Time magazine has already weighed in with its verdict, calling the first 10 years of the 21st century the "Decade from Hell," citing everything from the contested election of George W. Bush and the Sept. 11 attacks, to wars in Iraq and Afghanistan, Hurricane Katrina, the 2004 tsunami in South Asia and the collapse of global financial markets to illustrate its point.

For the media, there is, yes, a not-so-hidden agenda. End-of-year wraps fill the news hole at a time when most reporters are off somewhere tucking into their Christmas turkey or Hanukkah latkes. What better way to plug in the spaces between Boxing Day ads, dreary holiday house fires and Third World catastrophes than retrospectives on Bob Dylan's gazillionth comeback or the gizmo of the year?

But news outlets wouldn't devote considerable resources to playing Santa, making those lists and checking them twice, if there wasn't such an appetite for information in neat little packages tied up with bows.

Lists can be cheap: The 50 Worst Movies of the Year, or The Year of Dressing Ridiculously. They may be dirty -- witness the tabloid frenzy of women who slept with the Tiger. Yet in this age of information overload, they allow us to keep chaos at bay and wrap our heads around events, ideas and trends without feeling totally overwhelmed in the noisy clutter of everyday living.

End-of-year lists are the Coles notes of storytelling, a way to bone up, before the next act begins, on all those books we didn't have time to read and bands we didn't hear and political coups that might have passed us by.

For Italian novelist and deep thinker Umberto Eco, the list is much more than the sum of its shallow parts.

Eco is curator of a new exhibition at the Louvre in Paris devoted to the role of lists in art and literature -- from Moses's Top 10 and Homer's Iliad to a description of what's in Leopold Bloom's dresser drawer in James Joyce's Ulysses.

"This list doesn't destroy culture, it creates it," Eco explained in a recent interview with Der Spiegel International. "What does culture want? To make infinity comprehensible. It also wants to create order -- not always, but often. And how, as a human being, does one face infinity? How does one attempt to grasp the incomprehensible? Through lists, through catalogues, through collections in museums and through encyclopedias and dictionaries.

"We have always been fascinated by infinite space, by the endless stars and by galaxies upon galaxies. How does a person feel when looking at the sky? He thinks that he doesn't have enough tongues to describe what he sees. Nevertheless, people have never stopped describing the sky, simply listing what they see."

For Eco, there's "an irresistible magic" to lists, although he's at a loss to explain quite why they fascinate him so much. "I like lists for the same reason other people like football or pedophilia. People have their preferences."

So what is it that drives us to channel our inner librarian, collecting, collating and compartmentalizing?

On rainy summer days when I was little, we played a game called Categories. Adults and children would sit around the long wooden table at the Horner family's cottage and make charts on pieces of scrap paper. Five lines down, five lines across. Everyone took a turn coming up with a letter and a category: flowers, bodies of water, jobs. Then we'd knuckle down to see who was able to fill in all the squares within the allotted time.

Daffodil, Danube, Denmark, Dentist, Detroit. Cornflower, Champlain, Canada, Carpenter, Caracas.

Decades before the Internet would lure us into endless hours of addictive brain games and mindless searching, we stretched our synapses with fun, but ultimately meaningless, lists.

And some people just can't help themselves.

Back in the 1850s, an eccentric British doctor named Peter Mark Roget published his self-titled thesaurus. He'd been working on the dictionary of synonyms for the better part of 50 years, an exercise that biographer Joshua Kendall suggests helped Roget stave off the risk of madness that snaked through his family tree. Without the benefit of a modern medicine cabinet of tranquillizers, Roget poured his obsessive-compulsive tendencies -- there's speculation he probably suffered from what today would be described as a type of high-functioning autism called Asperger's syndrome -- into endless cross-referenced lists of words.

It's worth noting here that Roget, a man of little humour, even when experimenting with laughing gas, invented the slide rule and tried building a calculator, that rudimentary predecessor of what is today's ultimate list-maker, the computer.

The first decade of the new century has taken us into realms of cyberspace previously imagined only in science fiction. In this time of gadgets and widgets, we Twittered and MySpaced. We Map-Quested and Ebayed, YouTubed and iPhoned, friended and unfriended. We bragged about who had the most Apps, or kept quiet and hoped no one would guess we had no idea what they were talking about.

Thanks to BlackBerry and social networking, we are more connected than ever. And yet, strangely, we don't always communicate all that well, rescuing colons and parentheses from the grammatical slag-heap to add a grain of humour or sarcasm to sterile e-mail messages.

Most of all, we tapped into Google to make customized lists, to check spellings, find news and shop. In the process, we put music shops, magazines, newspapers and telephone books on life support and reinvented the very essence of how we learn, what we read, and how long we can concentrate.

At times, it felt like we were competing with those computers we depend on so much to organize and process information at an alarming clip. Dashing madly in all directions, we became a society plagued by too many distractions. There's even a name for the online equivalent to attention-deficit disorder. Wilfing -- short for "what was I looking for?" -- describes the act of aimless browsing as one stream of search words begets another.

Umberto Eco sees in the computer, and especially the omnivorous Google search engine, a place where order and anarchy converge.

"The minute I look at my Google-generated list, it has already changed. These lists can be dangerous -- not for old people like me, who have acquired their knowledge in another way, but for young people, for whom Google is a tragedy. Schools ought to teach the high art of how to be discriminating."

And yet it must be said that only through the extraordinary miracle of Google can a journalist in Canada punch in a couple of keywords like lists and culture and in two seconds flat stumble on remarks as strikingly relevant as those of an Italian scholar interviewed by a German newsmagazine about an exhibit at a French museum.

"We have a limit, a very discouraging, humiliating limit: death," said Eco. "That's why we like all the things that we assume have no limits and, therefore, no end. It's a way of escaping thoughts about death. We like lists because we don't want to die."

Honestly, few of us are consciously thinking about anything quite as profound as death and our place in the universe when we make lists or discuss them between mouthfuls of sweet potato and Yule log.

But we do want to know what happened and why. Nifty news summaries, capsule reviews and those timely lists of the best, worst and indifferent help us keep up with what's going on out there in the world and feel we are a part of it.

It doesn't matter if you didn't see Avatar or read this year's Booker prize winner (Hilary Mantel's Wolf Hall) or follow every gassy burp in the climate change talks in Copenhagen or the water meter scandal at city hall. In a pinch, Google can fill in the blanks.

Provided you know where to start.

Hurt Locker. Hawaii. House foreclosures. Harry Potter. Hurricane Katrina.

Twilight. Tiger Woods. Terrorism. The Tipping Point. Tsunami.

Slumdog Millionaire. Seabiscuit. Sub-prime mortgages. Sopranos. September 11 ...

---------

Bites of history: Top reasons why we like lists

-- Peggy Curran

1. They make us feel smart. Or at least smarter than the people we are reading about. The Guinness Book of World Records, a bestseller for 50 years, keeps tabs on accomplishments of all shapes, sizes and levels of peculiarity. How else would you ever find out who holds the record for the fastest time running 100 metres barefoot on ice (Nico Surings, in the Netherlands, December 2006), the most cockroaches eaten in one sitting (Ken Edwards, 36 cockroaches, in England, 2001), or the heaviest weight dangled from a swallowed sword (Thomas Blackthorne, 25 kg, in Italy, April 2009)?

2. They make us feel smug. Didn't make the list again this year? Obviously, you aren't David Letterman, Tiger Woods, Earl Jones, Octomom, Jon and Kate Gosselin, Chris Brown, Rihanna, Jessica Simpson, Mel Gibson or Bernard Madoff.

3. They tell us what to read, see, do. Or really avoid. Without those timely end-of-year lists, would you know what CD your mother will want this Christmas (Susan Boyle, Michael Buble) and what movie you absolutely must/under no circumstances see ( Avatar/The Lovely Bones)?

4. They tell us where to go. Even if you never seem to get farther than the AMT station or the Champlain Bridge, there's something invigorating about the endless possibilities in books like 1,000 Places to See Before You Die, which dazzles with the magic of Turkey's whirling dervishes, Count Dracula's castle in Romania and sunset on Australia's Ayers Rock. On the other hand, Mercer International lists Baghdad, Kinshasa, Port au Prince and Khartoum among the least livable of world cities again this year.

5. They teach us the error of our ways. Fortynine years have gone by since the late Richard Blackwell began tabulating the best and worst of fashion and design. What have we learned from Cher's golden beads, Celine's backward tuxedo and Bjork's feather duster? Not much, according to a coven of fashion mavens, who cite such style faux pas of our times as Uggs, Crocs, harem pants and very short shorts paired with tights.

Or is that 12 or 14? Telescope
Sunday Reader
Peculiar, picayune and persnickety of 2009
Terry McConnell
Edmonton Journal
2367 words
27 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
E2
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

Friends, there are year-end reviews -- and there is Telescope's year-end review. The year just concluding has been rich in its bounty of the odd, the obtuse and the obscure. Take for instance, these nuggets:

- - A bacon sandwich is the ideal cure for a hangover. The bread is high in carbohydrates and the bacon is full of proteins that break down into amino acids, something your body needs to develop a clearer head. Plus, you know, it's bacon.

- - By the way, say researchers, women's underarms typically smell like onions and men's like Gouda cheese. That has to be bad news for the purveyors of Gouda cheese.

- - Then there was the trouble in Australia on International Whores Day when prostitutes marched in protest against the high rates that local newspapers charge for escort ads. "I'm paying too much," said one sex worker, "for a measly two inches."

Friends, there is plenty more where that came from. Read on for Telescope's Best of 2009:

January

A survey in Britain has revealed the most irritating invention in recent memory is the karaoke machine. Cellphones, alarm clocks and 24-hour sports channels also scored high.

Speaking of being irritated, a four-year-old boy in Columbus, Ohio became so upset when his teenage babysitter accidentally stepped on his foot that he went over to the closet, pulled out a gun, loaded it, then shot the babysitter. The teen survived.

In terms of popularity, the fastest growing name given to newborns in the U.S. is Nevaeh. That's "heaven" spelled backward.

In Beverly Hills, Calif., a liposuction doctor admitted to using the excessive body fat extracted from his patients to create a bio-diesel fuel to power his Ford SUV.

Officials in Berlin, responding to a noise complaint, found a 60-year-old man living in his apartment with 1,700 parakeets. The man said he had adopted two birds because he felt lonely, and that nature sort of took it from there.

By the way, one of the organizations to cancel its meeting when an ice storm was forecast but had yet to hit was the St. David Optimists Club of Fulton County, Illinois.

February

The "eternal" flame at a veterans' memorial in Bullhead City, Ariz., was turned off after only one month. The $1,200 Cdn monthly gas bill to commemorate the veterans' sacrifice was deemed too high for the city to bear.TVviewers in Tuscon, Ariz.,

were cheated from watching their Cardinals lose the Super Bowl in the final 30 seconds of the game. The broadcast was interrupted by a clip from a porn movie. Fans were watching the Steelers drive the ball downfield for the winning touchdown when, suddenly, there was a woman on the screen unzipping a man's pants and, um, engaging in sportsmanlike conduct.

A student in San Diego announced she was auctioning her virginity to pay for her education. Natalie Dylan needed the money to fund her master's degree in Family and Marriage Therapy. An estimated 10,000 men poneyed up bids for her virtue that topped out at more than $4.4 million Cdn.

Valentine's Day shoppers in Eau Claire, Wis., were getting more bang for their buck: Buy a pair of earrings, get a free gun. They called it the "Bang Bling Deal."

Then there was this headline in the New York Post: "Peter Pan Actress: Hook is All Hands!"

March

Officials at the famed Globe Theatre in London agreed with a request to meet with police to discuss mounting concerns that two Shakespearean plays performed at the theatre -- Romeo and Juliet and Julius Caesar -- promote knife crimes.

Meanwhile, police in Silver Spring, Md., arrested 16 people after a brawl broke out at a Stop the Violence concert.

Then there was the guy in Austria who started knocking off banks to get the money to pay for his fiancee's expectations for a lavish wedding. "The money from the first robbery went in one day, so I just kept going," the 36-year-old bridegroom told the judge at his trial.

An unnamed Illinois newspaper reported in the first paragraph of a news story that, "About 140 employees will be laid off at Caterpillar Inc." -- only, um, the word "off" was left out.

Then there was the headline in the Arizona Republic: "Dog Eats $400, But Woman Recovers Some Cash Later"

April

The Essex parish council in England decided to leave its crop of potholes intact as "the original traffic-calming measure."

Meanwhile, a guy in Moscow, Alexei Roskov, was so despairing for his life that he downed three bottles of vodka, then threw himself out of the fifth-floor window of his apartment. Somehow he survived the fall with barely a scratch. Yet when Roskov staggered back upstairs to his apartment, his wife started in on him, so he jumped again. That leap didn't kill him either.

Civic officials in Webster, Mass., were all embarrassed. For years, they'd been calling the local watering hole Lake Chargoggagoggmanchaoggagoggchaubunaguhgamaugg.

Well, they were wrong. It's actually Lake Chargoggagoggmanchauggagoggchaubunagungamaugg.

A British supermarket chain renamed its stock of pollock fish because shoppers were too embarrassed to ask for it by name. Sainsbury's will now sell pollock as "colin." Pollock to the English ear sounds like a slang word for testicles.

May

A teenage boy in Findlay, Ohio, was banned from his Baptist high school graduation because he attended his girlfriend's prom and admitted to, um, dancing with her. "Should a Christian place himself at an event where young ladies will have low-cut dresses and be dancing in them?" asked the school's principal. The boy decided the answer was definitely yes.

In West Haven, Utah, the principal of a junior high school was told to apologize to a student he sent home for wearing a kilt. The principal told the boy the kilt could be construed as cross-dressing.

Meanwhile, two workers at Yellowstone National Park were fired after they were caught peeing into the Old Faithful geyser.

Then there was this health alert from the Weekly World News: "The swine flu epidemic has reached the United States. This new mutated strand of influenza starts as a normal flu, then turns people into pigs. ... The new swine flu can be spread by coughing, sneezing, or being bit by another werepig."

June

In New York City, the appropriately named Lucky the cat slipped from his owner's apartment window and fell 26 storeys to the street below. There he made a graceful and typically feline landing, with nary a scratch. Evidently, Lucky achieved "terminal velocity," the speed at which the body relaxes and allows for a softer landing.

A travel agency in Harbin, China, was finding success offering tours for couples that are on the verge of divorcing. The advertising pitch? "If it's destiny for us to break up, then let's have more romantic memories than painful ones."

And providing proof that perspective is a terrible thing to lose is this headline from the Bloomington-Normal Daily Pantagraph: "Deputy Tasers 72-Year-Old Woman During Traffic Stop"

July

In one office in England, the Casual Friday custom of wearing jeans to work was replaced with something a little more radical: call it Naked Friday. The team-building strategy struck a responsive chord with the firm's workers. Said Samantha Jackson, the company's manager: "Now that we've seen each other naked, there are no barriers."

An Olympic athlete from New Zealand opened his own brothel to raise the money he needs to compete in the 2012 London Olympics. It cost Logan Campbell close to $110,000 Cdn to represent his country at the Beijing Olympics, thus the idea of the brothel. Licensed prostitution is legal in New Zealand and Campbell hopes to raise about $200,000 in the next two years.

The teaching staff at Southway Junior School in West Sussex, England, wanted to spark the flame of creativity in their students, so they staged the crash of a fake UFO near the school, then encouraged the kids to write about it. However, many of the kids were so traumatized, they went home crying and wondering if the world as they knew it was about to end. Parents were not amused.

August

A male tortoise in England finally won his mate, 15 long years after she first began to spurn his advances. The problem might have been their age difference. Billy is 110 years old and Tammy is only 47. Yet the happy couple became proud parents of seven eggs. Said the tortoises' owner, Peter Crane: "I didn't realize Billy had it in him."

A 25-year-old tow-truck driver in Buffalo was texting on one cellphone and talking on another when he slammed into a car and drove into a swimming pool.

Meanwhile in Melbourne, Australia, thieves stole 58 curling rocks that were being stored in a refrigerated truck outside a downtown ice rink. Their haul represents one-third of all the curling rocks to be found in Australia. Police suspect the burglars were looking for beer.

Then there is the theme park in Britain that banned roller-coaster fans from raising their arms during the ride. Why? Complaints of body odour. Surmised Thorpe Park director Mike Vallis of the unpleasant aromas on hot days: "It can get really pongy."

We can't overlook this headline from ananova.com:"Family Harassed By Vengeful Weasel"

September

Police in Britain found a pretty good lead on solving a break-in at a school in Gateshead. The burglar left his dog behind. Police found the Jack Russell at the school after responding to a burglar alarm.

A Bank of America branch in Tampa, Fla., refused to cash a cheque for a customer with no arms because -- and we are not making this up -- he couldn't provide a thumbprint.

In Vienna, Austria, a faithful Catholic named Gunther Link believed his prayers had been answered when he was rescued from a stuck elevator. Filled with gratitude, he immediately went to church to offer his thanks to God. It was there he was later found, dead, crushed under the 860-pound weight of a stone altar that had fallen on him. Said a spokescop: "He seems to have embraced a pillar on which the stone altar was perched and it fell on him, killing him instantly."

October

The University of Florida has abandoned the emergency response plan it developed in the event the campus is attacked by zombies. A university spokesflak said, "It didn't really belong." The school's other plans for dealing with hurricanes and pandemics will remain.

In the North Carolina burg of Canton, a Baptist preacher announced plans to burn Bibles and books by Christian authors. Pastor Marc Grizzard said the King James Version of the Bible is the only one his followers, um, follow. All other versions, including the Living Bible, are "satanic." Also on the torch list are books by evangelist Billy Graham.

While we're in the Southern U.S., police in Little Rock say a mugger dropped his own wallet during a robbery, then phoned his victim to ask for it back. An arrest quickly followed.

Then there is the fellow in Duluth, Minn., who was convicted for driving drunk. That might not seem all that unusual but the guy was, um, piloting his La-Z-Boy chair at the time. The chair was powered by a converted lawn mower engine and featured a stereo and cupholders.

November

Police in Azamgarh, India, admitted they put the remains of a 22-year-old man in a body bag and left it on the roof of their police station -- for two years. The corpse was supposed to be sent away for DNA testing, but was put on the roof instead when local officials declined to pay for the tests. Then everybody forgot about it. The body, which was really nothing more than a collection of bones after two years, was returned to the family.

Police in Carroll, Iowa, picked up two burglary suspects who were still wearing their "masks" -- and that's a liberal interpretation of the word "mask." What these two guys did was to scribble on their bare faces with one of those permanent black markers.

Meanwhile, a baffled bank teller in Poland refused to hand over any cash to the robber who was threatening her -- with a spoon. The crook burst into the bank branch in Lublin brandishing the utensil and shouting, "This is a stickup." Much mirth and merriment ensued.

Tasha Johnson and Markeith Brown's wedding day in Tampa, Fla., was turning out beautifully until a full-scale brawl broke out. Before anyone knew for sure what was happening, one of the guests had the groom's 74-year-old grandma in a headlock. She was unhurt.

Then there was the headline in the Arizona Republic that suggested that maybe your average Godzilla movie is not so far-fetched: "Japanese Fishing Boat Capsized By Giant Jellyfish"

December

Remember that Walking With Dinosaurs show that played in Edmonton the past two summers? It was the one with the giant animatronic reptiles of old. Well, one of those dinosaurs went missing in Guadalajara, Mexico. Since the show's people don't believe it just walked off on its own, foul play is suspected. The missing dinosaur stands around five feet tall and answers to the name "Larry." Well, we made up the Larry part.

Getting into the White House isn't as tough as the secret service makes it out to be. Take the family in the camper van who were waved in because they honked their horn as they approached the gates.

And finally ...

British researchers say the most stressful time of the work week is Tuesday at 11:45 a.m. Keep your head down.

News
Facebook's top 2009 status update trends includes angry FML
Amy Minsky
Canwest News Service
302 words
27 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
A6
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

Who knew there was room for anger in the kingdom of Internet fads ruled by LOLCats?

"FML," digital slang used to express frustration, ranked second among Facebook's Top 15 words and phrases used in American status updates in 2009.

The acronym, which stands for "f-- my life," came blazing onto the Facebook lexicon scene, moving from a relatively low usage rate to one of the most mainstream status updates in 2009, according to the social networking site's data team.

The month of May -- a time when many students are wrapping up their final exams and hunting for summer jobs -- saw the biggest spike in statuses containing the acronym.

After a relative lull in usage during the summer, FML crept back toward chart-topping levels throughout the first semester of a new school year.

Facebook's data team also noticed a weekly trend in the number of frustrated status updates. "As we'd expect ... Mondays and Tuesdays are particularly rough," the data team said in a Facebook note.

"Surprisingly, it is not Mondays that rate highest, but Tuesdays."

In order to make the list more comprehensive, the data team used keywords to group similar items. Keywords "Michael Jackson," "Patrick Swayze" and "Billy Mays" were used to map the frequency for status updates mentioning celebrity deaths (fourth place).

Movie titles, such as New Moon, Transformers and The Hangover were used to track discussion of movies (sixth place).

Status updates featuring Facebook applications topped the list, while swine flu (third), family (fifth), sports (seventh), health care (eighth) and FB (ninth) all cracked the Top 10.

Twitter came in a respectable 10th place, even though activity peaked in April and has been decreasing steadily ever since.

News
Seven dead as Iraqis mark Ashura
AFP
272 words
27 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A8
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

Hundreds of thousands of Shiite pilgrims descended on Iraq's holy shrine city of Karbala yesterday for the climax of Ashura commemorations, despite fears of violent attacks by Sunni extremists.

As they poured into Karbala, violence across Iraq claimed the lives of seven people, including two worshippers killed when a bomb struck an Ashura procession in eastern Baghdad.

Security forces have beefed up their presence in Karbala and authorities are also checking on about 60,000 foreign worshippers expected to attend the ceremonies to ensure they do not have swine flu.

Black flags, representing the sadness of Shiites during Ashura, were seen all over the central Iraqi city, south of Baghdad, along with pictures of Imam Hussein and Imam Abbas, who are both buried here.

The 10-day Ashura rituals which end today commemorate the killing of Imam Hussein, a grandson of the prophet Mohammed, by armies of the Sunni caliph Yazid in 680.

The Karbala police chief said that about 25,000 officers and soldiers had been deployed to secure the commemoration ceremonies. Helicopters are providing extra surveillance and bomb-sniffing dogs are being used at checkpoints.

To counter female suicide attackers who have struck Karbala before, police have deployed 600 female personnel on three roads into the city. Cameras are also monitoring pilgrims' movements across Karbala.

During Ashura in March 2004, near-simultaneous bombings at a Shiite mosque in Baghdad and in Karbala killed more than 170 people.

Colour Photo: MORTEZA NIKOUBAZL REUTERS / Shiite devotees beat themselves during Tasoua yesterday, a day ahead of Ashura, in Yazd, south of Tehran.

Crew member dies of H1N1 complications; auto racing
Citizen news services
104 words
27 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
D2
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

A crew member for Richard Childress Racing has died of complications from the H1N1 virus. Donald "DJ" Richardson died late Friday after a lengthy hospitalization for acute respiratory distress syndrome. Richardson contracted H1N1 during a Thanksgiving visit to his home state of Massachusetts, and team officials say he had been hospitalized ever since.

The 37-year-old Richardson spent last season on Kevin Harvick's crew. He began his NASCAR Sprint Cup Series career with Andy Petree Racing in 1999 and had previously worked for Penske Racing, Hendrick Motorsports and Braun Racing.

News
15 memorable moments of 2009
Jack Knox
Times Colonist
60 words
27 December 2009
Victoria Times Colonist
VTC
Final

News
Behind the scenes of the H1N1 flu outbreak
Sharon Kirkey
Canwest News Service
1862 words
26 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
D10
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

Dr. Rob Fowler does not know how long a person with severe H1N1 disease can remain on a ventilator and still be discharged from intensive care alive.

If there is a limit, he has not seen it yet.

There are H1N1 patients in Canada who have been on ventilators since September.

Some of the sickest patients, their lungs so inflamed and fluid-filled, have had to be turned onto their stomachs and ventilated, face down, to keep secretions and bacteria from pooling in the back of their lungs.

Immobilized by drugs, their muscles are wasting. They need high doses of sedatives and paralyzing agents that stop their nerves from sending any signals to the muscles, to keep them from fighting against the ventilator. Like astronauts on long space flights, "their muscles are completely de-conditioned," says Fowler, assistant professor in the departments of medicine and critical care medicine at the University of Toronto. "Most of the time, at the end of this, they often can't hold something in their hand, let alone stand up. That's only something that will come back after many, many months of rehabilitation."

Fowler, a soft-spoken critical care doctor and researcher who has helped inform colleagues the world over how to care for patients with severe H1N1 disease, is aware of patients who have been living in this state of almost suspended animation for months.

He is still hoping they will recover.

- - -

Seventy per cent of the population infected. Thirty-three per cent seriously ill. Up to 58,000 Canadians dead. Soon after the first hints of a worldwide infectious disease outbreak emerged in March, when a baffling respiratory infection struck residents of the small Mexican village of La Gloria, Canadian public health officials braced for what they had been planning for for years: a grave viral attack.

History holds that pandemics occur three or four times each century, and it had been unnervingly long since the last outbreak, in 1968. The world could only guess at the next danger that might come to pass, and for five years everyone had been watching a different kind of virus -- H5N1, or "bird flu," a pathogen that kills birds like a shot, and is fatal in up to 60 per cent of the humans it infects.

The new illness -- called H1N1 -- came from pigs, not birds, but as human infections with a "novel" flu strain of swine origin were identified in Mexico, then in Southern California and Texas, and then Canada, the World Health Organization quickly warned governments to prepare for a "public health emergency of international concern."

Eight months out, H1N1 has killed at least 10,582 people worldwide, 397 of them in Canada, all of the deaths heartbreaking, and some of them shocking: 10-year-old Vanetia Warner, a figure skater and Grade 5 student from Cornwall, Ont., who so loved Miley Cyrus that a continuous loop of the pop star's music played during the little girl's wake; Evan Frustaglio, a 13-year-old Mississauga North Stars hockey player who collapsed after a bath, his warm body limp and lifeless in his father's arms; 23-year-old Fatiha Idrissi Kaitouni of Montreal, who spent two months in a medically induced coma after her son was delivered by caesarean section, waking only once, according to reports, to ask for her baby.

But the surge in deaths the pandemic plans predicted hasn't happened. So far, H1N1 may be on track to kill fewer people than regular seasonal flu. Some of the most-feared scenarios have not materialized. Intensive care units have been strained, but not crippled. Doctors have not had to decide which patients were "salvageable" and which would get the last available ventilators. After weeks of near-panic and confusion, the mass vaccination clinics have closed, the number of people getting sick is falling, and public interest and concern appears to have all but evaporated.

The first global influenza pandemic of the century appears to be going out with a whisper, and not a bang.

"The virus writes the rules," World Health Organization director-general Margaret Chan said on June 11 when she raised the global pandemic alert from Phase 5 to 6, the highest, and H1N1 has not attacked as ferociously as feared. Most importantly, the people most likely to die from human swine flu if infected -- older people -- are the ones least likely to get H1N1, because of past exposure to similar variants that circulated in the 1950s.

Without that existing immunity, H1N1's death toll would be higher by tenfold. Essentially, life had already vaccinated the most vulnerable against the pandemic virus.

The illness is mild in most, and in the small number who have become seriously sick, intensive, aggressive care and dramatic "lung rescue" therapies have made it possible to save people who otherwise would have died.

The H1N1 narrative is still being written but, to this point, in Canada and other developed countries it's a story of unprecedented global scientific collaboration and almost-real-time information-sharing. Never before has a pandemic been detected so quickly, or tracked more closely. The fears about health care workers not showing up for work were unfounded. The public health system mobilized to deliver the largest vaccination program in the nation's history.

But it's also a story about transparency, or lack thereof. The public wasn't aware of how decisions were being made, or who was making them.

"There was lots of confusion about who got what, when, who was eligible for the vaccine, and it was changing on a daily basis," says Dr. Ross Upshur, director for the Joint Centre for Bioethics at the University of Toronto. "As a clinician, when you have a family and only one of them is on a priority list, it's very hard to say, 'You get the vaccine and the rest of you don't,' because it makes no sense. Infections spread in families."

- - -

The main road map for the response was a federal pandemic plan built on assumptions: that the next pandemic would first emerge most likely in Asia, giving Canada time to prepare; that between 34,000 and 138,000 people would be hospitalized but recover, and that 11,000 and 58,000 would die.

But H1N1 didn't follow the script.

Mexico, not Asia, recorded the first known death from H1N1, on April 12.

Fourteen days later, Canadian public health officials announced the virus was in Canada.

Soon, Canadian researchers discovered the virus was behaving in startling ways. It was preferentially attacking the young and generally healthy -- a group flu normally spares -- and causing catastrophically low levels of oxygen in the blood, serious lung damage and multi-organ failure.

"Yes, mortality has been less than expected, but look at who's dying," Upshur says. "Younger people who had a probability of death that was as low as can be. And all of these were preventable deaths. Yes, I'm glad there weren't tens of thousands of deaths -- who would wish that? But the deaths that did occur were disproportionately in younger people, who you would not have expected to die."

This was an eerie flashback to SARS. For health care workers who experienced that outbreak, H1N1 at first stirred memories of healthy, relatively young people in acute respiratory distress.

"It's easy to say that people who work in intensive care units see very severely ill, relatively young people all the time," says Dr. Allison McGeer, director of infection control at Toronto's Mount Sinai Hospital and a SARS survivor.

"But to see them in an outbreak, in a setting of respiratory disease, just looks more like SARS. I think pandemic stress, the stress of young people with very severe illness, pregnant women at risk of dying themselves and losing their babies, those are not circumstances you see very often in ICUs. So it's stressful anyway. But when it's layered on top of people's experience with the SARS epidemic, it makes it harder to care of those patients and deal with those circumstances."

- - -

SARS was what some doctors initially feared when the Mexicans began dying.

Earl Brown was in his Ottawa lab in early April when reports of an outbreak in Mexico of a strange respiratory infection were posted to ProMED, an online global electronic reporting system for infectious disease outbreaks.

"Mexico started to get alarmed when they started getting some severe pneumonias, and they started to get quite a few. They couldn't identify the virus. In some of the people, their lungs looked like people who had SARS," says Brown, a University of Ottawa virologist. "That got people nervous. 'How big is the outbreak in Mexico?' "

Then, on April 17, American health officials reported that two children in California had been infected with an influenza strain rarely seen in humans -- a flu of pig origin.

"That was sort of weird, but you know that periodically farmers have been infected with viruses from their pigs and they don't get too sick," Brown says.

"So there were these two kids in California, and there were these little niggles of reports of some respiratory virus going on in Mexico. But the two hadn't been put together."

In Mexico, virtually all the tests they were using were coming back negative for every prototype strain of flu known. "That raised the question: either it's a new flu virus, or it's something else," says Dr. Tim Booth, director of viral diseases at the Public Health Agency of Canada's National Microbiology Lab in Winnipeg.

Scientists get very worried if there's any kind of flu they cannot type.

Dr. Frank Plummer, director of the Winnipeg lab, was at home watching a hockey game when he received an e-mail from the head of Mexico's national microbiology lab, asking Plummer's help testing specimens from Mexico.

On April 22, 51 samples arrived from Mexico. The Winnipeg researchers were nervous.

"We don't know what we are dealing with. Is it another SARS or another new, emerging respiratory illness? We don't know," recalls Dr. Yan Li, chief of the lab's respiratory viruses section.

By 10 p.m. that night, 18 of the samples had tested positive for influenza A, but investigators still didn't know what subtype. They worked through the night, and by 3 a.m. the next morning, the picture was clear: the Mexican virus was the same H1N1 "swine flu" virus as had struck in California.

Canada was the first to announce the link.

It would also be the next country hit.

First of a two-part series

Photo: Canwest News Service / Dr. Yan Li, chief of the respiratory viruses section, is shown in a lab at the National Microbiology Laboratory in Winnipeg on Dec. 18. Dr. Li led a team that played a key role during the H1N1 outbreak. He also helped to develop a test that allowed public health labs across the province to determine rapidly whether a specimen was positive for H1N1.

Josh Freed
News
The last 10 years whizzed by in a few gazillion nano-seconds
JOSH FREED
FREELANCE
839 words
26 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A2
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

The decade is almost over and we still haven't decided what to call it.

Is it the zeros, or the 00s? Many suggest the "ohs!," or the "oh-ohs," or the "uh-ohs." In England, they're calling them the "oughties" and the "naughties."

Allow me to toss a suggestion into the ring for a name that symbolizes the past decade perfectly. How about the "zips" - and not just because zip stands for zero? It's also because we've zipped through the fastest-moving decade in human history - 10 years that seemed to whiz by in a few gazillion nano-seconds.

Much of this is because of the information revolution that's left us all breathless as we bark into our cellphones while firing off 17 emails while surfing the Web 24/7 while shouting at our kids to "get off the computer now!"

The world has transformed so crazily, we actually have to pass laws forbidding people from driving a car while typing text messages on their cellphones while eating take-out noodles with chopsticks.

Constant change is now the only constant.

During the Y2K crisis, we worried the Millennium Bug would shut down our Stone Age computers that hissed and gurgled like a kettle for 45 seconds each time we pressed a key. Back then, there was slow speed or no speed. Now, Videotron informs me my extraordinarily fast high-speed Internet is totally outdated because they're offering new "extreme high-speed" and "extreme high-speed-plus" and even "ultimate speed."

Soon these, too, will be outpaced by "extra-apocalyptic, utmost-transcendental, unsurpassable super-sublime speed."

Everything over 10 years old is now under threat, from car companies to TV networks and newspapers. Everything new is already being reinvented.

In 2000, there was no such thing as Wikipedia, just a quaint thing called an encyclopedia.

There were no iPods, no YouTube, no Facebook, no GPS and no wi-fi - while a BlackBerry was an ingredient in a fruit salad.

A decade ago, only 10 per cent of people owned a cellphone and I was not one of them. Now, 60 per cent of the world owns one, and it's rising to 61 per cent while you read this.

There also are 3.2 billion email accounts - and all of them are getting junk mail from former Nigerian princes.

The earth has been literally wired and now it's set to go wireless, creating a brave new world where our children may be free at last to sit at their desk without a tangle of wires by their feet.

Yet it's not just technological life that's moving at zip speed. It's our political and emotional lives, too, as we careen from one panicky global crisis to another.

In the last year alone, we have lived through a Great Depression and a Great Recovery - and we all know that everything could crash again next week, then recover, then crash, then ...

This year, we also survived a Swine Flu panic-demic that never really arrived, while earlier in the decade we lived through Y2K, 9/11, the dot.com bubble, the Enron collapse, the GM near-bankruptcy, the Asian tsunami, Hurricane Katrina, the Iraq-Afghanistan wars and the shameless demoting of Pluto from planet to space lump.

Life is a roller-coaster where one crisis constantly eclipses the last - and what ever happened to the hole in the ozone layer?

Now we face panic over global warming Armageddon, if the 2012 Mayan doomsday prediction doesn't get us first.

And if you're still not worried, astronomers have just discovered that the Milky Way is spinning 161,000 kilometres an hour faster than they thought - so even the universe is speeding up this decade.

This increases the chance of a collision between our galaxy and another galaxy, say scientists - and where do you think that will leave you in the year 200,010, buddy?

The pace of life is transforming so quickly we need new humans to cope with it - and brain scientists say we may get them.

During periods of rapid change, the human brain can apparently evolve quickly in only generations. Our era may be one of them as our brains strain to become a new Homo Googlensis.

For now, we still haven't reached our speed limit. As the "zips" decade ends and a new one begins, I suspect life will move from extremely high-speed to unbearably high-speed. By the time this century enters its mid-teen years, I think the "slow movement" will be speeding up, urging us to return to a more gentle and bucolic era - like a decade ago.

By 2020, there will be a planet-wide trend to slow down for at least a decade. Then get ready for the snoring 20s.

Meanwhile, a happy and slow-speed holiday to all.

DOUG CAMILLI
Culture
Vampires suck; But that's a good thing. Without blood to drink, their non-lives wouldn't be sexier than ours. And the undead personality is so like the year finally ending - cheerless, narcissistic, greedy and ominous. That's why 2009 is the Year of the Vampire
DOUG CAMILLI
Canwest News Service
3366 words
26 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
E1 / BREAK
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

What? Huh? December again already? Time to take stock and sum up, you say? Well, somebody's got to do it, so I sat down with the tools of my trade.

While cutting the lime into wedges, I gave 2009 the mental once-over: Recession. Afghanistan. Swine flu. Bernie Madoff. The Canadiens looking (and playing) like the Lollipop Guild. The Michael Jackson industry. Iran. The climate change issue. Earl Jones. Olympic marketing overkill. The coalition government fuss. The New York Yankees winning the World Series. Michael Ignatieff. City hall scandals. Tiger Woods. Ugh.

I poured another shot and tried to think of good things about '09, but stalled after the Alouettes and Olivia Wilde.

All those various bad things were just too dreary for me to fool with. I need something to mock, not mourn over.

Fortunately more tequila always brings more inspiration, so I persevered. Hmmm. "The year of '25 random things about ...' "? Nahh, that fad died as quickly as it arose. Twitter? That, too, now seems to be fading fast. Maybe "climategate" (because I kind of suspect this is both imminent disaster and a vast conspiracy). But everybody gets so mad about it. Nope.

I considered "infandous," an archaic word some over-educated pompousoid in a cabinet minister's office used about British loudmouth MP George Galloway. It means "beneath contempt" and "beneath notice" and clearly suits '09 perfectly. But I couldn't decide if 2009 would have to be the year of infandousness or the year of infandocity. I thought about the Beatles - over-marketed, half of them dead and half of them wrinkly, but apparently as eternal as ... bingo! There it was. And here it is:

2009 is surely the Year of the Vampire.

The lifeless undead were ubiquitous this year, first in teen fiction, then in teen movies, teen TV, everywhere. True Blood, Twilight, The Vampire Diaries, etc., etc.

It wasn't all utterly witless. There was an indie movie (with Devon Aoki!) called Rosencrantz and Guildenstern Are Undead, a Tom Stoppard reference which will not be lost upon the well-read.

But most of it is, shall we say, popular culture. I get 14 million Google hits on "vampire," but under 4 million on "Stephen Harper," who come to think of it, is in many ways ... but let's not go there.

Why are we obsessed with vampires? Sociologists droned on about anxiety in the culture. Novelist Anne Rice calls vampirism (vampirity?) "a powerful metaphor for the outcast and monster in all of us."

Nuts. I just think vampires have a lot in common with the personality of this whole year: cheerless, scary, narcissistic, parasitic, greedy, ominous, menacing but ridiculous, pale but bloodsoaked, immortal and immoral, horny and sexy (or so I am told by women who should know better).

And let's face it, their non-lives are better than our real ones: They never age, they have lots of money, they don't have to get up early, they don't fret over calories or exercise, many of them have glamorous accents, few have actual jobs ... being a vampire would be even better than being a rock star!

And, you know, every society needs role models. Vampires are undeniably the meta-phenomenon, the archetype, of 2009.

Vampires were not, however, the only absurd thing about '09. Here are some of my other favourites:

Those wacky Belgians: Belgium's national body-building championships were cancelled after government anti-doping inspectors showed up - and all 20 competitors ran away.

They should have expected some testing: last year three-quarters of competitors tested positive for steroids and other bad stuff.

Annals of nursing: In 2008, the Connecticut Nursing Association held a banquet to honour Betty Lichtenstein as "nurse of the year." This year she was arrested and charged with pretending to be a nurse.

It turns out, cops said, that there is no Connecticut Nursing Association. She had spent $2,000 to put on the dinner for herself. She could end up volunteering in a prison infirmary for five years, on charges of reckless endangerment and criminal impersonation.

Pickup line of the year: "I love you - and I have a condom!" Yeah, that oughtta work. (It's the first line of a justly forgotten movie called The Uninvited.)

National unity explained: Conservative minister John Baird, cranky because Toronto city hall missed a deadline for some stimulus money and was blaming him, was overheard suggesting angrily that Toronto should just "(bad word) off!"

Conservative popularity dropped in Toronto - but took off like a rocket in every other part of the country.

Tweet of the year: Singer Lily Allen: "People say I'm egocentric. But enough about them."

Toughest review of the year: Deborah Ross in The Spectator mag: "There really isn't a lot to say about Lesbian Vampire Killers apart from this: don't go anywhere near it. Just don't see it. Do something else instead. Do anything else instead. Catch up with your ironing ... at 87 minutes, it's probably a good 87 minutes too long."

Annals of education: Actual employment ad from the School of Sociology and Social Policy of the University of Leeds, in England:

"Research Officer - The rise and regulation of lap dancing and the place of sexual labour and consumption in the night time economy."

The fine print says the job - salary £31,513, or about $54,500 - involves interviewing 300 lap dancers.

Like a complete unknown: In Long Branch, N.J., cops got a complaint about an "eccentric old man acting suspiciously" and sent a squad car. A 22-year-old officer found a guy who wasn't a local. He gave his name but it meant nothing to the cop or her partner, he had no ID, and he seemed pretty scruffy, so they put him in the back of the car. When they radioed their sergeant, he seemed a little surprised that they had rousted Bob Dylan.

Not that there's anything wrong with that: This May headline from CNN's website revealed a previously unknown side of the Dear Leader: "Gay soldier appeals to Obama."

Correction of the year: N.Y. Times TV critic Alessandra Stanley wrote an obituary appraisal of Walter Cronkite. Then came the correction, noting that Stanley had "misstated the date that the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. was killed and referred incorrectly to Mr. Cronkite's coverage of D-Day. Dr. King was killed on April 4, 1968, not April 30. Mr. Cronkite covered the D-Day landing from a warplane; he did not storm the beaches. In addition, Neil Armstrong set foot on the moon on July 20, 1969, not July 26. The CBS Evening News overtook The Huntley-Brinkley Report on NBC in the ratings during the 1967-68 television season, not after Chet Huntley retired in 1970. A communications satellite used to relay correspondents' reports from around the world was Telstar, not Telestar. Howard K. Smith was not one of the CBS correspondents Mr. Cronkite would turn to for reports from the field after he became anchor of The CBS Evening News in 1962; he left CBS before Mr. Cronkite was the anchor."

What will they call their kids?: Kelly Hildebrandt of Florida married Kelly Hildebrandt of Texas. The first one's middle name is Katrina; the second one's middle is Carl. They met online, after she was searching for her own name on Facebook. After making sure they aren't closely related, they started dating.

What could possibly go wrong?: Solange Magnano, Miss Argentina of 1994, died from complications after buttock-enhancement therapy. She was 38.

Annals of banking: Every year there are stupid bank mistakes, but this year's was a doozy. Josh Muszynski of Manchester, N.H., swiped his debit card at a gas station to buy cigarettes. His bank account was billed $23 quadrillion. Then the bank added a $15 overdraft fee.

Annals of tourism: The people of Appenzell Inner Rhodes, an area mountainous even by Swiss standards, voted to ban naked hiking after an infestation by German nudists. Actually they wore socks and boots, but that was all; not always a pretty sight, I understand.

Wedding of the year: In Suvereto, near Livorno in Italy, a bride and groom had arranged for the bride's bouquet to be tossed to the brides-maids and single female guests from a small plane. But the flowers somehow got sucked into the engine and the plane caught fire, exploded, and plunged into a hostel, Corriere della Sera reported. One person was badly hurt.

You can see she's got a future in politics: Paula Abdul, asked about a report that Simon Cowell had asked her out on a date, answered this way: "There's some truth to that, and no truth to it."

You've got mail - and clamydia: Brazil's health ministry set up a website you can use to warn your sexual partners, by email, that you have a sexually transmitted disease.

New ways to be a chick magnet (#1 in a series): In Afghanistan, Cpl. Christopher Reynolds of the Black Watch (the Scottish one, not the Montreal one) has made the longest recorded kill by a sniper, gunning down an Afghan drug lord - hey, that's his story and he's sticking to it - at a range of almost one mile.

"I was quite proud of that shot," Cpl. Reynolds told the Daily Express. "I am going to use that fact as a chat-up line in the pub when I get back home."

Politician of the year: If you crossed Hugh Hefner with Mackenzie King, you'd have Italy's Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi, still in office after a year of scandals involving prostitutes, strippers, models, his quasi-secret hideaway Villa Certosa, etc., etc. I say nothing of his financial scandals.

At one point before she left him, his wife Veronica Lario spoke up to denounce the old goat's habit of appointing showgirls to be his party's candidates for the European Parliament. She also mocked him for turning up at the 18th birthday party of a blonde model whose parents he knows: "It really surprised me, given that he never went to the 18th birthday parties of his own children."

Teacher of the year: Crystal Defanti, a Grade 5 teacher near Sacramento, Calif., gave each of her students an end-of-year gift: a DVD she made herself, with footage of school events, outings, prize-givings - and her on a sofa, naked and intimate with a boyfriend.

It was an accident, she said, apologizing. This being California, the school board didn't fire her.

Annals of crime-fighting: Kwara State Police, in Nigeria, arrested a goat suspected of car theft. A police official explained that two guys stealing a Mazda were chased off by righteous citizens and "while one of them escaped, the other was about to be apprehended by the team when he turned his back ... and turned into this goat."

I can find no follow-up, but I'm pretty sure this case ended in capital punishment, a nice marinade, and some delicious kebabs.

"Men are pigs" dept.: Mel Gibson, Jon Gosselin, David Letterman, Ronnie Wood, Gordon Ramsay, John Edwards, Mark Sanford, Eddie Cibrian, and, of course, Tiger Woods.

My favourite is Sanford, governor of South Carolina, who was supposedly "hiking the Appalachian trail," when, in fact, he was in South America with a mistress. All over South Carolina, guys in bars started getting slapped in the face just for inviting girls to go hiking.

This never happens to Horatio Kane: When German cops found the same female DNA at 39 different crime scenes, over two years, they were sure they had a serial killer who also dabbled in other types of crime. But it turned out that all 39 samples matched the factory worker who had made the cotton swabs cops use to collect samples at crime scenes.

Criminal of the year: Police say they caught Claude Menard, 58, of Windsor, Ont., driving while drinking not one but two beers. When they pulled him over and asked for his licence, he allegedly handed them a bag of marijuana.

"Hi Mom. Can you guess where I am right now?" Nepal Telecom announced plans to provide cellphone service on the top of Mount Everest. Next year, I suppose, there'll be a Tim Horton's up there.

The leaders of tomorrow: In Swansea, Wales, more than 2,500 people - mostly university students - dressed up and painted up as Smurfs for a party at Oceana, a night club. This more than doubled the previous world record for most nitwits in blue paint.

And the organizers aren't finished yet. "We are hoping to set, break and smash Guinness World Records for iconic characters from Daleks to

Superheroes," said one.

Thanks for nothing, kid: A Tel Aviv woman identified as Anat bought her mother a new mattress, as a surprise gift, and had the old one hauled away to the dump.

Mom was not pleased, however, since she had her life savings - currency worth about $1 million U.S. - squirrelled away in the old mattress.

Oh, man, no more space? I was just getting started. But I suppose there's a limit to how awful a year can be. I hope so, anyway, because from here it looks like 2010 will have all the same old problems, plus who can say what new ones?

Oh well. You've got to keep trying, or laughing, or drinking. Maybe all three. Happy New Year, folks!

camilli@thegazette.canwest.com

Down Memory Lane: A Look Back at Recent History, Camilli Style

2008: The year of Sarah Palin: "Inexplicable, pretentious, ignorant, grasping, vain, persistent, ominous, loud, foolish and crude. I ask you: Was this woman 2008 in the flesh? You betcha!"

2007: The year of "¿Por qué no te callas?" when King Juan Carlos of Spain asked Venezuelan President Hugo Chavez, at some international gathering, "Why don't you shut up?"

2006: The Year of the ABBA Museum - "The most profound statement of which (our generation is) capable."

2005: The Year of the Blog, when "a critical mass of brain-dead opinionating squeezed the last scraps of actual fact and real wisdom out of the Internet."

2004: The Year of William Hung, the geeky, hilariously bad American Idol contestant who parlayed his lack of talent into a career, sort of.

2003: The Year of artist Mark McGowan, "who spent much of November sitting in a bathtub full of baked beans, with french fries stuffed up his nose in the window of a London art gallery."

2002: The Year of Frankenfish, a monster species from Asia that, scare stories told us, was invading North American rivers: "Hideous, ridiculous, ominous, ludicrous, tedious, omnivorous, preposterous, slimy and out of control; menacing and yet impossible to take seriously."

2001: The Year of the Xtreme Football League: "Equal parts of media hype, violence, mediocrity, cleavage, inanity and financial failure. It was loud, it was rude, it was stupid, it was unoriginal, it was sleazy, it was cheap, it was oversold."

2000: The Year of Marty McSorley: "Every time I see that replay (3,478 times and counting), I get a little shiver of patriotic pride. McSorley whacks that poor Donald Brashear just the way a stereotypical Canadian lumberjack chops into a tall pine - we're talking genuine national symbol here."

1999: The Year of Jar Jar Binks. Yousa knowa Jar Jar? The Phantom Menace character, which the Spectator mag called "a cross between a homosexual donkey and lop-eared rabbit," was not the best-loved Star Wars character.

1998: The Year of Social Union: "Canadians earnestly, sincerely believe that it's somehow better, nobler, more Canadian, to quibble over compensation for opting out of any new non-shared-cost welfare programs in provincial jurisdiction than to snigger over (Bill Clinton and his) blow ... uhhh, over a good political sex scandal."

1997: The Year of Howard Stern: "Malign energy, synthetic emotion, media manipulation, bad taste and comic excess."

1996: The Year of the Macarena: "Inane, peppy and quasi-mechanical."

1995: "The year before the Internet fad collapsed." I see now that I was premature, not wrong. The Internet remains "a worldwide network of Star Trek fans, corporate advertisers, amateur pornographers, failed authors, religious zealots, suicidal maniacs, pretentious rock stars, Gilligan's Island fans, gun nuts, and teenage dweebs unable to get a date."

1994: Oh, that's enough of this.

Enough Already

How serene and pleasant life would be if only we could hear no more, ever, about the following: sexting; Farmville; Roman Polanski; Taylor Swift and Kanye West; Christian Bale ranting; "green shoots;" Susan Boyle; Lost; "death panels;" Tiger and his lady friends; Labradoodles; "Balloon Boy;" Shepard Fairey; Spencer and Heidi and all who care about them even a little; John Mayer; "birthers"; Chesley Sullenberger; "25 random things about ..."; the web comic xkcd; Rod Blagojevich; Caster Semanya; Lizzie Miller; celebrities adopting Third World kids; Glee; Heath Ledger; Twitter; "stimulus;" Jorma Hogbacka; "I want to go to there;" Nadya Suleman; Liskula Cohen; Madonna's arms; Cheetos-flavoured lip balm; Cheesus the Christ-shaped Cheeto; So You Think You Can Dance?; Bo the First Dog; Massive Dynamics; the Phoenix Coyotes; "Baracknophobia;" Karl ...
