SPORTS
The sports year in review
By Christine Rivet
Record staff
2721 words
26 December 2009
Waterloo Region Record
TKWR
Final
C1
English
Copyright (c) 2009 Kitchener-Waterloo Record.

WATERLOO REGION - From Cam Burke to Alyssa Lagonia, Whitney McClintock to Ron VanKoughnett, Waterloo Region's finest athletes put their best feet forward on the national and global sports stages in 2009.

Others, like Colin Doyle, Bryan Little and Luca Congi continued to make inroads in their professional careers.

From direct hits like Blair Ezekiel's Canadian championship-winning RBI single to swings and misses like Jim Balsillie's latest failed effort to woo an NHL team, 2009 was chock full of cheers as well as a few jeers.

Here's a look at some of the local sports stories that made headlines in The Record this past year.

JANUARY

7 - A Kitchener Rangers' fire-sale special sends veterans Josh Unice, Scott Timmins and Ben Shutron to Ontario Hockey League rivals, the Windsor Spitfires, in exchange for Brandon Maxwell.

14 - Former National Lacrosse League rookie of the year, Kitchener's Ryan Benesch, lands with the Edmonton Rush following a trade from the Toronto Rock. He is dealt to Minnesota at the conclusion of the season.

24 - Despite repeated unsuccessful efforts to buy an NHL team, Waterloo billionaire Jim Balsillie is named Waterloo Region's most powerful person in sport by The Record.

FEBRUARY

16 - Waterloo's John Mitchell, who cracked the Toronto Maple Leafs' lineup earlier in the season, wins the NHL team's fastest skater award during a skills competition.

17 - The University of Waterloo's Eric Dingle wins the Ontario University Athletics squash player of the year award for a third consecutive season.

19 - The Kitchener Rangers send problem child Mike Mascioli home to cool off following the veteran's third confrontation with an on-ice official of the season.

26 - Sir John A. Macdonald's Andrew Mealing battles back from a knee injury to capture the all-Ontario high school alpine skiing championship at Blue Mountain.

27 - Wilfrid Laurier University's two Andreas, Bevan and Ironside, help Canada strike gold in women's hockey at the World University (FISU) Games in Harbin, China.

MARCH

5 - Laurier business student Alyssa Lagonia, of Kitchener, makes her debut with the national senior women's soccer team, a 1-1 draw with New Zealand at the Cyprus Cup.

5 - Close to 9,000 fans in London watch the Knights deliver the knockout punch to the Kitchener Rangers' OHL playoff hopes with a 7-1 pasting.

6 - Waterloo-Oxford's Jason Brenton-Hurst and St. David's Jake Jagas win their respective weight classes at the Ontario high school wrestling championships in Brampton.

8 - Kitchener teen Mitch O'Donnell wins gold at the Ontario track and field championships in record-setting fashion with a triple jump of 12.65 metres.

10 - Laurier's blueliner Andrea Bevan is named OUA hockey player of the year, while WLU's Rick Osborne is presented with his fifth outstanding coach award.

12 - Kitchener's Steve Dietrich and his longtime pal, coach Ian McGough, leave the National Lacrosse League's Edmonton Rush. Dietrich is traded to the Toronto Rock, while McGough quits his job as assistant coach, citing a lack of player commitment.

23 - Jordan Hardy ends the agony that is a quadruple overtime epic, as his winner lifts the Kitchener Dutchmen to a 6-5 decision over the Waterloo Siskins in Game 7 of a Mid-Western Conference hockey semifinal.

24 - St. David point guard Bryson Johnson wins District-8 MVP honours after he leads the Celtics to the local and regional boys' basketball championships.

24 - Cambridge's Bryan Little cracks the 30-goal mark with the Atlanta Thrashers in only his second NHL season.

25 - Laurier's Mark Voakes, named the Ontario-Quebec conference's outstanding player, and UW's Kyle Sonnenburg are voted first-team All-Canadian hockey players at a ceremony in Thunder Bay.

26 - St. Benedict's Chris Vasile wins the Waterloo County boys' hockey MVP award after potting a league-leading 18 goals.

31 - Mike Flemming, David Mathers, Nolan Karges and Dylan Cawker bring KCI its first provincial boys' curling championship.

APRIL

2 - Laurier curler Hollie Nicol wins the Outstanding Woman of Laurier award after leading her rink to back-to-back Canadian university crowns and a second-place finish at the FISU Games in Harbin, China.

2 - Sniper Alex Szczechura and his Brantford Golden Eagles trounce the Kitchener Dutchies 7-3 at the Brantford Civic Centre to claim the Mid-Western Conference's Jr. B hockey crown in five games. The Eagles go on to win the Ontario title.

8 - Sir John A. Macdonald's Justin Tomas is named the Moser Award winner as the region's top high school basketball player.

11 - The Cambridge Turbos claim the national ringette title with a 4-1 win over Edmonton.

18 - Kitchener's Colin Doyle notches a franchise-record 111 points for pro lacrosse's San Jose Stealth, four points shy of the NLL's single-season record.

20 - UW's assistant cross-country coach Stephen Drew is the top Canadian at the Boston Marathon, finishing 21st overall.

MAY

8 - Jr. A lacrosse's Kitchener-Waterloo Braves play their first home game outside Waterloo in 18 seasons, at Kitchener's Activa Sportsplex and suffer an 11-6 loss to the Six Nations Arrows.

10 - Waterloo native Matt Beiler wins the rings event at the Canadian gymnastics championships in Hamilton.

13 - Pan-Am and four-time Canadian champion boxer Mandy Bujold is named 2008 Kitchener-Waterloo and area athlete of the year.

24 - Six locals, representing Kitchener's Heritage Greens Lawn Bowling Club, establish a Canadian record for longest outdoor game, a gruelling 24 hours.

24 - Bluevale Collegiate's Stacey Jensen sets four world records at the Canadian powerlifting championships in Mississauga.

28 - Sir John A. Macdonald's players are pulled from a regional semifinal soccer game following a 30-player melee in a game against Eastwood Collegiate at Bechtel Park.

JUNE

5 - Eight varsity teams at Laurier - ranging from baseball to rugby - have their funding chopped by the school due to the economic downturn.

6 - Mitch O'Donnell of Cameron Heights sets a meet record in the triple jump at the Ontario high school track and field championships in Toronto. Calvin Arsenault, of St. Mary's, is first in the 400 metres.

7 - Local filly Milwaukee Appeal wins the $500,000 Woodbine Oaks.

11 - Cambridge message therapist John Carson helps bring the world's fastest man, Jamaica's Usain Bolt, to Toronto's Festival of Excellence.

11 - Three of the Region of Waterloo Swim Club's most famous sons, former club coach Cliff Barry and Olympic medallists Mike West and the late Victor Davis, are enshrined in the club's new hall of fame.

22 - Texas-born figure skater Kaitlyn Weaver takes her oath of citizenship at a Kitchener courthouse, clearing the path for her and her ice dancing partner, Waterloo's Andrew Poje, to represent Canada at the Vancouver Olympics.

27 - Kitchener lefty Mitch Clarke signs a $100,000 deal with Major League Baseball's Cincinnati Reds.

29 - David Edgar of Kitchener inks a four-year deal with Burnley of England's Premier Soccer League.

29 - Federal and provincial governments announce $2-million grants to fund repairs, saving Wilfrid Laurier's 50-metre pool.

JULY

2 - UW's Warrior Field snags $1.2 million in government money for improvements including a permanent grandstand, artificial turf and lighting.

7 - The Kitchener Rangers consider a new home, one that could cost upwards of $85-million, to replace the 58-year-old Aud.

13 - Cambridge's Jeff Hunt, a 15th round draft pick of the Los Angeles Dodgers, signs with the MLB club.

18 - Major Series Lacrosse's Kitchener-Waterloo Kodiaks set a franchise record for wins (nine) and best regular-season finish (fourth) following a 15-10 home-floor victory over the St. Regis Indians.

21 - Chicago blueliner John Moore signs a pro deal with Columbus and announces his intention to play for the Kitchener Rangers.

29 - Waterloo teen Matt LeMay wins his second straight Ontario bantam boys' golf title at Puslinch Lake.

AUGUST

2 - Kitchener siblings Jake and Matt Jagas combine for three gold medals at the national cadet/juvenile wrestling championships in Fredericton, N.B.

6 - London's Mitch Sutton shoots a six-under 286 to win the Canadian junior championship at Westmount Golf Club.

7 - Kitchener Rangers sign Gabriel Landeskog, a projected top NHL pick in 2011. He and countryman Patrik Andersson are to become the first Swedes to play for the OHL club.

7 - Waterloo Minor Hockey dumps an embattled Waterloo referees association, following a similar move by Wilfrid Laurier University's Lady Hawks.

12 - Eleven-year-old Doon Public School student Max Clift wins his age group at the Canadian Optimist junior national golf championships in Ingersoll with a one-shot victory.

13 - New Hamburg's Cam Burke defends his title at the Canadian men's amateur golf championship in Blainville, Que., carding his second consecutive 4-under 68 in the final round.

16 - Erin Thomas's one-hitter and game-winning RBI lifts the host Kitchener Krush to a 4-0 verdict over defending champs Winnipeg Smitty's in the title game at the Canadian senior women's fastpitch championship.

22 - In Moline, Ill., the Kitchener Rivershark Twins collect a 1-0 win over Nebraska's Broken Bow Patsy's and their second straight International Softball Congress senior men's world title.

22 - The Kitchener-based Tri-City Outlaws advance to their third straight Northern Football Conference title game, only to lose 31-18 to the eventual national senior football champs, the Sault Ste. Marie Steelers.

25 - Kitchener's Jake Jagas wins a pair of wrestling gold medals at the Canada Summer Games in Charlottetown, P.E.I. Baden's Jason Brenton-Hurst collects a wrestling bronze.

29 - Waterloo Collegiate grad and University of Vermont hotshot May Kotsopoulos pours in 37 points en route to a 111-44 romp over home-court Laurier in exhibition women's hoops action.

30 - Much-ballyhooed goalie Brandon Maxwell makes his first career start for the OHL's Kitchener Rangers in a 7-1 exhibition win over Brampton at the Aud.

30 - Bolstered by several current and former K-W Kodiaks, the Owen Sound Woodsmen defended their Canadian Sr. B lacrosse title with an 11-5 decision over British Columbia's Tri-City Bandits at Six Nations.

SEPTEMBER

1 - Edmonton's Lyall Adams claims the Canadian senior triples lawn bowls championship at Kitchener's Heritage Greens. Winnipeg's Elaine Jones wins the women's title.

1 - Nandi Kormendi takes over from departing coach Dean Boles following the latter's 22 year-run with the Region of Waterloo Swim Club.

6 - Blair Ezekiel's run-scoring single lifts the Kitchener Rivershark Twins to a 3-2 victory in nine innings over the Jarvis Gamblers and their first Canadian senior men's fastpitch title in the club's 42-year history.

13 - Close to 1,500 fans file into the new Woolwich Memorial Centre to watch the hometown Jr. B hockey Elmira Sugar Kings make their debut at the arena, a 5-3 loss to Waterloo.

15 - News breaks that Waterloo County football coaches pulled out of the Western Bowl because of safety concerns for their players.

18-20 - The Waterloo Jr. B hockey Siskins celebrate their 75th anniversary season with a weekend reunion.

24 - Retired Waterloo English teacher Ron VanKoughnett wins his age group at the world duathlon championship in Concord, N.C.

OCTOBER

2 - NHL prospect John Moore pots two goals in his Kitchener Rangers' debut, a 6-5 OT win over Saginaw.

3 - UW's gridiron Warriors are zapped by lightning delays and absorb a 34-24 loss to home-field Guelph, dashing the Warriors' playoff hopes for a sixth straight season.

10 - Kitchener boxer Mandy Bujold loses her Pan-Am title to Yanina Benavides of Argentina during a bout in Ecuador.

12 - Kari Jansen's fourth-inning, three-run blast cues the comeback for her Laurier Golden Hawks en route to their 7-3 victory over the University of Saskatchewan at the Canadian intercollegiate fastpitch championship.

18 - The NHL's Atlanta Thrashers practice before 1,400 fans at the new $23-million Woolwich Memorial Centre's Dan Snyder Arena in honour of their former teammate who died in a car crash in 2003.

26 - St. Mary's High School pulls the plug on its senior football team, citing a lack of player commitment.

28 - Kitchener Ranger Ben Fanelli is airlifted to a Hamilton hospital after he absorbs a check from Erie's Michael Liambas at the Aud. Fanelli suffers lacerations and skull fractures and lies on the ice convulsing before 6,000 horrified fans.

31 - Ontario University Athletics postpones a pair of women's hockey games because of the UW Warriors' two confirmed cases and five probable cases of H1N1 flu, the first of several such cancellations across the conference.

NOVEMBER

2 - Jim Balsillie's bid to buy the Phoenix Coyotes ends when a U.S. bankruptcy judge approves the sale of the money-losing franchise to the NHL.

4 - The OHL suspends Erie's Michael Liambas for the rest of the season because of his check on Rangers rookie Ben Fanelli.

5 - World champion water skier Whitney McClintock is named Cambridge's athlete of the year.

7 - Kitchener St. Mary's runner Jaimie Phelan wins gold at the Ontario high school cross-country championships in Vaughan.

7 - Breslau school St. John's-Kilmarnock notches a 3-0 victory over Nelson Lords of Burlington at Budd Park to capture a record fourth straight provincial field hockey title.

7 - Nathan Riva rushes for 279 yards to lift his Western Mustangs to a 26-16 verdict over home-field Laurier, before 3,472 fans in an Ontario university football semifinal.

9 - The Ontario Federation of Schools Athletic Association denies Saskatchewan transfer student Nicola Schaan's petition to play high school sports at KCI.

10 - Preston slugger Scott Thorman signs a one-year deal with MLB's Kansas City Royals.

12 - Laurier's Gary Jeffries wins his fourth Ontario university football coach of the year award in seven seasons. Laurier's defensive co-ordinator Ron VanMoerkerke is named the conference's top volunteer coach.

15 - Cambridge runner Lindsay Carson paces her Guelph Gryphons to a record-setting fifth consecutive team title at the Canadian Interuniversity Sport cross-country championships in Kingston, Ont.

19-22 - Canadian Joannie Rochette and her compatriots, ice dancers Tessa Virtue and Scott Moir, capture their respective divisions at Skate Canada International held at the Aud.

23 - The UW Warriors win their first provincial badminton title in four years.

26 - Laurier's grid star and Waterloo native Dillon Heap is named an All-Canadian after he piles up a Canadian university record 912 punt return yards and leads the nation in average all-purpose yards per game.

27 - An emotional Kitchener Rangers rookie Ben Fanelli is in the seats and receives a standing ovation from 6,422 OHL fans, his first trip to the Aud since he suffered head injuries in a game 28 days before.

29 - Former Laurier standouts Anthony Maggiacomo and Joel Wright are on the sidelines as their Montreal Alouettes win a dramatic 28-27 decision over the Saskatchewan Roughriders in the Grey Cup. Waterloo product Luca Congi and former UW offensive lineman Chris Best are on the wrong end of the thriller.

DECEMBER

4 - KCI point guard Rachael Urosevic is named Waterloo County girls' hoops MVP. Rockway's Laura Burnett earns D-8 honours earlier in the week.

11 - As part of Team Canada's staff, Kitchener Rangers coach/GM Steve Spott and trainer Dan Lebold leave for the world junior hockey championship in Saskatoon.

13 - Laurier grad John Morris helps his skip, Kevin Martin, punch their tickets for the Vancouver Games by beating former UW student Glenn Howard 7-3 at the Olympic curling trials in Edmonton.

17 - Skier Kelly VanderBeek's Olympic dreams are dashed when she tears the ligaments in her left knee after crashing during a training run in France.

Mark Leiren-Young
Arts & Life
Find out how weird the year was in this speed-read look at pop culture
Mark Leiren-Young
Special To The Sun
801 words
26 December 2009
Vancouver Sun
VNCS
Final
H2
English
Copyright © 2009 Vancouver Sun

It was the year of vampires and bloodsuckers, as fiction lovers feasted on undead heroes and the news dined on dead celebrities.

Vancouver mooned over New Moon as B.C. entered the Twilight zone and Harry Potter became "The Boy Who Was Yesterday's Pop Culture Phenom."

Balloon Boy was a bust, Susan Boyle was a must and Octomom was ... just plain disturbing.

Seriously, the Octomom thing ... creepy.

Trevor Linden retired, Mats Messier expired. Tony Parsons weighed anchor, but Bill Vander Zalm won't go away.

"Unfriended" was the word of the year and if you don't know what that means I'll tweet you about it.

Sexting replaced texting, which was a shock for a generation still getting used to e-mail.

It was the year pigs had wings as swine flu replaced bird flu -prompting a panic about the lack of vaccine by people who were in a panic to take the vaccine.

Alleged Prime Minister Stephen Harper sang the Beatles with Yo Yo Ma while Michael Ignatieff sang the coalition blues.

Premier Gordon Campbell invited the world for 2010 with a campaign showing off B.C.'s arts and culture, because the province is cutting off all funding for arts and culture after 2010.

The Bachelorette was from B.C., but still couldn't get online to buy Olympic tickets.

Prince Charles and Camilla, Duchess of Cornwall, arrived in B.C. and were immediately mistaken for Olympic mascots, Miga and Quatchi.

The Dalai Lama visited Vankarma to talk peace, but lost his temper when forced to drive down Cambie Street.

Osama bin Laden is still at large, but Polanski is no longer roaming.

Canada's hottest new music star was Leonard Cohen, prompting Avril Lavigne to make plans for her 80th birthday tour.

Tiger was a hound. Kanye West went south.

Paula Abdul-cated American Idol.

And Lady Gaga made the world miss the good old days when the always-demure Madonna did more than adopt African babies.

Barack Obama guest starred in The Amazing Spider-Man and immediately

received the Nobel Peace Prize for defeating Dr. Octopus.

Sarah Palin released a new book, Going Rogue -which is apparently best enjoyed with a fresh pack of crayons. Palin went from rogue to rouge after her shotgun-son-in-law, Levi Johnson, shed his Levis for Playgirl.

Slumdog Millionaire won the Oscar for Best Picture, prompting The Curious Case of Benjamin Button to shoot a new ending featuring a Bollywood dance number.

David f---ing Mamet was hired to script a new Anne Frank movie.

Looks like Miley Cyrus has her next gig. One can only hope Tarantino is directing.

Despite all evidence to the contrary Fox "News" is still called Fox News and America's most trusted news source, The Daily Show, is still called a comedy.

Jay Leno moved to 10 p.m. His audience didn't.

Battlestar Galactica frakking ended.

Corner Gas ran out of fuellishness.

Hockey players challenged figure skaters in The Battle of the Blades -which almost explains Don Cherry's outfits.

Marvel Comics Inc. was acquired by Disney and Mickey Mouse joined The X-Men.

The Pittsburgh Penguins beat a frumpy Scottish woman to win the Stanley Cup.

After interrupting Taylor Swift at the Grammys, Kanye West announced plans to interrupt winners at the Emmys and Oscars.

The Junos came to Vancouver to celebrate Surrey's adopted sons Nickelback with album of the year.

Kanye West declared the award should have gone to Beyonce.

Meanwhile, Nickelback was named "group of the decade" by Billboard, forever confirming this decade was tragically unhip.

The Barenaked Ladies stripped off Stephen Page and replaced him with a monkey.

Haven't you always wanted a monkey?

Chris Brown and Rihanna took their Ike and Tina Turner shtick too seriously.

Michael Buble did colour commentary for the Canucks, while Tommy Larscheid sang show tunes at the River Rock Casino.

Michael Jackson moonwalked off this mortal coil and everyone pretended they'd always been more interested in his music than where he lost that other glove.

That same day, Farrah Fawcett joined the angels.

If we had lost a Bee Gee or two it would have been the week the ''''70s died.

And that's how pop culture popped in 2009.

Time to settle in for some seal meat and vitamin water, while using that new app to adopt a Malawian orphan on your iPhone -which is illegal to hold while driving in 2010.

Mark Leiren-Young won the 2009 Stephen Leacock Medal for Humour for his debut book, Never Shoot a Stampede Queen -A Rookie Reporter in the Cariboo.

Canada & World
No shortage of Canadian political problems in 2009; The beginning and the end of the year found us wondering how Ottawa will go about ridding itself of a bulging federal deficit
Barbara Yaffe
Vancouver Sun
675 words
26 December 2009
Vancouver Sun
VNCS
Final
B1
English
Copyright © 2009 Vancouver Sun

How will we remember the political year that was 2009?

During the past dozen months Canada's two main parties experienced see-saw swings in support, while offering few solutions to the country's most daunting policy dilemmas.

As at the beginning of 2009, Canadians were left wondering at the end of the year how Ottawa will go about ridding itself of a bulging federal deficit that has ensconced itself on the Canadian body politic.

Wondering, too, how the country will reconcile Alberta oil-sands expansion with the imperatives of reducing greenhouse gas emissions.

And what Ottawa will do as a NATO member to address the mayhem and murder in Afghanistan post-2011 as a deadline for the Canadian Forces' military withdrawal fast approaches. While these hot potatoes simmered, the political parties were on full boil with their own problems.

Liberals, last January, were raring to go with their new leader, Michael Ignatieff. Polls had them tied with the governing Conservatives by the time the tulips bloomed in Ottawa. Then -- whoosh -- all the petals fell off in the cool autumn air as Liberal support cratered.

The Conservatives, meanwhile, who were in the dumpster in early January following Stephen Harper's desperate appeal for a parliamentary recess last Christmas, spent 2009 climbing steadily in the polls.

The party's support reached majority territory by October when the PM dared to play the piano for voters at a National Arts Centre gala and, in doing so, looked--ye gods--human.

It was a year when New Democrats had some success, finding gut-punching issues to champion -- the harmonized sales tax adoption in B.C. and Ontario, and the pensions plight. But the party remains no closer to convincing voters it's a realistic governing alternative at the federal level. The election everyone widely anticipated throughout the year never did materialize. But at least the notion has been put to bed for a while, given the Grits' faltering condition. Meanwhile, a Nanos poll announced in December that Canadians are in a better mood than a year ago, with 64 per cent thinking Canada is moving in the right direction, up from 53 per cent in 2008.

Some fun stuff happened in 2009 as well.Gov.-Gen. Michaelle Jean got herself a new Afro hairdo. Defence Minister Peter MacKay finally got himself a would-be wife in Jana Juginovic. And U.S. President Barack Obama paid a visit to Ottawa, for a few hours.

Prince Charles and Camilla, the Duchess of Cornwall, also dropped in and stayed longer, but, in terms of excitement and crowds, there was no contest between the two visits.

Canada got nine new Conservative senators in 2009, to join 18 others appointed last December. The tally currently is 46 Conservative senators and 51 Liberals. Any guesses about which party will lay claim to the six additional seats in the Red Chamber that will need filling by the end of 2010?

This was a year when Canadians finally got the truth, or as near as they'll ever get, in two separate government inquiries: the Braidwood Inquiry into the 2007 Vancouver International Airport death of Robert Dziekanski after he was repeatedly Tasered by four Mounties and the Oliphant Inquiry into business dealings between Brian Mulroney and Karlheinz Schreiber. Testimony at both events was nauseating.

It was also a year for hand-wringing about climate change and how Canada's petro-powered economy is going to adapt to a new world order.

A year, too, for hand-washing as people got the willies about the H1N1 virus even if they didn't get the flu itself. Of course, the past dozen months were devoted to preparing for Vancouver's 2010 Olympic Games, a spectacle that lent the year end a delicious air of excited anticipation.

Editorial
Apocalypse, when?
The Ottawa Citizen
632 words
26 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
B6
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

I am old enough to remember reading The Late Great Planet Earth by author Hal Lindsey during the late 1970s. The premise of the book was based on Christian eschatology and how prophecies of the end times were unfolding before our very eyes. The book predicted an increase in earthquakes, pestilence, famines and wars, which, at the time, seemed a bit of an exaggeration.

As I recall the '70s, besides having some of the lamest music ever recorded, there seemed to be a lot fewer of the aforementioned signs of the end of the world than there are today. In the past decade alone, we have had SARS, Mad Cow Disease, Avian flu and H1N1. It seems every time a new viral strain emerges, we have the potential of a pandemic.

Then there's the growing tension between Iran and Israel, war in Afghanistan and Pakistan, with the growing fear about some extremist terrorist group getting access to nukes. Even the Mayans are getting into it by predicting the end of the world at exactly 11:11 a.m., December 12, 2012. It is, therefore, no coincidence that two of my favourite cartoons I drew this year are based on the Four Horsemen of the Apocalypse.

Speaking of End Times, I should mention how much I've enjoyed cartooning the floundering Liberal party. Polls indicate that the party is in a free fall under the leadership of Michael Ignatieff and, as it turns out, he has quickly become one of my favourite targets of all time.

I thought Stéphane Dion was great fodder, but I have to say Ignatieff is like manna from heaven. It's been so difficult for Ignatieff that he even appointed an old Chrétien aide, Peter Donolo, as his chief of staff. Please, Donolo, don't change a thing; I like Michael Ignatieff exactly as he is.

This was also the first year of Barack Obama's presidency. After a slow start, editorial cartoonists have successfully been able to draw cartoons critical of the man and his policies without concern that by doing so we would unwittingly stir up racial imagery, or, more likely, too greatly upset his adoring fans.

Obama showed that he is not superhuman and a sense of public disappointment is beginning to set in.

As I reflect on his presidency, it strikes me as marked by contradictions. He has presided over a jobless recovery, and has received the Nobel Peace Prize while escalating the war in Afghanistan.

I'm not sure where this is headed, but suddenly, in cartoons, we see him being drawn shorter and with bigger ears. The mystique is falling away.

One of the highlights for me this year was winning the National Newspaper Award for editorial cartooning. It was my second NNA and, as happened the first time I won, the hardest cartoon to draw was the one immediately following. I still remember how I felt after the first win, as if I needed to prove why I was chosen.

This year was no different and I produced what I consider one of my better efforts: a drawing that featured Uncle Sam in an old saloon scene with Kim Jong Il quoting the line, "I am become Death ..." Some cartoons I like better than others, and I like that one.

Editorial cartoons that work best are those featuring a nice graphic with beautiful rendering, backed by a solid editorial statement. But these things are often matters of personal taste, and can vary from reader to reader.

All my cartoons won't be to everyone's liking, but my hope is that more often than not they will resonate.

As for next year, I think we're still OK. The Mayans didn't mention 2010.

Saturday Observer
Age of Anxiety; Nothing to fear but fear itself?
Robert Sibley
The Ottawa Citizen
2809 words
26 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
B1 / FRONT
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

Not in the '00s, a decade riven with uncertainty and the fear of asteroids, BSE, SARS,

avian flu, swine flu, bio-engineered viruses, nanotechnology, nuclear war, global warming and countless other doomsday scenarios

By Robert Sibley

... Uncertain and afraid

As the clever hopes expire

Of a low dishonest decade:

Waves of anger and fear

Circulate over the bright

And darkened lands of the earth ...

-- W.H. Auden, September 1, 1939.

t is a mere calendar convention that sees us mark the arrival and departure of a new decade with the neat demarcations of zero. Historical periods, as journalist George Packer observes, seldom conform to clean-cut, 10-year periods. Rather, "eras are defined less precisely but more truly by events, a prevailing moral atmosphere, what it felt like to live during a certain time."

The Depression era -- the Dirty Thirties -- stretched from the Wall Street collapse on Oct. 29, 1929, to the opening salvoes of the Second World War on Sept. 1, 1939. The Sixties were a decade of hedonism and hysteria, beginning with John Kennedy's assassination on Nov. 22, 1963, and ending with the last helicopter lifting off the roof of the U.S. Embassy in Saigon on April 29, 1975. The 1990s were a 12-year holiday from history -- or, perhaps, a fantasy about the end of history -- that began with the fall of the Berlin Wall on Nov. 9, 1989, and concluded with the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks on the United States.

What about this first decade of the 21st century? It has been a short one, I would argue. It began, obviously, with the 9/11 terrorist strikes on the Twin Towers, zigzagged through the dot.com bubble and Enron, segued into nasty wars on the bloody borders of the West (still ongoing), and wrapped up with the collapse of Lehman Brothers in September 2008 and the near-meltdown of the world's financial system.

Some, no doubt, will wish to conclude this foreshortened decade on a positive note, preferring Barack Obama's assumption of the U.S. presidency in January as a more suitable ending. The new president certainly offered the rhetoric of hope, at least for a few months, but it is the debacle in the financial system that endures. In any case, if, as Packer suggests, eras are marked by "what it felt like to live" during that period, by its "moral atmosphere," then it seems to me the seven years between 9/11 and the onset of the global financial crisis possessed a particular moral atmosphere, a distinct "feel."

This decade was certainly marked by uncertainty. Whether it was fear of terrorist attacks, puzzlement at the weaknesses of nation-states, bewilderment at the way technology continues to alter human relationships (think of the emergence of social media), concern about mass migration and demographic change, or even an inchoate sense of a dispirited world spinning out of control, there is little question the '00s were as riven with anxiety as the '90s were cocooned in complacency.

If so, it is not unreasonable to describe the past decade as an age of anxiety, or, more accurately perhaps, an era of displaced anxiety. And if that is the case, as I contend, then it seems to me the singular characteristic of the new century's first decade -- its "feel," if you will -- was moral panic.

British sociologist Stanley Cohen coined the phrase "moral panic" to describe the public reaction to the huge fights between English gangs -- the working-class Rockers and the middle-class Mods -- during the 1960s. Newspapers portrayed the clashes in sensational terms, casting them as a symptom of Britain's cultural and geo-political decline.

Cohen, however, thought the media were indulging in moral panic; that is to say, promoting unwarranted concern about an ostensible threat posed to the values and principles that sustain an orderly society. The characteristics of moral panic are, he wrote, "a condition, episode, person or group of persons (that) become defined as a threat to societal values and interests." Cohen labelled the promoters of moral panic -- whether politicians, interest groups, academics or journalists -- as "moral entrepreneurs" who, unwittingly or otherwise, amplify the nature of a particular situation out of all proportion to its reality and, thus, generate widespread fear and anxiety.

Moral panics have a long history. Adolf Hitler's warning in 1938 that "the Jew will destroy civilization by poisoning us all" was an extreme example of moral panic. But history is replete with collective panic attacks -- from the witch hunts of 17th century Europe and white slavery in Victorian England to claims of widespread satanic ritual abuse in North America during the 1960s and, most recently, the oh-my-gawd-we're-all-gonna-die hysteria surrounding swine flu.

But panics occur at even the most mundane level. Barry Cooper, a political scientist at the University of Calgary, points out that small-town religious leaders denounced the arrival of the car and the radio in the 1920s as conducive to backseat sex. Most every dance craze or musical fashion from the waltz to rap has been condemned for promoting immoral behaviour. "Moral panics are reactions to bad events that, to common sense, seem disproportionate," says Cooper. And "indignation and outrage" are the most common tone of those who promote moral panic. (You hear a great deal of moral entrepreneurship during question period in the House of Commons.)

n

Moral panic may seem a puzzling characterization of our times. Despite our economic woes, the threat of terrorist attacks, the ongoing war in Afghanistan and concerns about "global warming" and "climate change," most of us continue to enjoy the bounty of prosperity and technology: plentiful food, decent shelter, warm clothing, reasonably safe streets, general good health, a wealth of cultural opportunities, and a host of high-tech diversions to keep ourselves entertained.

Yet we are fearful and anxious. "Fear plays a key role in 21st century consciousness," says British sociologist Frank Furedi. "The rise of catch phrases such as the 'politics of fear,' 'fear of crime' and 'fear of the future' is testimony to the cultural significance of fear today. Many of us seem to make sense of our experiences through the narrative of fear." Indeed, historian Joanna Bourke regards fear as "the most pervasive emotion of modern society."

The 2004 U.S. election provided some good (read: bad) examples of the politics of fear. The Republicans ran advertisements showing growling wolves slouching through a dark wood, while a voiceover warned the electorate "weakness attracts those who are waiting to do America harm." The Democrats were no better, with Al Gore ringing the bell of doom with his warnings about Iraq as another Vietnam and the imminence of environmental apocalypse. We've seen something similar, if sillier, in recent Canadian elections, when the Liberals and New Democrats cast Conservative leader Stephen Harper as an ogre.

The political use of fear is more than a campaign tactic, however. It would not be effective if it did not reflect a prevailing mindset. Perhaps the most obvious example of moral panic is the overblown anxiety attached to environmental concerns. Opinion polls consistently show that many Canadians and Americans are convinced environmental catastrophe is around the corner, that we'll soon run out of natural resources (including oxygen) and that population growth dooms us to mass starvation -- never mind that such dire warnings have been around for 40 years and, so far, none has come true.

Paul Ehrlich's The Population Bomb, published in 1968, warned of mass planetary starvation by the 1980s. In 1972, the Club of Rome predicted apocalypse by overpopulation. Both theses ended up on the compost heap of history in the wake of the Green Revolution. The biggest fear in the 1970s, though, was global cooling. In late 1972, a group of scientists wrote to then U.S. president Richard Nixon, saying the world's climate might change by an "order of magnitude larger than any hitherto experience by civilized mankind."

The media loved the idea, of course. By 1974, Time magazine reported that "a growing number of scientists" studying "the bizarre and unpredictable weather pattern of the past several years" were wondering if "a global climatic upheaval" might be in the works. "There are ominous signs that the Earth's weather patterns have begun to change dramatically," Newsweek declared in 1975, "and that these changes may portend a dramatic decline in food production -- with serious implications for just about every nation on Earth."

Since the 1990s, we've heard dire warnings from numerous moral entrepreneurs -- Al Gore and David Suzuki come to mind -- but freezing is no longer fashionable. Today's popular doomsday scenario is that we'll fry, drown or choke to death because of "global warming" and/or "climate change." As Barry Cooper says, "the biggest moral panic" nowadays is that surrounding the question of climate change and what role, if any, humans play in it.

Sundry "experts" often turn themselves into moral entrepreneurs, inadvertently or not. "Life on Earth is at the ever-increasing risk of being wiped out by a disaster, such as global warming, nuclear war, a genetically engineered virus or other dangers we have no yet thought of," Stephen Hawking, the celebrated physicist, said in a 2006 lecture.

But moral panic is not simply associated with high-profile threats such as terrorist attacks or "global warming." There is also the constant anxiety of everyday life that makes us, in political theorist Zygmunt Bauman's phrase, prone to being "neurotic about matters of security." As journalist Christopher Booker and Richard North, an environmental health officer, write, the "environmental scare" is of a piece with other "moral panics" that grip the western mind -- everything from AIDS and BSE to SARS, avian flu, and, most recently, swine flu (or H1N1 as the technocrats prefer).

n

I don't want to suggest that concerns about the environment are unwarranted, or that concerns about pandemics, geo-political events and economic problems are unjustified. Clearly we should clean up the rivers and lakes, minimize pollution and curb deforestation. What is problematic about many of our moral panics, though, is the mood of catastrophism, the implication mankind is doomed to do itself in unless extraordinary measures are undertaken and immense sacrifices made. Such rhetoric -- the rhetoric of fear -- is fertile soil for demagogues and authoritarian rule.

"Our constitution continues to work, the courts continue to work, and people by and large continue to conform to the laws and policies that compromise our social and political order," says Carleton University political theorist Peter Emberley. "Yet we have this kind of self-fabricated conception that we are on the brink, that we're on the edge of some world-ending abyss, that the apocalypse is just around the corner."

But then even if the end-times fail to live up to their billing, if the flu viruses lack virility, well, we still have plenty of moral entrepreneurs to warn us about second-hand cigarette smoke and obesity. It sometimes seems we are half in love with dreams of doom, at least judging by our proclivity for and response to disaster movies. NASA received dozens of phone calls and e-mails from people frightened 2012 might come true. One woman, according to news reports, wondered if she could avoid the disaster by killing her daughter, her unborn baby and herself. Another asked whether she should euthanize her dog so it wouldn't suffer in 2012. But 2012, which is based on much-debated Mayan prophecies of doom, is only the latest is a long list of end-of-the-world-as-we-know-it films -- from 2004's The Day After Tomorrow and the Road Warrior trilogy in the early 1980s to Planet of the Apes in 1968, When World's Collide in 1951, and, in 1938, Orson Welles' radio broadcast of H.G. Wells' story War of the Worlds. That broadcast, incidentally, created panic throughout the United States, as many believed the Martians really were invading.

Our sense of panic is only inflated by books such as Our Final Century, by Royal Society president Martin Rees, who lists bio-engineered viruses, asteroids, nanotechnology and nuclear war as among the many dangers we face. Tomes on peak oil theory such as Richard Heinberg's The Party's Over and The Long Emergency by James Kunstler take moral panic to pragmatic levels.

Perhaps this propensity for doom mongering is something of a cultural peccadillo, an impulse rooted in our Christian heritage and its prophecies of end-times and the Judgement Day to come. On the other hand, doomsday movies and books -- say, for example, Cormac McCarthy's The Road, or Stephen King's The Stand, or, my favourite, George Stewart's Earth Abides, a 1949 novel in which most of the human race falls victim to an airborne pandemic -- may provide a cathartic release from the undercurrent of anxiety that, in the words of British sociologist Phil Hubbard, "saturates the social space of everyday life."

Psychologists and sociologists have linked our inclination for moral panic to the rapidity of social, political and technological change. Shifts in long-established social traditions and moral standards -- the waning of traditional marriage and the emergence of same-sex marriage, for example -- are psychically unsettling, they say. Too much change too fast leaves people disoriented and uprooted, which fosters an anxiety-inducing feeling that the world is increasingly senseless and, therefore, meaningless.

"Rather than learning to cope with (rapid social change), we are in fact becoming more vulnerable to experiencing our world as a place of threatening uncertainty," says British sociologist Iain Wilkinson. The result, he says, is "a growing 'sense of crisis' that has produced "a 'social state of mind' which is pessimistic to the point of 'paralysing desperation'." Wilkinson draws on the thought of scholars to argue that "'we have got used to talking as though the future is not only provisional, but is dominated by dangers rather than opportunities'."

This crisis of self-confidence has consequences, both for the individual and society. At the social level, citizens are increasingly open to the blandishments of the moral entrepreneurs, who, in their warnings of bad things to come, promise that they can solve our uncertainty, ease our anxiety. And therein lies the real threat. Misplaced anxiety opens the door to the politics of fear and the temptations of political demagogues, and, ultimately, to the soft totalitarianism of rule-by-experts. Anxious and uncertain, a panic-prone citizenry lacks not only the moral stamina to insist on the rule of common sense, but also the moral courage to defend its freedom.

At the individual level, living under the constant strain of anxiety may well prompt a flight to whatever diversions -- from video games and pornography to drugs and celebrity worship -- offer a release from fear or numb the undercurrent of anxiety. People stop thinking about the future -- including, arguably, avoiding having children -- because the future looks too scary. Better to live for the present.

"It's possible that people don't have high hopes for the future. It does strike me that we don't talk about the future anymore in sweeping terms," says Waller Newell, a political theorist at Carleton University. He suggests our anxiety, our moral panic, is rooted in the fear of things slipping away. "There's nothing epoch-making that collectively excites us. We don't have any great millennial expectations. We want to hold on to what we've got."

No great expectations. A sad epitaph for a decade, to be sure, but that was the moral atmosphere of the last seven years, that's what it felt like.

Robert Sibley is a senior writer at the Citizen.

Colour Photo: Doug Kanter, Afp/Getty Images / 2001 After the collapse of the first World Trade Center Tower in New York on Sept. 11, 2001, a man stands in the rubble and calls out to those in need of help. Photo: War and terror In an unprecedented show of contempt in Baghdad, U.S. troops pulled down a statue of Saddam Hussein on April 9, 2003. Earlier, Iraqis attacked the base of the statue with a sledgehammer. Photo: Darfur was already in crisis when refugees from nearby Chad started to make their way to the region in western Sudan. Photo: On July 7, 2005, a series of co-ordinated terrorists blasts rocked London and killed 52 people, injuring more than 770. News
H1N1, Chalk River made it a busy 2009 for rookie health minister
Meagan Fitzpatrick
Canwest News Service
769 words
26 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
A16
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

Health Minister Leona Aglukkaq had many worries as she led Canada's response to the H1N1 pandemic this year, but one personal anxiety often trumped all others -- her son.

"My biggest fear ... was that I would get H1N1 and bring it home to my son and to my husband," Aglukkaq said in a recent interview following her 49th press conference on the pandemic. "That was very stressful."

She was travelling a lot, constantly interacting with people, including health-care workers, and Aglukkaq could have easily picked up the virus along her way.

"It was at the back of my mind, if I get this flu I'm not going to my house because there's no vaccine yet," she said.

Like other parents, she anxiously awaited its approval so that her 18-month-old son, Cooper, could be protected from the H1N1 virus. He was immunized as soon as the vaccine was made available to Canadians in late October.

"I would say it's been a very interesting, rewarding, challenging year for me," the health minister said.

Challenging indeed for a minister who had just barely settled into the job after arriving on Parliament Hill in the fall of 2008.

It was baptism by fire for the new MP, who, within weeks of being sworn-in as health minister, was dealing with a medical isotope shortage because of a shutdown at the Chalk River reactor facility.

A few months later, a mystery virus in Mexico turned into a worldwide pandemic and Aglukkaq was the lead minister in charge of Canada's response. At the same time, Chalk River shut down again, this time indefinitely. In between managing the pandemic and the ongoing isotope shortage, Aglukkaq was trying to push tobacco legislation and a bill on consumer product safety through the House of Commons.

She's faced her share of criticism and skepticism about whether a rookie minister such as herself is competent enough to handle the job.

The soft-spoken 42-year-old says the only person she needs to prove anything to is herself.

She's new to the federal scene, yes, but she's had a long career in public service, Aglukkaq notes. She was a municipal councillor for six years before moving to territorial government, where she served as deputy minister for several portfolios and, when elected as a member of the legislative assembly in Nunavut, she was named finance minister, house leader and then health minister.

"I might come from what they call a small territory, but my riding is bigger and larger than Ontario. Three time zones, 25 isolated communities, and to deliver health care in that environment is far more challenging than downtown Toronto," said Aglukkaq.

She's been open and honest with Canadians, the health minister says, and she purposefully resists partisan comebacks when under attack from the opposition.

"At the end of the day, as a Canadian, as an individual from this country, if I saw my health minister and whoever, getting into the games of party politics instead of health and safety, I would be concerned," she explained.

But Liberal MP Carolyn Bennett flatly rejects Aglukkaq's claims that she's been transparent and stayed above the political fray.

"I am so sad about the partisan nature and that when we were trying to be constructive, trying to help, we would just get outright nonsense," said Bennett, her party's health critic.

Bennett said she had "high hopes" for Aglukkaq when she was appointed because she was familiar with her "excellent experience and record." When Aglukkaq was health minister at the territorial level, Bennett was Canada's minister of state for public health at the federal level.

Now, Bennett says she is "unbelievably disappointed" by Aglukkaq's performance. She's not communicating well with Canadians, not listening to the provinces and territories and has shown a partisan streak, said Bennett.

"This is not the Leona I knew as the minister in Nunavut. ... I just can't believe that she can say what she is being asked to say in terms of the partisanship," said the Toronto MP.

The health minister, however, says she's been guided by the expectations of Canadians over the last year.

"My task here was to focus on what would a Canadian want of me and what would I expect of my health minister if we were in the middle of a pandemic," said Aglukkaq.

- - Kudos go to the woman who put one-and-a-half shopping carts full of toys directly into the Santas Anonymous bin at the Toys R Us store on the south side. If only there were more people like her.

- - We want to thank the three young people who helped us get our truck unstuck in -25 C weather. There was a nasty ice patch at the entrance to the car wash and we slid straight into a two-foot snowdrift. While several other people drove by, these young people got out and pushed until we were free. They were our guardian angels.

- - I was in Walmart when I saw a medical emergency. The store's first-aid person wasn't responding to the page, so I stepped forward to help and asked the cashier to put my items aside. All turned out well -- the patient is doing fine -- so when the medical personnel arrived, I returned to the till to pay. My items were already bagged and the cashier said the man behind me in line paid for my $37 worth of purchases. How heartwarming.

- - Due credit to all the staff at Edmonton's H1N1 flu clinics for their efforts in carrying out the Herculean task of immunizing tens of thousands of citizens in such a short time. They carried out their work with efficiency and an excellent attitude.

- - It always seems the best customer service is provided at the lower-end discount stores.

News
What put the pop in pop culture in 2009; High-and lowlights of the year just past
Misty Harris
Canwest News Service
1369 words
26 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
B6
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

Call it two-thousand-and-swine: the year we were either sick with H1N1 or just plain sick of hearing about it. On the economic front, pink slips became the new black, while the entertainment world tried to make sense of a deity that took Michael Jackson, Patrick Swayze and Brittany Murphy but somehow left the cast of New Moon unscathed.

Of course, 2009 wasn't without its uplifting moments -- none more literal than those courtesy of "balloon boy." And you can't deny the fun factor of a year that, in the span of two days, saw U.S. President Barack Obama win the Nobel Peace Prize, NASA bomb the moon and Marge Simpson pose for Playboy.

Today, we look back at the moments that put the pop in Canadian culture in 2009.

The Newsmakers

1. Michael Jackson

The King of Pop's untimely death left the world struggling to reconcile the legend who brought them Thriller with the man-child who sought shelter among children and monkeys at a ranch called Neverland. The singer's online memorial service was watched by an estimated one billion people worldwide.

2. Jon and Kate Gosselin Not since Brad gave Jen her walking papers have culture vultures been so swept up in a celebrity divorce, with millions tuning into TLC to witness the reality-TV couple's unravelling. The Gosselins eventually took their fight to the tabloids, making them harder to avoid than the flu -- and equally bad for public health.

3. Tiger Woods

This year, America's highest-earning athlete turned into a one-man infideli-tainment show, with allegations of extramarital sex romps, conspiratorial voice messages and enough rumoured mistresses to make Charl ie Sheen blush. Amid talks of a high-priced divorce, Woods announced a hiatus from golf -- and, presumably, cocktail waitresses.

4. Nadya "Octomom" Suleman

Talk about a womb with a view: in January, Suleman gave birth to octuplets -- only the second full set born alive in the U.S. -- which took her tally of children to 14 from six. When it came to light that the single mother was unemployed and living on public assistance, widespread debate ensued over the ethical use of assisted reproductive technology.

5. Lady Gaga

With four No. 1 hits, a relentless appearance schedule, and a fashion sense so eclectic as to make Bjork's swan dress look understated, the singer earned enough media coverage this year to drain a small city's ink supplies.

Also newsworthy: Michael Phelps (we learned there was more behind the Olympic swimmer's red eyes than chlorine after he was photographed taking a hit off a bong); singer Susan Boyle (she "dreamed a dream" and it came true, with her debut album drawing the biggest opening-week sales of any album this year); Sarah Palin (the former U.S. vice-presidential candidate went "rogue" with a new book, and we were all forced along for the ride).

The Moments

1. Celebrity deaths

It's hard to recall a recent year in which there were as many premature celebrity deaths as were seen in 2009. Among the most startling losses were Brittany Murphy, 32, Jett Travolta, 16, Exodus Tyson 4, Natasha Richardson, 45, David Carradine, 72, Farrah Fawcett, 62, John Hughes, 59, DJ AM, 36, Patrick Swayze, 57, and, of course, Michael Jackson, 50.

2. Social media

With more than 420 million users between them, Facebook, Twitter and LinkedIn are perfect examples of how social media are, to a large extent, becoming the new mass media. At the height of June's Iranian protests, Twitter emerged as one of the most reliable sources of communication from the front lines when text messaging and mobile services were severed by authorities.

Time magazine subsequently reported that "the Revolution will not be televised, but it will be tweeted."

3. Undead mania

If Anne Rice wasn't embarrassed for her genre already, 2009 surely did the trick, with everything from bloodthirsty Barbie dolls to vampirethemed sanitary napkins hitting the market. Most notably, the Twilight sequel New Moon saw its gaggle of glittering vamps set a record for the biggest single-day domestic gross ever at the box office, with the movie going on to earn more than $626 million US worldwide. Zombies came a close second, thanks to the surprise lit-hit Pride and Prejudice and Zombies and the Woody Harrelson zom-com Zombieland.

4. Balloon boy

Three days after the world was held captive by live footage of a giant runaway balloon said to be carrying six-year-old Falcon Heene, investigators discovered the whole thing was a hoax.

Somewhere in Hollywood, Pixar producers breathed a sigh of relief that the movie Up wouldn't have to be augmented with a disclaimer.

5. Celebrity Fight Club

Kanye West vs. Taylor Swift, Chris Brown vs. Rihanna, Mariah Carey vs. Eminem, Candy Spelling vs. Tori Spelling, Perez Hilton vs. will. i. am, Gosselin vs. Gosselin, Sarah Palin vs. Levi Johnston, Kiefer Sutherland vs. Jack McCollough. Yes, 2009 was the year the headlines looked less like news stories than boxing fight cards.

Also momentous: Oprah announces her decision to end her talk show, Marge Simpson appears on the cover of Playboy, Barack Obama wins the Nobel Peace Prize, a monosyllabic Joaquin Phoenix bombs on Letterman, Christian Bale is caught on tape unleashing verbal hell on a crew member.

Pop Parlance

1. Swine flu: Politically incorrect name for the H1N1 virus that sent Canadian hand-sanitizer sales up 189 per cent and Miss Piggy into hiding (see also: aporkalypse).

2. Twitter: The social media site -- which features posts of no more than 140 characters -- hit its stride this year, proving people could show just as much economy with their words as their wallets.

3. Climate change: The politically neutral phrase that was to global warming what H1N1 was to swine flu.

4. Unfriend: Named "word of the year" by Oxford, this verb describes the act of removing a friend from one's online social network.

5. Gleek: A fan of the hit TV show Glee, which racked up four Golden Globe nominations and helped make show choirs cool again (assuming they ever were).

Honourable mentions: "Opposite marriage," a description of same-sex marriage used by (now dethroned) Miss California Carrie Prejean, a family values champion ultimately revealed to have had a pre-pageant boob job and secret sex tape; "Sexting," which is the exchange of sexually explicit text messages (see also: Tiger Woods).

YouTube Sensations

1. Susan Boyle -- Britain's Got Talent (more than 100 million views).

2. David After Dentist (more than 36 million views).

3. JK Wedding Entrance Dance (more than 33 million views).

4. New Moon movie trailer (more than 29 million views).

5. Evian Roller Babies (more than 26 million views).

The "Newlys"

1. Newly married: Kevin Jonas and Danielle Deleasa; Josh Duhamel and Stacy "Fergie" Ferguson; Salma Hayek and Francois-Henri Pinault; Tom Brady and Gisele Bundchen; Mandy Moore and Ryan Adams; David Letterman and Regina Lasko; Maggie Gyllenhaal and Peter Sarsgaard; Alyssa Milano and David Bugliari; Robert Redford and Sibylle Szaggars; Ivanka Trump and Jared Kushner; Michael

C. Hall and Jennifer Carpenter; Mark Wahlberg and Rhea Durham.

2. Newly split: Mel Gibson and Robyn Moore; Usher and Tameka Foster; Bradley Whitford and Jane Kaczmarek; Billy and Katie Lee Joel; Jon and Kate Gosselin; Avril Lavigne and Deryck Whibley; Sean Penn and Robin Wright; LeAnn Rimes and Dean Sheremet.

3. Newly born: Charlie Axel, to Tiger Woods and Elin Nordegren; Seraphina Rose, to Jennifer Garner and Ben Affleck; Max and Bob, twins to Charlie Sheen; Atlas, to Anne Heche; Ikhyd Edgar Arular, to rapper M.I.A.; Mars Merkaba, to Erykah Badu; Mercy James, adopted by Madonna; Marion Loretta Elwell and Tabitha Hodge, twins to Sarah Jessica Parker and Matthew Broderick; Sparrow James Midnight, to Nicole Richie and Joel Madden; Stella Luna, to Ellen Pompeo; Anniston Kae, to Chyler Leigh; Naleigh, adopted by Katherine Heigl and Josh Kelley; Lou Sulola Samuel, to Seal and Heidi Klum.

IT WAS A SILLY YEAR
Compiled from Hamilton Spectator news services
1096 words
26 December 2009
The Hamilton Spectator
HMSP

G03
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Hamilton Spectator.

Well, that was interesting. That's about the best -- and worst -- you can say about 2009, a year that seems to have had twin personalities: one of them profound and sombre, the other a goofy distraction from the important stuff. It was a year marked by continuing struggles with the economy and unemployment, a battle over health-care reform and the H1N1 flu. It's also been 12 wacky months of media frenzy over Jon and Kate Gosselin, the balloon boy, real housewives, Lady Gaga, vampires and more. Um, 2009, we'd like to introduce you to 2009. Can you two get your act together and figure out how to be more cohesive by 2010? It's been exhausting keeping up with both of you. Here's a look at some of the people and events that rocked the pop-culture world this year, from the sublime to the ridiculous.

Fastest fall from grace: The good news for Tiger Woods? He's the Associated Press athlete of the decade. The bad news? Every other recent story about him. The scandal involving the world's best golfer has prompted an avalanche of jokes about his infidelity and alleged mistresses. But is there anything funny about instantly going from a hero to a punch line?

Most unexpected overnight fame: The last time someone became famous for looking like a middle-aged British woman in a dowdy housedress, it was Monty Python. But a video clip of Susan Boyle performing a ballad from Les Miz became a global web sensation. Talk about dreaming an online dream.

Most unwelcome overnight fame: Can someone tell White House party-crashers Tareq and Michaele Salahi their 15 minutes are up?

Most riveting tribute: Michael Jackson's death in June prompted a feeding frenzy of cable coverage, speculation and rumour. But the songs and tributes at the Staples Center memorial service brought humanity to an entertainer who emerged, tragically, as the most talked-about star of the year.

Worst attempt to get attention with a Jiffy Pop look-alike: Remember the day when everyone was sick with anxiety at the thought of a little boy flying around in the sky in a crazy homemade balloon? Well, let's try to forget it -- if only to remind fame-seeking parents such as Richard Heene that hoaxes like this won't get you a reality show.

Best interruption: Captain Chesley (Sully) Sullenberger's spectacular impromptu jet landing in the Hudson River, which turned a potential disaster into a slight change in flight plans.

Worst interruption: Kanye West's uninvited impromptu remarks during Taylor Swift's win at the MTV VMAs. Even if he had brought Sully onstage with him, no one would have clapped.

Most persuasive argument for keeping your mouth shut at work: The audiotape of Christian Bale's expletive-spewing meltdown on the set of Terminator: Salvation.

Least intriguing couple who kept trying to be the most intriguing: Can anyone recall why celebrity journalism was once obsessed with Jon and Kate Gosselin's marital breakup? Ah, yes, that was the pre-Tiger era.

Worst activity to pretend you're doing when you're really in South America and having an affair with your "soulmate": As the married South Carolina Governor Mark Sanford discovered, it's hiking the Appalachian Trail.

Most surprising moment for a late-night host: It seemed like just another weeknight on a talk show until David Letterman dropped a bombshell out of the blue. Describing an alleged extortion plan against him, the Late Show host candidly shared the details and admitted that he had slept with female staffers. At least he didn't turn the revelation into a Top Ten list.

Most disappointing ratings for a not-so-late-night-anymore host: Jay Leno's NBC show at 10 p.m. debuted to about 18 million viewers in September, but the audience plummeted in the months that followed. One bright spot? He's moved "Headlines" back to its rightful place after the monologue.

Best attempt by a movie to deal with the economy: Capitalism: A Love Story was more specifically about the financial meltdown. But Up in the Air, a funny movie about a guy who flies around the country firing people, is just as deep in its own way (and features cameos from real people who've been laid off).

Hottest supernatural creatures: Sexy vampires ruled at the box office with The Twilight Saga: New Moon and on TV with HBO's True Blood and CW's The Vampire Diaries. But let's hear it for werewolves (also in New Moon) and zombies (in the Zombieland flick and the Pride and Prejudice and Zombies book) for being almost as scarily popular.

Hottest controversy with an American Idol connection: Although Paula Abdul leaving the show was an early contender, Adam Lambert's racy performance at the American Music Awards turned out to be much, much more buzz-worthy.

Trends you couldn't ignore (as much as you tried): Bravo's Real Housewives franchise, people yelling at town hall meetings, weird celebrity beards (Joaquin Phoenix and Brad Pitt especially) and Megan Fox mania.

Best nickname: Octomom.

Worst nickname that we're actually starting to like: The Situation from MTV's Jersey Shore.

Most delightful meeting: After out-Madonna-ing Madonna with her daring high-concept costumes and groovy dance hits, Lady Gaga capped a successful year by being introduced to Queen Elizabeth.

Weirdest stunt that wasn't worth it: Sacha Baron Cohen got free publicity when his Bruno character put his derrière cheeks next to Eminem's facial ones at the MTV Movie Awards. But his Bruno film still disappointed at the box office.

Nicest conclusion to a fake romance: Pam and Jim getting hitched on The Office.

Meanest conclusion to a fake romance: After Jason Mesnick proposed to Melissa Rycroft on The Bachelor, he dumped her for the runner-up, Molly Malaney. But Rycroft had the last laugh -- she competed on Dancing With the Stars and just got married to another guy.

Best TV reunion: Larry David brought together the cast of Seinfeld on Curb Your Enthusiasm.

Best TV fresh start: The Sterling Cooper Draper Pryce agency that was launched during the stellar finale to the Mad Men season. If Don Draper is starting a new business, maybe there is hope for the job market.

a decade in photos -
By Margo Goodhand
334 words
26 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
H4
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

For Canadians, it was a decade of quiet yet profound change.

We endured the annual floods, fires and natural disasters with our usual fortitude and resilience. We shuddered over the human disasters -- the sexual predator on Vancouver's Lower Mainland, the lone gunmen in Montreal, Mayerthorpe and elsewhere.

We shot into space a robot (Dextre), our first woman (Julie Payette) and a clown (Cirque du Soleil founder Guy Laliberte), along with Dr. Robert Thirsk, who became the first Canadian to live on the International Space Station.

We saw the meteoric rise of self-described hockey nut Jim Balsillie of Ontario, whose Blackberries transformed the lives of millions of white-collar workers around the world. And with a great technological leap forward, the mass availability of Broadband wireless, we morphed seemingly overnight into a nation of bloggers, Twitterers, Facebookers and YouTubers.

In this decade, we lost Pierre Elliot Trudeau and watched the Liberal Party implode; and we saw the uniting of Canada's political right and the rise of Stephen Harper.

We survived SARS and H1N1 -- at least, so far.

But when it came to social issues, the true north strong and free made some profound decisions that will resonate for years.

We said yes to gay marriage, we said yes to legalized marijuana for medicinal purposes. We said we were sorry -- to victims of residential schools, to Chinese forced to pay a head tax to enter the country, to Indo-Canadians for denying some of them entry in 1914.

We saluted a Governor General who first came to this country as a Haitian refugee; we heralded the first female Chief Justice of the Supreme Court of Canada.

We said no to the Iraq war; we said yes to the UN Afghanistan mission, and we said goodbye -- time and heartbreaking time again -- to 134 soldiers killed there since 2002. A nation of peacekeepers, we remain divided on this issue. And look forward to the decade that lies ahead.

News
War named top news story; Afghan mission cited by 29 per cent in poll
277 words
26 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
A04
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

Liberals returning to power in Ottawa?

Political stability in Quebec?

A woman serving as a premier of a province?

These are all foreseen happening in the next decade by a large majority of respondents in a wide-ranging Angus Reid survey done for the Star and Montreal's La Presse.

The survey asked Canadians to rate the most important news event of the past decade, then asked Canadians, Americans and Britons to look ahead to the next decade.

The mission in Afghanistan topped the list of most newsworthy events in Canada this decade, cited by 29 per cent of respondents.

One in five picked two major health issues (the outbreak of SARS and the H1N1 flu), and 18 per cent cited the federal sponsorship scandal as the story of the decade. The Vancouver Olympics and the election of Stephen Harper ranked further down the list.

Looking ahead, Canadians cited global warming as the biggest world issue in the coming decade, as did Britons. Americans overwhelmingly cited the economy as the decade's greatest global challenge.

Almost half of Ontarians (48 per cent) said life in this province is worse than it was 10 years ago. Three-quarters say Ontario's position in Canada has worsened over the past decade. Thirty-six per cent of Ontarians believe their standard of living will be better in 10 years.

Angus Reid surveyed 1,017 randomly selected Canadian adults in mid-December. Its findings have a margin of error of plus or minus 3.1 per cent. Its Ontario sample was 806 adults.

It took a further month for the penny to drop, however, on another small matter: "We're not going to be able to rebuild Afghanistan into a Jeffersonian democracy."

No, again we're thinking not. Any more than Iraq has taken a step forward, going by Baghdad official Mazin al-Shihan's pre-Enlightenment plan to pay men to marry Iraqi war widows:

"If we give the money to the widows, they will spend it unwisely because they are uneducated and they don't know about budgeting," reasoned Mazin. "But if we find her a husband, there will be a person in charge of her and her children for the rest of their lives."

The incomparable Boris Johnson, mayor of London, shares hair of purest white with our own dear, about-to-be-ex city father, D. Miller, but has much loftier goals:

"At the moment I am like some underwater sea creature, a gigantic coelenterate, sucking everything in, and later on I am going to start expelling. It's going to take me some time to come up with a complete theory of urban civilization but I'm sure I will." So are we, not that it will be comprehensible, but never mind, we'll want to hear it.

This year, 34-year-old Wendy Brown of Wisconsin decided it would be a heckuva good idea to relive her childhood. So she pretended to be her own 15-year-old daughter, attended classes at a local high school and joined the cheerleading squad. When she didn't turn up one day, truant officers found Wendy locked up in the local jail on fraud charges, and proceeded to charge her with identity theft.

Reaction was mixed: "You just go, aw, all right, whatever," said articulate student Spencer Corpus.

"The entire staff of Ashwaubenon High should be drug tested for this completely inexcusable incident," huffed a letter to the editor of the local newspaper, which is probably in its last throes and now devoid of staff and hope and ... (er, sorry, that's another story).

Explaining to the New York Times why he gave up being a "public intellectual" to return home - or as the paper graciously put it, "Canada no less" - Liberal Leader Michael Ignatieff took a load off our collective mind with, "I've been a spectator, and now I'm in the boat fishing. That part of it, from a spiritual point of view, feels good."

A London friend recalled strolling with Ignatieff "arm in arm around Covent Garden singing, poorly, of course, some of the great quintet in the first act of Cosi Fan Tutte." As one does. Later in the year, the spiritual, operatic, yet icily ambitious Ignatieff warned PM Stephen Harper, "If you mess with me, I'll mess with you until I'm done." Put that in the Times.

Speaking of Jeffersonian democracy, Venezuela's president of 10 years, the ever-gutsy if mostly bonkers Hugo Chavez won a referendum in February that lets him stay in power till the end of time. While he sang the national anthem at the victory celebration, the crowd chanted, or perhaps howled, "Hey ho, Chavez won't go."

People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals requested a South Dakota high school change its name from Spearfish High to Sea Kitten High. Since you ask, PETA's theory was that if students were taught to refer to fish as "sea kittens" - "individuals who have friendships" - fewer fish would be murdered. They didn't do it.

On the royal front: Prince Chulls is clearly a philistine, a quality which would not be a handicap in his line of work were it not for the presumption that prompts him to believe he is an expert on subjects about which he is ignorant - ex-British MP Roy Hattersley on HRH's interference in an ultra-modern housing development for which the royal family of Qatar was going to pony up.

And on the quasi-royal front: "My love life's diabolical," announced Fergiana, Duchess of York. "I don't want to sound desperate, but I'd like someone to come and join me on Planet Sarah."

Well, there's Eric in Lincolnshire, Miyuki in Japan, or Miss Universe, Dayana Mendoza, who may already be on it, going by her blog after visiting Guantanamo: "I didn't want to leave, it was such a relaxing place, so calm and beautiful."

Not to forget John Lydon, a.k.a. Johnny Rotten of the Sex Pistols. "For me, the best rock is not what you play," he noted, "it's what you're not playing." Which in his case is true.

Or Kanye West. After he barged on stage to interrupt wisplike singer Taylor Swift at the MTV video awards, Barack Obama joined the planet's sentient majority in calling him a "jackass."

Speaking of jackasses, Whoopi Goldberg didn't think it fair for Switzerland to arrest film director Roman Polanski for the drugging and rape of a 13-year-old girl 32 long years ago.

"It wasn't rape-rape," she idiotically observed.

In April, the unemployed, unwed Nadya Suleman, who'd given birth to octuplets three months earlier, trademarked the word "Octomum" in a bid to promote her career as a, uh, who knows? "We've had phone calls," said her lawyer, "but it's a stretch to call them offers."

Nothing is a stretch for Alberta. After a tourism video for the landlocked province showed a more-or-less azure sea and gold beach, premier's aide Tom Olsen explained there'd been "no attempt to mislead here. The picture used just fitted the mood and tone of what we were trying to do." Which was, let's face it, Tom, to mislead.

And where was the actual beach? Barbados, the Maldives, Pago Pago? Uh-uh. The village of Beadnell in Northumberland, England, population 528, one of whom, wouldn't you know, spotted the bizarre pretense and ratted.

Libya's increasingly unhinged Moammar Gadhafi droned on for 100 excruciating minutes at the UN General Assembly this fall. With a face like a wet weekend, Gadhafi ranged through the failings of the UN to who was behind JFK's assassination (Israel, natch) to his theory that swine flu was created for military purposes to the imminent arrival of "fish flu." He then promoted his website.

As his aides later explained, jet lag is a terrible thing. And so it is.

But so was 2009.

Time now to bow our heads, Harry, and thank Google this one is done and dusted.

Will we miss the hurly-burly of it all? Like a hole in a parachute.

Insight
And the 'winners' are ... Recognzing this year's high achievers in fields as diverse as piracy and adultery
620 words
26 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
IN02
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

Most Endearing Underdog: Levi Johnston. She was a candidate for vice-president of the United States. He was a 19-year-old "redneck" whose claim to fame was the impregnation of her daughter. Yet Johnston managed to hold his own in a ceaselessly entertaining war of words with Sarah Palin - in between lucrative topless photo shoots.

Best Local Idea Gone Terribly Wrong: Ethnic food carts. Exorbitant start-up costs and bad locations made vendors wonder why they became vendors. Bad food made customers wonder why they didn't just buy hot dogs.

Most Successful Adulterer: Silvio Berlusconi. Tiger Woods has a bunch of affairs and has to leave his job: golfer. Berlusconi has a bunch of affairs and gets to keep his job: prime minister of Italy. Forget therapy and repentance - Tiger needs to take a study abroad trip to Rome.

Quickest Thinking by an Airplane Pilot: Chesley Sullenberger. After his Airbus A320 hit a flock of bids, U.S. Airways Captain Sullenberger saved 155 people by effortlessly landing the plane in New York's Hudson River. That he is known as "Sully" made the story especially delightful.

Most Praiseworthy Acts of Defiance: Iran's Greens. They could have meekly accepted Mahmoud Ahmadinejad's fraudulent election victory; they could have responded with violence. Instead, tens of thousands of green-clad supporters of Mir Hossein Mousavi took to the streets in peaceful protest. We should all have their courage and restraint.

Most Entertaining Unsuccessful Attempt to Acquire a Phoenix Hockey Team and Move it to Hamilton: Jim Balsillie. The Research in Motion billionaire lost his bitter battle with NHL commissioner Gary Bettman. Nonetheless, his outspokenness and tenacity turned an interminable and confusing saga into riveting theatre.

Worst Family Planning: Octomom. Though Nadya Suleman already had six children, she convinced a fertility doctor to implant six embryos. After two of them split, she gave birth to octuplets. Even her mother thought she was crazy.

Most Boneheaded Mistake: Saskatchewan Roughriders. The Roughriders had already begun celebrating their Grey Cup victory when they found out they had sent one too many players onto the field for the game's final play. Given another chance, Montreal made a field goal for the win. Ouch.

Best Reminding the World that Pirates Still Exist: Somali pirates. Who knew?

Most Deserved Deportation: Karlheinz Schreiber. In the words of one newspaper, the arms dealer/lobbyist/distributor of cash in envelopes to Brian Mulroney had "exhausted his legal options." He had also exhausted us. Good riddance, sir.

Most Deserved Failure By a Not-Funny Man: Jay Leno. He was always bad, but he was always successful. In his new 10 p.m. time slot, he was dreadful, and so, finally, were his ratings.

Least Satisfying Tale of Redemption: Alex Rodriguez. Long derided as a choker and a prima donna, the best-paid baseball player ever hit rock bottom with his February admission of steroid use. He then led his New York Yankees to a World Series title. It was hard to be happy for him.

Worst Preparation: North American public health officials. The H1N1 vaccine rollout was a mess in Canada, a disaster in the U.S. Governments are lucky the pandemic was so mild.

Most Needless Cosmetic Change by a News Program: The National. On the advice, apparently, of an American consulting firm, the CBC directed its newspeople to stand, CNN style, rather than sit at desks. Peter Mansbridge doesn't seem to like it; viewers certainly don't either.
