LUCK

great escapes

The greatest escape

Eight years after the terror attacks of Sept. 11, 2001, killed 3,000 and sparked the war in Afghanistan, the man blamed for those deaths remains elusive, despite a massive manhunt. Occasionally rumoured to be dead and sometimes dismissed as irrelevant, Osama bin Laden sends a steady stream of messages that make a mockery of the $50-million price on his head.

“Emergency over water”

In what was dubbed the “Miracle on the Hudson,” Captain Chesley Sullenberger successfully ditched his U.S. Airways flight on the New York river last January after both engines were knocked out by a flock of geese. All 155 people aboard survived.

Mad Max

Max Mosley, son of an infamous British fascist, faced outrage and calls to resign as president of Formula One racing's governing body after a tabloid reported last year that he took part in a Nazi-themed orgy. Irate, he sued and a judge found no evidence of Nazi intent and awarded him £60,000. Fade-away jumper

Kobe Bryant was a rising star with the L.A. Lakers in 2003 when he was accused of rape by a 19-year-old hotel employee. He was dropped by several sponsors (Nike didn't even feature him when marketing his own shoe) but the charges were withdrawn, a civil settlement was reached and Mr. Bryant earned $45-million last year, according to Forbes.

Dog's best friend

People say they would do anything for a pet, but Ed Dyson means it. Last winter, the 72-year-old Labrador resident took a shovel to a polar bear mauling his miniature dog, Foot. Then the bear looked at him “very serious,” and Mr. Dyson knew he needed more. The bear fled after taking a round from his .22 rifle.

SCANDALS

hey, we tried

CANADA

l'affair Julie Bikers, lost documents and décolletage – this was our one taste of the salacious. Julie Couillard was a mystery woman in a low-cut dress when she turned heads at Rideau Hall in 2007, arriving on the arm of Maxime Bernier for his swearing-in as foreign affairs minister. Then the media learned of her Hells Angels connections, and he resigned after leaving confidential papers at her apartment.

SPONSORSHIP skulduggery The story of the fake invoices used to line the pockets of Quebec businessmen and Liberal organizers was infuriating, of course, but not exactly juicy.

mulroney's cash Brian Mulroney denied taking cash payments from lobbyist Karlheinz Schreiber until he finally told a public inquiry that he not only took the money but waited six years to report it to Revenue Canada. The fallout: The ex-PM is banned from contact with the government and his accuser has been shipped back to face jail time in Germany. THe rest of the world

AMERICA'S CHEATIN' HEARTS

Men in U.S. high office clearly didn't learn from Bill Clinton: New York Governor Eliot Spitzer resigned in 2008 for using a VIP prostitution service. Senator John Edwards admitted to an affair during his run for the 2008 Democratic presidential nomination. Republican scandals featured social conservatives caught in gay trysts. Senator Larry Craig pled guilty to disorderly conduct in 2007 after propositioning an undercover officer in a men's airport washroom. Ted Haggard, the president of the National Association of Evangelicals, resigned in 2006 after admitting he solicited a male prostitute for sex and drugs.

ITALY'S SEXY SENIOR

Silvio Berlusconi is 73 but calls himself a “playboy” prime minister and courts controversy wherever he goes. His personal finances led to fraud charges and this year his philandering finally broke up his marriage. The decade has not ended well for Mr. Berlusconi, who was seriously injured and bloodied recently by a protester.

FRANCE'S NAKED AMBITION

Nicholas Sarkozy was elected French President in 2007 on a platform of controversial reform, but his flashy personal life overshadows his politics. Shortly after divorcing for the second time, he appeared shirtless on an Egyptian beach with his new girlfriend, singer and former model Carla Bruni. Within months they had married – even as she was appearing au naturel in GQ.

SCARES

false alarms

The Millennium bug

Did you stock up on canned goods? Gasoline? “I'm advising people to have three months of cash on hand,” one computer consultant tells The Globe. But the bug proves to be a bust.

The death of mail

As e-mail works its way into Canadian life, some see the end coming for Canada Post. Wrong. Last year, posties handled 11.6 billion pieces of mail – two billion more than 10 years earlier. Coalition rule

The opposition unites to topple Stephen Harper's government and replace him with Stéphane Dion. To buy time, the Prime Minister asks the Governor-General to “prorogue” Parliament. The term is new to many people, but the tactic works beautifully: The coalition disappears, followed soon by Mr. Dion.

Fidel's farewell

As the Cuban revolutionary's health deteriorates, the Western world is on alert. In 2007, celebrity blogger and Cuban-American Perez Hilton reports that Miami police are about to announce his death, and celebrations break out in the streets. Oops. Too soon.

Cheap oil

In early 2000, Alberta worries that its oil is too expensive. When the price hits $30 a barrel, the Canadian Energy Research Institute says it will be “relatively short-lived.” And it is – the figure goes as high as $147 before fading.

The global pandemic

Pick your poison. This decade has seen the West Nile Virus, avian flu and now H1N1, but no “global health disaster” – not yet, anyway.

Balloon Boy

But for one misstep, young Falcon Heene might still be in our hearts. First, he supposedly sails away in his father's test balloon. Then, he goes on Larry King Live and says “we did it for the show.” Junior stunt man.

OUR IMAGE

what makes us cool

Research in Motion, 2002

Okay, the BlackBerry began as a pager but the smart-phone model is what vaults RIM to tech stardom, selling more than 50 million CrackBerrys, racking up lawsuits and tormenting an NHL commissioner in the process.

Same-sex marriage, 2003

The Supreme Court makes Canada the third country in the world to recognize same-sex unions, simultaneously creating a boom in wedding tourism.

The Governor-General, 2005

She eats seal. She prorogues Parliament. She sports military regalia. Whatever you think of our monarch's rep, she's anything but boring.

Indie flicks, 2007 (Juno)

If Canadians do one thing well, it's endearing awkwardness, especially on film. Take Brampton's Michael Cera, Montreal-born Jason Reitman and Haligonian Ellen Page, add sweatbands and

indie tunes. Wait for acclaim.

The Economist says so

Seriously: In September, 2003, the self-possessed Brit magazine put a moose with sunglasses on its cover and trumpeted “Canada's New Spirit.” Stodgy? On the contrary, it opined, “a cautious case can be made that Canada is now rather cool.”

HARPER

top dog

If this decade belongs to anyone in Canadian politics, it is Stephen Harper, who returned from the wilderness to lead the dispirited remnants of conservatism to victory. It has been quite a ride.

Autumn, 2000: The new Canadian Alliance, led by Stockwell Day, fares miserably in a snap election. The Progressive Conservatives do even worse, and ex-Reform MP Stephen Harper retreats to a foxhole in Alberta. Conservatism is at its nadir.

Spring, 2002: Belay that. Conservatives have farther to fall. Alliance MPs rebel against Mr. Day He calls a leadership vote, and is whupped by Mr. Harper.

Autumn, 2003: Peter MacKay now leads the bankrupt PCs. Mr. Harper comes a-callin'. By the year's end, the Tories are united, and Paul Martin has finally forced out Jean Chrétien.

Spring, 2004: In trouble because of the sponsorship scandal in Quebec, Mr. Martin calls an inquiry and a vote he barely wins.

December, 2005: After much dithering, Mr. Martin calls another election, and loses.

Feb. 6, 2006: Mr. Harper is sworn in. He has a minority government, but the Liberals spend a year replacing Mr. Martin.

October, 2008: Voters give him another minority, but Mr. Harper gambles and cuts public funding to political parties, which would destroy the opposition.

December, 2008: Instead, the opposition almost destroys him with a coalition. The PM survives, barely, by suspending Parliament.

December, 2009: He fights the recession like a Keynesian and fights a non-existent crime wave by jailing people. He can afford to; Michael Ignatieff's political instincts are worse than Mr. Dion's. No one is taking this government down any time soon.

9/11

how it changed the world

1 "AFPAK"

Seemingly vanquished the Taliban and al-Qaeda reconstituted in Pakistan's frontier lands. Their foes wonder if the war can ever be won when Taliban fighters cross borders freely while Western soldiers cannot.

2 THE COST OF IRAQ

Saddam Hussein was caugh, but with so much capital spent in Iraq, U.S. might and moral authority may never recover. Meanwhile, bombs continue to explode in Baghdad.

3 AIRPORT SECURITY

Every plane is still seen as a potential guided missile. Every passenger remains a danger - at least until through security. And secret "no-fly" lists show no signs of abating.

4 HOMEGROWNS

Who would have known hip-hop-quoting teenagers from the suburbs secretly harboured hopes of exploding truck bombs downtown? The case of the "Toronto 18" speakd to a global phenomenon: Terrorism can as grow at home as well.

5 THE "DARK SIDE"

The Bush Administrations's revelations left little to the imagination. Can Barrack Obama help the United States reclaim its moral clout and recover the rule of law?

6 GWOT

President Bush's "global war on terrorism" cuts both ways. More and more international terrorists are enlisting to fight "Jews and Crusaders" everywhere.

DEMOGRAPHICS

Canada, you've changed

AGING The average life expectancy for Canadians has increased, cracking the 80 barrier in 2005 – 78 for men and 82.7 for women, and the gap is narrowing.

The median age, the point at which half the population is older and half younger, rose to 39.1 years in 2008, up from 37.2 in 2001. In 1972 it was 26.3.

diversity Since 2000 the population has grown about 1 per cent a year to 33.5 million, mostly because Canada has one of the highest immigration rates among wealthy nations.

From 2001 to 2006, the visible-minority population grew five times more rapidly than the population as a whole – 27.2 per cent compared with 5.4 per cent. Visible minorities now make up 16 per cent of Canadians, up from 13 per cent in 2001 (47 per cent in Toronto, 42 per cent in Vancouver).

Almost 60 per cent of new immigrants who arrived during the decade came from Asian nations, a trend that shows no sign of slowing and China is about to be surpassed by India as the top source country for Canadian immigrants.

divorce

In 2004 divorces fell below 70,000 for the first time since 1998 – a far cry from their 1987 peak of 96,200.

In 2004 the divorce rate was 21.8 per 10,000 people, compared with 36.4 in 1987.

pregnancy This was the decade when, for the first time, women in their 30s became the dominant age group of new mothers. Women between the ages of 30 and 34 led the way in 2005, just ahead of those between 25 and 29, part of a trend to starting families later in life. Just under half of those who gave birth that year, 48.9 per cent, were over 30.

the west booms Ontario's share of new immigrants plummeted from 58.7 per cent in 2000 to 47 per cent in 2007.

In 2000, Ontario gained 23,292 people from other provinces. By 2007 it had a net loss of 17,762.

Saskatchewan, whose population had been in decline, turned the trend around in 2007, adding 10,174 people,.

Alberta drew citizens from across Canada throughout the decade, with the biggest influx a whopping 58,166 recorded at the boom's peak in 2006.

***

CHINA

rising star

Economic engine

A decade ago, the People's Republic had the sixth-largest economy. Billions of “made in China” labels later, it has joined the World Trade Organization (2001), more than tripled its gross domestic product and almost surpassed Japan as economy No. 2.

Deal maker In 2001, after the United States was attacked, no country could stop it from lashing out in Afghanistan and Iraq. That was then. While America made war, and spent trillions it didn't have, Beijing accumulated U.S. treasury bills and expanded its influence to end the unipolar world.

Political model

Ten years ago, opening the economy and the advent of the Internet were seen as the end for authoritarian regimes. Things have changed. Instead of crumbling, the Communist Party has charted a course – largely free markets under one-party rule – that others (notably Vladimir Putin in Russia) are studying.

Web wrangler

The Internet was supposed to challenge the regime, but China now has the world's largest online community (more than 300 million users), and the government makes sure the conversation never includes: “Hey, are these really the leaders we want?”

Climate chameleon

Two years ago, China passed the United States to become the world's largest carbon-dioxide emitter, and the country's leadership has refused to commit to firm emissions caps. But there's a green lining to its black clouds: China is becoming a world leader in solar and wind power.

Language instructor

It's probably time to start learning Mandarin, or at least suggest it instead of Spanish for your kids. The trend has already started. The government claims that 40 million foreigners are taking lessons. Ni hao ma?

***

DISTINCT

only in quebec

Brother Bonhomme In a move befitting Canada's most labour-friendly province, Bonhomme Carnaval unionizes.

Fromage perdu Entrepreneur Luc Boivin drops a ton of cheddar deep into the Saguenay River, hoping the pressure and the chill will give Quebeckers a new gourmet treat. If only divers could find it again.

Short stop In 2005, the Parti Québécois makes André Boisclair the first openly gay leader of a major Canadian party. Two years later, it pushes him out.

OLD Hab-it RDS launches Quebec's third nightly talk show dedicated almost entirely to the Montreal Canadiens. French-language TV now has more Habs chatter than local news.

Seal of approval Two Montreal chefs respond to anti-sealing activists by putting the object of their affection on the menu. Their reward: brisk business and death threats.

Survivor Despite alleged corruption, Gérald Tremblay defeats a 9/11 conspiracy theorist and an ex-cabinet minister who is afraid to speak English, for a third term as mayor.

JUSTICE

pivotal decisions

CANADA

Halpern v. the Attorney-General of Canada (2003): Ontario's top court crafts a legal and cultural milestone that creates an immediate right to same-sex marriage.

R v. Pickton (2007): Convicted of six murders. Robert William Pickton is suspected of killing another 20 women and burying them on his pig farm. He faces 25 years before becoming eligible for parole.

Charkaoui (2007): The Supreme Court of Canada rules that Adil Charkaoui, an alleged al-Qaeda sleeper agent, had his Charter rights violated under a procedure used to deport suspected security risks. The ruling forces the federal government to redraft its legislation on security certificates.

Chaoulli v. Quebec (2005): The Supreme Court sparks debate by opening the door to privatized health care, striking down Quebec's ban on buying private insurance for medical services

THE WORLD

Bush v Gore (2000): The U.S. Supreme Court decides the presidential election by ending a Florida recount in faviour of George W. Bush.

Belmarsh detainees case, 2004: The House of Lords weakens British counter-terrorism policy by ruling against the indefinite detention of suspects.

Hamdan v Rumsfeld (2006): The U.S. Supreme Court holds that military commissions set up to try detainees being held without charge at Guantanamo Bay are illegal.

Slobodan Milosevic (2002): The first head of state indicted by a UN war-crimes tribunal is accused of heinous crimes in former Yugoslavia. He represents himself in an erratic, hectoring style. but the trial ends abruptly when he dies in his cell from heart trouble.

U.S. v Conrad Black (2007): The media mogul goes to jail for obstructing justice and mail fraud.

***

DISASTERS

nature gets ugly

Oxfam reports that by 2015, extreme weather will affect an estimated 375 million people, versus the annual average of 250 million recorded during a decade that has seen an unprecedented series of natural disasters.

Euro heat wave (2003)

An intense heat wave grips Western Europe, killing an estimated 35,000 people.

Iran earthquake (2003)

A 6.7 magnitude earthquake hits Bam in eastern Iran, and the death toll tops 30,000.

Indian Ocean tsunami (2004)

Sparked by an earthquake off Sumatra, the tsunami sweeps the Indian Ocean coastline, killing more than 230,000 people, mostly in Indonesia, Sri Lanka, India and Thailand.

Hurricane Katrina (2005)

Bringing havoc to the Gulf Coast of the United States, Katrina ravages New Orleans, kills almost 2,000 people and causes more than $80-billion in damage to become one of the more expensive disasters in U.S. history.

Kashmir earthquake (2005)

A 7.6 magnitude earthquake hits Pakistan-controlled Kashmir, killing close to 80,000 in Pakistan, India and Afghanistan.

Cyclone Nargis (2008)

Devastated by Nargis, Myanmar's Irrawaddy Delta loses almost 140,000 people with another 2.5 million left homeless.

Sichuan earthquake (2008)

Almost 70,000 are killed and 374,000 injured in a 7.9-magnitude earthquake that devastates central China.

***

SCIENCE

hits and misses

discoveries

• The genome is mapped, water is found on Mars and planets are photographed circling other stars. • In the Arctic, paleontologists find a “missing link” fossil with leggy fins, showing how fish first clambered onto land. In Ethiopia, they put together Ardi, adding a face to humanity's family tree. • Doctors perform the first face transplant and find that circumcision slows down HIV-AIDS. • Stem-cell scientists reprogram adult skin cells into stem cells. • Neuroscientists learn how thinking changes the brain; molecular biologists find genes can be switched on and off by environmental factors, such as parenting.

disasters

• A Korean researcher is shamed after faking that he'd cloned human embryos. Dolly the cloned sheep dies young. • Hormone-replacement therapy increases the risk of breast cancer, and $2.5-billion-a-year-painkiller Vioxx goes bust after it causes heart problems.

• The “Mozart Effect” is debunked: Classical music does not enhance intelligence in babies, nor do edu-videos. Walt Disney offers refunds on Baby Einstein.

• The space shuttle Columbia disintegrates as it returns to Earth, killing seven crew members and grounding the NASA fleet. • Pluto loses its status as a planet.

obamanation [O-BAM-a-na-tion]

-noun

1. The ostensibly horrible state of affairs that resulted after the election of Barack Obama as U.S. President in 2008. Usage

“It's an Obamanation, what that new President is doing.”

-noun

Usage

“I'm not watching The Jon Stewart Show any more because it reeks of politainment.”

- noun

“He insisted that, while he feared Islamic terrorists, he was not guilty of Islamophobia.”

man date [MAN-date]

noun

1. Two straight men out for what might be a date – dinner and a film, for example.

Usage “Mr. Johnson and his friend, Mr. Jones, were both reluctant to call the their dinner a man-date, but both knew that it was.”

-verb

1. The act of sending sexually explicit messages or photos electronically, primarily between cellphones.

Usage

“Sure, he conceded he had been sexting his mistress, but not to the same degree as Tiger Woods.”

*

recessionista [Re-cess-ion-EEST-a]

-noun

1. A person, typically a woman, who manages to dress with flair while adhering to a recession-reduced budget.

Usage

“You know, for someone with no job, you're quite the recessionista.”

ANALYSIS
Globe Toronto
David Miller's summer of lovelessness; The garbage strike was Toronto's defining political struggle in 2009, and though the mayor eventually got what he wanted from the city's unions, writes John Lorinc, he lost for winning.
JOHN LORINC
SPECIAL TO THE GLOBE AND MAIL
2217 words
26 December 2009
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
M4
English
2009 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

When David Miller reflects on 2009, he must wish he could erase 39 days in June and July.

The Great Strike of '09 overshadowed everything else that happened at City Hall this year, demolishing the mayor's approval ratings and cementing his decision not to seek a third term.

In the public's eye, Mr. Miller was undone by, of all things, a decade's-old sick bank benefit. The obscure perk became a metaphor for unaffordable public-sector benefits in a time of economic crisis. Torontonians were livid when it appeared Mr. Miller had caved on his vow to eliminate the benefit, settling instead for phasing it out over time.

Yet what most Torontonians don't realize is that months before the strike, Mr. Miller and the city's negotiators had aimed to phase out, not scrap, the sick bank. In the end, the mayor got exactly what he wanted on the issue – on top of an arguably good wage deal that will cut the city's 2010-2012 payroll by $42.4-million compared to the previous contract.

So how did David Miller lose for winning?

His summer from hell is a story of love lost with the unions that swept him to power, of the steep political price of containing costs, and of one big gamble that paid off at the bargaining table but cost him dearly at the kitchen table.

It is also a cautionary tale for the people who want his job.

Prepping for hard bargain

In the summer of 2008, with the global economy on a precipice, Bruce Anderson sat at the helm of the City of Toronto's human-resources division. He joined the city in 2007 after six years with Simon Fraser University.

As lead negotiator, he was working on a plan to modernize the city's aging civil service. His officials also knew they faced a financial crisis heading into talks with 23,000 employees whose contracts expired Dec. 31, 2008. The city's net liabilities had ballooned to $2.84-billion, driven by infrastructure debt and employee benefits.

The new human-resources boss impressed his masters with his research skills. But Mr. Anderson (who declined to be interviewed) was also versed in the art of fending off elected officials. As one put it, “He's one of those guys who can look at you blankly and talk in dense paragraphs that leave you with a headache.”

In September, a week after Lehman Brothers collapsed, Mr. Miller's executive committee met to consider the city's books. In an otherwise grim assessment, there was a glimmer of good news. Since 2006, the sick-bank liability had fallen 4.7 per cent due to council's decision to discontinue the benefit for non-unionized employees. If the city could do the same for its much larger unionized work force, it could save much more.

Originally conceived as an incentive to fight absenteeism in a bygone era, the sick bank allowed workers to save 18 sick days a year and cash them out for as much as six-months pay when they retired.

Between September, 2008, and April, 2009, Mr. Anderson obtained secret approval from council's employee and labour-relations committee to press ahead with a strategy to eliminate the sick bank during negotiations with Canadian Unions of Public Employees 416 and 79, representing Toronto's outside and inside workers.

His goal was to replace the sick bank with the same short-term disability-leave plan granted to the city's non-unionized employees. Under that scheme, common in the private sector, ill or temporarily disabled workers receive most or all of their pay while they're laid up.

To get there, council's employee and labour-relations committee, chaired by the mayor, had to sign off on bargaining “goals” for Mr. Anderson's negotiating team.

The committee gave Mr. Anderson a mandate to cut a deal to “grandfather” the sick bank, meaning it would no longer be available to new hires, although existing employees could continue accumulating days.

Some months later, the committee allowed Mr. Anderson the option of offering employees a buyout package if they relinquished future sick-bank benefits.

Committee member Doug Holyday, a right-of-centre councillor, had long promoted the buyout approach from his days as mayor of Etobicoke. Mr. Miller consulted Mr. Holyday to ensure he'd help sell the plan to other councillors.

By May, Mr. Anderson had a mandate to negotiate the phase-out. But he wanted to drive a hard bargain before making any compromises.

Unions refuse to budge

In early June of this year, the 416 bargaining team – representing 6,200 outside workers – set up a war room in the Canadiana Suite of the Delta Toronto East hotel in Scarborough. The windowless space was equipped with a fridge, a microwave, a bank of printers and Internet access. A table by one wall was heaped with coffee, candy and chips.

Local 79, representing 16,000 inside workers and led by veteran president Ann Dembinski, established its own headquarters in a similar hall at the Sheraton Centre. (She declined an interview request.) For months, the contract talks had been stalled as CUPE negotiators balked at the 118 pages of concessions Mr. Anderson presented as the city's opening position, a wish list that included the elimination of the sick bank.

Back in February, Mr. Miller had met 416's new president, Mark Ferguson, for a chat. A calm, self-possessed paramedic, Mr. Ferguson had been a member of the 416 executive since 2002, when the union was forging a relationship with Mr. Miller as he prepared to run for mayor.

Mr. Miller stressed the city's money problems and the recession, warning Mr. Ferguson he didn't want a settlement imposed by Queen's Park, as happened in 2002. Arbitrated deals tend to favour unions because arbitrators are not obligated to consider the employer's financial circumstances.

“I had an established track record of being supportive of our workers and [felt] we should be able to negotiate a rational collective agreement,” Mr. Miller says. In June, the mayor and city officials began to talk publicly about eliminating the sick bank. Many voters, hurt by the recession, were shocked to learn about what they saw as an unaffordable benefit.

What Mr. Miller and his officials didn't reveal, for tactical reasons, was that the city was prepared to grandfather the sick bank and compensate the employees.

As the June 22 strike deadline approached, each side waited for the other to yield. In 2005, Mr. Miller had averted a strike at the 11th hour with a promise to allow CUPE to deliver some outsourced services.

This time, he chose to stay out of the talks.

On deadline day, CUPE 416 negotiators waited for hours at the Delta East for the city's offer to arrive. Mr. Anderson, however, opted not to offer up that package of pre-approved sick-bank compromises.

The city, in short, rolled the dice. But CUPE wasn't making empty threats. Soon after midnight, Mr. Ferguson delivered the union response to Mr. Anderson: “You've just bought yourself a strike.”

Miller's big gamble

As soon as the strike started, city officials rolled out a carefully planned strategy for handling residential garbage.

Still, residents fumed about reeking local dumps and long lines at transfer stations. Thousands of children had been shut out of recreation programs, and parents scrambled to find daycare.

An Angus Reid poll showed most Torontonians wanted the city to scrap the sick bank and ask the province for back-to-work legislation. The poll showed residents were almost equally upset with both the mayor and the unions.

Mr. Miller seemed worn down. At a gathering at Councillor Kyle Rae's condo after the Gay Pride parade, he looked haggard, one guest recalls, “You could just see it on his face – exhaustion and frustration. This was killing him.”

Progressive councillors, some with close ties to CUPE, grew antsy. Twice, the left-leaning group gathered for private strategy sessions at Councillor Joe Mihevc's St. Clair West home. “A common thread we were all hearing is, ‘Anderson's the problem, you've got to bring in someone else to close,' ” recounts one participant. “This [was] so much the fog of war.”

Two weeks in, the mayor's office began talking to Mr. Anderson about changing the strike's dynamic. They decided to ante up one of those secret compromises – the buyout option – as part of a new formal offer. But there would be a tactical twist: They'd go over the heads of the CUPE negotiators by going public, posting the offer on the city website, along with a calculator to help employees estimate their payout.

The offer included hard numbers: a four-year contract with a total wage hike of 7.2 per cent; a short-term disability plan guaranteeing sick workers 75 to 100 per cent of their pay for up to six months; and an offer to partially buy out employees' accumulated sick banks, with an average payout of $8,500 for those with more than 10 years.

But it included no mention of the approved middle way: grandfathering the sick bank.

In the Canadiana room, CUPE negotiators tried to figure out their next move. By then, the summer's infestation of fruit flies had reached the snack table along the wall. Calls and e-mails flew back and forth to Ms. Dembinski's team. Mr. Ferguson felt the gambit had only entrenched the membership. Both locals publicly denounced the move. But with thousands of hits flooding the city's website, Mr. Miller and Mr. Anderson figured the bait had worked.

Yet to residents unfamiliar with the complex nature of contract talks, the offer began to smell like weakening resolve. “Some of this may well have been the choreography of collective negotiations,” notes Ryerson University political scientist Myer Siemiatycki. “But it did box Miller in.”

‘Only Nixon could go to China'

As July wound down, Mr. Miller knew that a looming H1N1 outbreak could trigger back-to-work legislation for striking public-health nurses. With a provincial arbitrator pressing both sides, Mr. Anderson's team dropped their concession demands, paving the way for bargaining over the sick bank and wages.

Mr. Ferguson's team responded with a counter-offer: grandfathering the sick bank. But CUPE informed Mr. Anderson that his group had until midnight on July 26 to ink a deal or face a moratorium on further talks.

The grandfathering proposal was the gesture Mr. Anderson had been waiting for. Without knowing the city's secret mandates, CUPE's position finally coincided with the goal negotiators established months earlier. Late into the night of July 27, the two sides struck a deal, with Mr. Miller learning the details from his High Park home. The strike was over.

Over the following days, pundits, conservative councillors and irate residents – all unaware of the city's internal bargaining strategy – attacked Mr. Miller for caving.

Mr. Anderson sketched out the game plan during the council ratification vote on July 30. CUPE negotiators were flabbergasted by his revelations. But few residents noticed: Their criticisms had hardened into received wisdom.

For many on a cash-strapped council, the story looked quite different. Councillor Pam McConnell, a member of the employee and labour relations committee, received congratulatory calls from other Ontario mayors. The conservative Mr. Holyday also felt the city got a good deal. The reduced sick-bank liability – a $140-million reduction – put the city on firmer ground.

Mr. Ferguson also says the union got a reasonable agreement, but he's still seething about the tortured process to get there. “If that was the mandate, why didn't they come to the table and bargain the mandate they had?” he asks.

Mr. Anderson is not telling. One point, however, is clear; and even Mark Ferguson acknowledges it – In collective bargaining, you never open with your bottom line.

When David Miller embarked on these negotiations, he wanted to modernize the city's relationship with its unions to insulate the civil service from privatization.

He still maintains it was the right thing to do. “I don't accept the criticism of my handling of the strike,” Mr. Miller says. “We didn't attack the unions, and we achieved our bargaining goals.”

With a 6-per-cent total wage hike over three years, the city's contract is certainly frugal compared to other public service contracts. “Only Nixon could go to China,” quips an insider, who describes Miller as “an inconvenient enemy.”

Still, the strike polarized the city's relationship with its unions, tarnished Mr. Miller's otherwise strong record, and set the stage for an election that may feature a raucous debate on outsourcing garbage collection.

For a progressive who launched his own mayoral campaign on the eve of a shorter but much more chaotic garbage strike, the irony must be difficult to ignore.

Travel
Non-stop flights sometimes hard to find -
Ask Journeys / Ron Pradinuk
857 words
26 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
E2
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

11A poet I may not be, but with the big day past, it only takes a few words to recognize what people are doing this morning. The traffic says it all. Manitobans are more interested in getting to their nearest shopping centre for bargains, than booking the fastest route out of town.

But tomorrow we begin to get back to normal.

For those of us who are not leaving this week, the questions are still about all-inclusive holidays and sun destinations.

QUESTION: Years ago, tour companies would regularly drop off vacationers to Manzanillo and Ixtapa as they passed over these cities en route to Acapulco. Now even though these same planes pass over all three of these cities en route to Huatulco, they refuse to land to let passengers off. Why is that?

ANSWER: We definitely could use more sun destination options, but there are two sides to this form of solution for new destination options.

Manitobans don't take well to the roundabout flights that are not completely non-stop.

For quite some time tour operators have used an extra stop strategy for many of the flights to the Dominican Republic. They go to both Punta Cana and Puerto Plata on one flight pattern. By going with the same circuitous route, passengers only have to endure one stop, either on the way out or on the way home.

Many do not like making this delay, even though it is relatively short, preferring to book the direct flights only. Frustration often occurs if the entire plane is asked to get off the aircraft and wait in a small holding area.

The challenge is that Manitoba does not seem able to fill planes on a weekly basis to smaller destinations, so it becomes a major obstacle for the tour operators.

However, all being said, it is time at least one more of our biggest tour companies take the leadership to create more destination options, and then give it the time to build a solid booking pattern.

QUESTION: You took your recent golf trip to Florida in November when it was still very warm. Is January too cool to plan a golf holiday to the Miami area?

ANSWER: I have spent time in Florida in virtually every month of the winter season. I did have to buy heavier wear while visiting Orlando one January a number of years ago.

Every season can bring a surprise, especially to the orange industry which has faced frost more than once over the last three decades.

However, I found the weather to really be quite excellent for the most part in January, particularly as you move south down to Miami. For the golfer especially, if the temperatures only reach 19-21 degrees Celsius, it's still very comfortable golf weather. The records for temperatures in Miami indicate the daily averages for January are 19.5 C with the average highs being 23.5C.

As reported, we stayed in two resorts in the immediate Miami area. The Fairmont Turnberry Isle was one of the more spectacular properties I have found in a while, with plenty to do for the entire family on site and nearby.

The vision of Phil Mickelson hoisting the cup after winning last year's World Championship, as we stood on the 18th green of Doral's famous Blue Monster course were both memory makers.

Certainly any risk you might face from weather can be avoided a couple of weeks later but I would not personally think twice about a January Miami vacation.

The service is also excellent. The area receives thousands of Canadian visitors each winter. They appreciate our business but they also are willing to express a fondness for most Canadians.

QUESTION: Do you need to get the H1N1 vaccine before you are able to get into Mexico? I heard that they ask you at the airport in Mexico if you have your documentation for the H1N1 vaccine, if not, they don't allow you in? Is that accurate?

ANSWER: At this point there is no country other than Saudi Arabia that is demanding that you report whether or not you have actually had the H1N1 vaccination. wNeither do I anticipate that that is likely to happen this winter.

Canada likely has a greater percentage of its population which has taken the vaccination than most countries, including most of the regions we are likely to contemplate visiting this season.

There are some countries, as has been pointed out in previous columns, who have instituted body heat screening mechanisms to help identify those wishing to pass through customs even though they are ill.

Nevertheless, if you have had the vaccination, there is no harm in ensuring you have the card with you that you were given upon vaccination.

Forward your travel questions to askjourneys@journeystravel.com. Ron Pradinuk is President of Journeys Travel & Leisure SuperCentre and can be heard Sundays at noon on CJOB. Previous columns and tips can be found on www.journeystravel.com[http://www.journeystravel.com] or read Ron's travel blog at www.thattravelguy.ca[http://www.thattravelguy.ca]

Working
H1N1 threatens systems
Canwest News Service
349 words
26 December 2009
Saskatoon Star Phoenix
SSP
Final
F14
English
Copyright © 2009 Saskatoon Star Phoenix

If making people sick weren't enough, the H1N1 virus risks claiming another victim: the corporate computer network.

If the swine flu pandemic becomes severe enough, businesses might have to ask a big chunk of their staff to work from home -- as much as 60 per cent, according to some projections. But very few companies are prepared to handle that level of telecommuting.

"Companies are very much in react mode when it comes to pandemic preparedness," said Paul Sexton, a consultant at IBM Canada's Business Continuity and Resiliency Services.

According to research firm IDC, only one-fifth of companies have a tested pandemic plan in place.

While many companies' systems can handle a 15 per cent level of telecommuting through remote connections, few have the capacity for a larger contingent. A sudden spike in external access can bring unprepared systems to a halt.

Communications and data security can also be a major headache in the event of a full-blown pandemic.

A business operating with high absenteeism still has to be reachable for customers and partners. Companies with call centres are especially vulnerable if agents have to work from home, and it's hard to implement a sophisticated call-routing system during a crisis.

Even if calls are properly routed, problems can extend to the home if there's no proper planning.

If a house has only one landline, families might start fighting over who gets to use it, Sexton said.

And it doesn't end there, warned Justin Folkerts, a security analyst at Fusepoint Managed Services in Toronto.

"Is there enough storage on the phone voicemail system to handle inboxes filling up over two weeks?" he asked.

"Do workers have the ability to access e-mail and check their voicemail from home?

"These are the gaps we see as problematic."

The first step any company should take in drafting a pandemic plan, analysts agree, is identify which systems are critical to operations.

Then make sure there's always someone who can manage those systems, either on-site or remotely.

Bronwyn Eyre
News
Don't needle me over this, please
Bronwyn Eyre
The StarPhoenix
769 words
26 December 2009
Saskatoon Star Phoenix
SSP
Final
A2
English
Copyright © 2009 Saskatoon Star Phoenix

And the news image of 2009 is . . . The Close-up Needle Jab!

Close runner-ups include Michael Jackson, Tiger Woods and Balloon Boy. But it was the nightly needle that dominated this year's newscasts.

Speaking of which, is all that H1N1 arm-stabbing over yet? Can I watch the TV news again without having to wince at the hundredth poke?

I realize a tight close-up of an arm getting vaccinated is grabby. But there's something excessive -- not to mention distracting -- about showing 10 or 12 injections in a brief news segment.

In fact, I wouldn't be surprised if needle-averse people were so unnerved by those continual close-ups, they actually passed on getting the shot.

The needle fixation makes me think of various other fads and fetishes that have become popular with news broadcasters.

Take, for example, the new standing shtick on CBC's The National -- picked up, apparently, from American entertainment and news shows.

In a sense, I suppose, standing signals immediacy. But mainly it distracts. Inevitably, the viewer pays more attention to the newscasters' clothes sense and posture than to the story being told.

I feel for them, as they try to decide how to stand -- casual or formal? -- and how to hold their news script, like a rectangle fig leaf covering their privates or, awkwardly, at waist height. Practically anyone would prefer a chair behind a desk.

And what's with the light show? The National ought to kill those gliding, blinking background visuals they recently introduced. They half-mezmerize as they flow and change, non-stop, from right to left.

Another news trend that's got to go is the mystery-voice technique -- that protracted voice-over you hear while you're looking at, say, a building or a back alley. A few seconds is fine. But often, you hear a voice talking about the story, and for maybe 10 seconds you have no idea who the person is. It's as if the building or the back alley is doing the talking.

It's hard enough to pick up words recorded in less-than-ideal conditions. Seeing the person speak them often helps.

The showing of video footage is often overdone anyway. If a reporter or the anchor is talking about a sawmill or a hospital, we really don't need to see endless visuals. We know what they look like. Or if footage must be shown, how about a split-screen?

If a story is complex or involved, it should be presented in a way that people watching will be able to get it, even if that means showing charts or maps or printing out certain names, places and quotations.

At the end of too many stories -- especially intricate, international ones -- you think: What was that all about? From time to time, news teams ought to have focus groups watch selected reports, then ask them to relate what they just heard. The result would probably be humbling, if not hilarious.

Some newscast practices are just plain irksome. For example, why run "streeter" interviews of citizens who prove to have no view on the matter at hand? Or zoom-ins that unflatteringly focus on the subject's skin pores or nose hairs? Or cheesy theme music or "swoosh" sound-effects interjected mid-broadcast?

And during the Christmas season, do we really need a nightly story on whether people have finished their shopping or not? Or what retail sales might be after the figures are tallied? The point is, no one knows yet. It's pure speculation.

Perhaps the oddest trend among the newsies -- especially on the national broadcasts -- is the ordering of the stories. Stories of highest consequence always used to lead, with others following in descending importance.

These days, lesser stories often open newscasts. Recently, for example, one of the national networks led with a three- or four-minute story on the World Cup soccer draw. A story on the fact that Russia says it may now help the West to impose sanctions on Iran -- jaw-dropping information with enormous implications -- was given a two-sentence reference about three-quarters through the program.

The story of another Canadian Olympic skier being injured is discouraging. But it oughtn't take precedence over, for example, more in-depth analysis of the complex interraction between the House of Commons and the Senate which could, we're now told, lead to another prorogation of Parliament.

But I digress.

Let the 2010 images begin.

bronwyn.eyre@sasktel.net

Celeb News With Attitude
Marquee
Plenty of absurdities to reflect back on
Doug Camilli
Canwest News Service
670 words
26 December 2009
Windsor Star
WINSTR
Final
B4
English
Copyright © 2009 Windsor Star

What? Huh? December again, already? Time to take stock and sum up, you say? Well, somebody's got to do it, so I sat down with the tools of my trade.

While cutting the lime into wedges, I gave 2009 the mental once-over: Recession. Afghanistan. Swine flu. Bernie Madoff. The Michael Jackson industry. Iran. The climate change issue. Olympic marketing overkill. The coalition government fuss. The New York Yankees winning the World Series. Michael Ignatieff. Tiger Woody. Ugh.

2009 is surely the Year of the Vampire. The lifeless undead were ubiquitous this year, first in teen fiction, then in teen movies, teen TV, everywhere. True Blood, The Vampire Diaries, Twilight, etc., etc.

Why are we obsessed with vampires? Sociologists droned on about anxiety in the culture. Novelist Anne Rice calls vampirism "a powerful metaphor for the outcast and monster in all of us."

VAMPIRES WERE NOT, HOWEVER, THE ONLY ABSURD THING ABOUT '09. HERE ARE SOME OF MY OTHER FAVOURITES:

- Those wacky Belgians: Belgium's national bodybuilding championships were cancelled after government anti-doping inspectors showed up - and all 20 competitors ran away. They should have expected some testing: last year three-quarters of competitors tested positive for steroids and other bad stuff.

- Tweet of the year: Singer Lily Allen: "People say I'm egocentric. But enough about them."

- Toughest review of the year: Deborah Ross in The Spectator mag: "There really isn't a lot to say about Lesbian Vampire Killers apart from this: don't go anywhere near it. Just don't see it. Do something else instead. Do anything else instead. Catch up with your ironing ... at 87 minutes, it's probably a good 87 minutes too long."

- What could possibly go wrong?: Solange Magnano, Miss Argentina of 1994, died from complications after buttock-enhancement therapy. She was 38.

- Wedding of the year: In Suvereto, near Livorno in Italy, a bride and groom had arranged for the bride's bouquet to be tossed to the bridesmaids and single female guests from a small plane. But the flowers somehow got sucked into the engine and the plane caught fire, exploded, and plunged into a hostel, Corriere della Sera reported. One person was badly hurt.

- You can see she's got a future in politics: Paula Abdul, when asked about a report that Simon Cowell had asked her out on a date, answered this way: "There's some truth to that, and no truth to it."

- Politician of the year: If you crossed Hugh Hefner with Mackenzie King, you'd have Italy's Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi, still in office after a year of scandals involving prostitutes, strippers, models, his quasi-secret hideaway Villa Certosa, etc., etc. I say nothing of his financial scandals.

At one point before she left him, his wife Veronica Lario spoke up to denounce the old goat's habit of appointing showgirls to be his party's candidates for the European Parliament. She also mocked him for turning up at the 18th birthday party of a blond model whose parents he knows: "It really surprised me, given that he never went to the 18th birthday parties of his own children."

- "Men are pigs" dept.: Mel Gibson, Jon Gosselin, David Letterman, Ronnie Wood, Gordon Ramsay, John Edwards, Mark Sanford, Eddie Cibrian and, of course, Tiger Woods.

My favourite is Sanford, governor of South Carolina, who was supposedly "hiking the Appalachian trail," when in fact he was in South America with a mistress. All over South Carolina, guys in bars started getting slapped in the face just for inviting girls to go hiking.

Oh, man, no more space? I was just getting started. But I suppose there's a limit to how awful a year can be. I hope so, anyway, because from here it looks like 2010 will have all the same old problems, plus who can say what new ones?

Oh well. Happy New Year, folks!

News
Word of the Year candidates revealed
Misty Harris
Canwest News Service
607 words
26 December 2009
Windsor Star
WINSTR
Final
A20
English
Copyright © 2009 Windsor Star

In a year of recession, our strongest currency was language. And over the next few weeks, North America's eminent lexicographers, grammarians and linguists will decide which word accrued the most social value of all.

From aporkalypse to Dracula sneeze, Botax to balloon boy, the buzzwords of 2009 hold a mirror not only to what shaped the headlines but also to the breakneck speed at which we saw language evolve. On Jan. 8, members and friends of the American Dialect Society will review the top coinages and, in what's become the lexical equivalent of the Oscars, bestow the distinction of Word of the Year.

"This isn't some government-appointed august body of intellectuals gathering in a room to officially induct words into English," says Grant Barrett, chairman of the ADS's New Words Committee. "It's a room full of rabble. And at the heart of it, the main purpose is to highlight changes in language."

Among Barrett's 2009 nominations are: "aporkalypse," describing worldwide hand-wringing and panic over swine flu; "I'mma let you finish," a meme spurred by Kanye West's interruption of Taylor Swift's acceptance speech at an awards show; "Dracula sneeze," the playful shorthand for sneezing into the crook of one's arm; and "Great Recession," widely used to describe economic downturn.

Other words with lex appeal, and nominated by fellow language experts, include: "Botax," a proposed levy that would be charged on cosmetic procedures to help pay for U.S. health care reform; "sext," a sexually explicit message or photo sent by mobile phone; "balloon boy," used to describe a six-year-old boy whose parents claimed he floated away in a homemade balloon; and "app," a once-niche abbreviation made mainstream, primarily, by ubiquitous iPhone ads.

"What makes these words so appealing is that they do define this particular moment in time," says Nancy Friedman, a name developer at California-based Wordworking and contributor to this year's nomination pool.

"But very few survive. The ones that carry over aren't the ones that pop out; they're the more pedestrian words that don't draw a lot of attention."

Google, for example, was never flashy enough to become an ADS Word of the Year but is a frontrunner for Word of the Decade because of its stickiness, utility and enduring cultural significance. Y2K, by contrast, easily won 1999's top honour but has all but vanished from pop parlance since.

Explains Barrett: "The faster a word spreads, the faster a word dies. The best language is unobtrusive."

Although language is always on the move, Canadian word watchdog Mark Abley remarks that the past two decades have seen the most dramatic rate of development.

"These (word of the year) exercises have become a kind of quasi-official recognition that the English language is evolving at amazing, even alarming speed," says Abley, the Montreal-based author of The Prodigal Tongue: Dispatches from the Future of English.

"By designating a particular word or phrase as emblematic of 2009, we highlight some event or person that mattered to lots of us in the past year. But we also do something more: we highlight the process of language change itself."

Previous winners of the ADS's Word of the Year include bailout (2008), truthiness (2005), metrosexual (2003), weapons of mass destruction (2002), 9-11 (2001), soccer mom (1996), information superhighway (1993) and Not! (1992).

On the web at http://www.americandialect.org/[http://www.americandialect.org/]

Photo: Steve McKinley, Canwest News Service / What new words will eventually make their way into our dictionaries?

Editorial/Opinion
Alcohol blood test seems out of wack
R.H. Eves
The Windsor Star
282 words
26 December 2009
Windsor Star
WINSTR
Final
A9
English
Copyright © 2009 Windsor Star

It was stated that the level of alcohol in a person's blood is found by blowing 50 milligrams, a weight value, per 100 millimetres, a distance value. What that has to do with the quantity of alcohol in a person's blood remains a mystery to me.

It should be a percentage of either a volume measurement to another volume measurement or a weight measurement to another weight measurement. In other words, if you had a container which contained one cubic inch of blood and you drained one-quarter of that amount of blood and replaced it with the same amount of alcohol, the alcohol content of the new mixture would be 25 per cent or .25 of the original.

What that instrument is saying makes about as much sense as saying there are three ounces of alcohol per foot. This would certainly leave the accuracy of this Alcotest 7410 GLC gadget in doubt, would it not?

The question of sanitation also has to be considered. This instrument apparently has to be completely placed in the driver's mouth. Does the driver of the vehicle do that? Or, does the officer do it? There certainly should be some kind of sanitation procedure carried out, should there not?

With all the gadgets around today, like cellphones, GPS units, calculators, etc., could there not be a personal portable type of detector developed for private use? Or, maybe a coin-operated one located in various public places, like bars, clubs, etc., where alcohol is served, and with sanitation procedures required, particularly, with the H1N1 epidemic.

R.H. EVES, Windsor

News
When there'snothing left to fear
Kathryn Blaze Carlson
National Post
1429 words
26 December 2009
National Post
FINP
National
A12
English
(c) 2009 National Post . All Rights Reserved.

More than 500 years ago, European explorers sailed into the dark and temperamental ocean, embracing uncertainty in the hopes of turning the unknown into the known. Modern-day explorers took their quest to the endless bounds of outer space, again championing the uncharted despite the perils that might lay ahead.

But since the fall of the Twin Towers nearly a decade ago, and arguably a short while before, uncertainty began making people anxious, not excited. That day, it became clear that faraway people are capable of unleashing devastation on the New World. Suddenly, America -- and the West by association -- was vulnerable.

Follow that with Hurricane Katrina, SARS, Avian Flu, the threat of climate change and, most recently, H1N1 influenza, and we have witnessed an era increasingly marked by fear, preparation and more preparation.

"When you regard the future with such dread, uncertainty is no longer something that can be managed in a relaxed way," said Frank Furedi, professor of sociology at the University of Kent and author of Politics of Fear, Where Have All the Intellectuals Gone? "If people feel vulnerable, then even the slightest challenge to their existence will be traumatic. And so we tend to dramatize the unexpected, and work hard to accident-proof ourselves."

Today, there is a multibillion-dollar industry devoted to accident-proofing and risk mitigation. Universities have launched masters programs in Business Continuity Planning and in Disaster and Emergency Management. Non-profit organizations such as Canada's nationwide Disaster Recovery Information Exchange have grown, and more and more people are flocking to gatherings like the World Conference on Disaster Management, held in Toronto earlier this month.

"The biggest change I've seen in the last 10 years is an increased awareness and acceptance at a more senior level within business," said Ralph Dunham, managing director of business continuity practice for Marsh Risk Consulting. "They've been faced with managing higher levels of risk, more governance demands from shareholders and more regulatory demands by the government."

In fact, on April 10, 2006, in the midst of growing concern over an Avian Flu pandemic, the Office of the Superintendent of Financial Institutions Canada sent a letter to all federally regulated financial institutions that said: "OSFI expects financial institutions to review their business continuity plans and consider the adequacy of those plans for dealing with a potential pandemic."

Three years later, governments and businesses have turned their attention to a possible H1N1 pandemic, which the World Health Organization framed as a threat to human existence and which governments deemed a threat to national security.

But the H1N1 pandemic did not strike as expected, and Mr. Dunham worried that another non-event -- in the vein of Y2K -- might dissuade businesses from crafting solid emergency preparedness plans.

"I feared that H1N1 might bring pandemic fatigue, that people might say the Chicken Littles of the world took our money," he said. "But companies seem committed to improving on the plans they scrambled together."

Prof. Furedi said this commitment stems from fear not only of the known and likely, but also the unknown and unlikely.

At a press briefing in February 2002, former U.S. defense secretary Donald Rumsfeld described the notion of "unknown unknowns," pointing to those threats that authorities have not yet fathomed and for which, therefore, they cannot prepare.

But Prof. Furedi said the notion of the "unknown unknown" was first channelled by environmentalists who warned years ago that, all things constant, oceans will someday swallow the Earth. Prof. Furedi argues this mentality has likewise gripped today's mothers and fathers, creating a generation of paranoid parents who obsess over their children's safety and buy gadgets to track their whereabouts.

"Once you assume that what's most dangerous is not what you know, but what you don't know, then the argument is that we must act now," he said.

In a sense, the probability of an event today matters less than the possibility of an event. For Lee Clarke, a professor of sociology at Rutgers University and author of Mission Improbable, this emphasis on possibilities offers a reasonable way to talk about extreme events.

"Purchasing life insurance is an insane bet, but we do it anyway -- from a probabilistic point of view, it's irrational," he said. "This is an example that, in some circumstances, it's worthwhile to make choices on the basis of possibilities because the consequences could be huge."

Prof. Clarke said preparedness is largely about the "what if," and the "what if" has grown ever louder over the years.

"We've moved to dangerous places, we concentrate ourselves and therefore make ourselves big targets, and we rely on technology," he said. "These are the kinds of hazards or risks that were simply impossible a hundred years ago."

But Prof. Furedi said today's propensity for preparedness is less pegged to past catastrophes, and instead born from a slow and steady cultural shift.

"From the 1980s onwards, the paradigm of the resilient human being gave way to the paradigm of the vulnerable human being," he said, pointing to what he considered a devaluation of masculinity as an example. "The stiff upper-lip gave way to showing your feelings."

With this, he argues, came a re-definition of humanity as highly vulnerable, a description governments today use to identify at-risk groups when speaking about pandemics, climate change and terrorism. "Included in that list of vulnerables are children, elderly, ethnic minorities and women, which essentially adds up to 110% of the population," he said. "The latest spin is 'The Vulnerable.' By putting 'The' in front of 'Vulnerable,' you equate the term with a person's identity."

This rise in existential insecurity opened the doors for what Prof. Furedi calls "fear entrepreneurs" -- those who prey on people's insecurities. Fear, he said, has become a resource, compelling the fearful to prepare for anything and everything, regardless of probabilities or cost.

But perhaps that is for the better, Prof. Clarke said: "When it comes to preparing for things that don't end up happening, we should all be so lucky."

---------

Out with the Aughts

Document FINP000020091226e5cq0002i


Allen Abel
News
Out with the Aughts; Exploring the ideas that launched the millennium and will shape our world for decades to come. A10-13, FP1-9, WP4-5
Allen Abel
National Post
1974 words
26 December 2009
National Post
FINP
National
A1
English
(c) 2009 National Post . All Rights Reserved.

It was during the last summer of the Second Millennium, as the universe vibrated with excitement and nervous expectation, that a New Yorker named David Samuels finally made up his mind. He would offer himself in marriage to the lovely Samantha Bilker as the centuries flipped, and he would tell everyone about his plan ... except Sam.

Mr. Samuels, a successful 32-year-old dot-com venture incubator -- remember them? -- called The New York Times and purchased a tiny advertisement to be placed at the bottom of Page One on Day One of the infant epoch. The ad was to read: "David M. Samuels and Samantha H. Bilker Announce Their Engagement 01/01/00 ADVT."

Mr. Samuels paid in advance. Then he booked a pair of plane tickets to Central America to ride out the end of the world with the woman he loved.

---

Who could know what was to come? It was the anxious eve of Y2K, when brokers selling the snake oil of Armageddon convinced many of us that, at the stroke of midnight on Dec. 31, computer systems would fail to recognize the correct date, the world financial system would crumble, and planes would fall from the sky.

In New York City, they were right on two out of three. It would take nearly nine years for the monetary meltdown to occur, but only six hundred and seventy-nine days -- until the 11th of September, 2001 --for hell to drop from the air.

On New Year's Eve, 1999, none of us could reckon that. We were gathered in our giddy millions in Times Square, exuberant, expectant, and, given the warnings of impending chaos, strangely calm. I remember filing my lead to the National Post that day: "We were casting out the century of Auschwitz and Apollo in the self-appointed centre of the world ..."

And at midnight, standing just outside T.G.I. Fridays at the corner of Broadway and 46th Street, as the screams of the multitudes filled the air and confetti snowed down from the skyscrapers, I kissed my sister full on the lips and then -- having leapt across centuries, still alive, still whole -- I started to cry.

---

David M. Samuels's little advertisement appears on the front page of the copy of The New York Times, now mounted and framed, that I bought just after midnight on 01/01/00. Above it were headlines that included "Computers Prevail in First Hours of '00" and "Yeltsin Resigns; Putin Takes Over." And there was another tiny notice at the bottom of the front page: "Be a noisemaker in the new millennium! Register your growing business with bizzed.com.ADVT." The ad didn't disclose it, but Bizzed.comwas an Internet tentacle of Citibank, intended to leverage its parent's "reputation and clout to create partnerships with many different online and offline firms." Well, it didn't. By January 2001, Money magazine was writing that "Citibank's Bizzed.commight as well as have been cryogenically frozen for the last year." Last winter, millions of suckers (including me) got clouted as Citi stock fell from US$40 a share to US$3. And, as 2010 dawns, UrbanDictionary.comdefines "bizzed" as "A feeling of frustration or anger often after an extreme insultation or verbal twatting." ---

A couple of weeks ago, I went back to Times Square to try to recapture that magical midnight and to talk to people about the tumultuous decade that followed.

From the lights and the din and the craziness, you never would have guessed that it wasn't New Year's Eve. There had to be a hundred thousand people on Broadway, gawking and hawking and absorbing the rays of radium that penetrate, just from being in New York.

In fact, the crystal-blue ball whose measured descent heralds each new year already was up in place, and behind it was a gigantic poster of a $100 bill and the cryptic message: "I'll Be Back."

I took this as a sign that the Great Recession was over and sidled up to a man named M-----, a son of a former French colony, who was selling tickets for a bus tour of Manhattan. So were about a dozen other immigrant vendors who laughed and nodded as M-----summed up New York and the Third Millennium this way:

"Every day, you are waiting for tomorrow, but you can never touch it. They say, 'If you can make it here, you can make it anywhere,' but you can't make it here. Here, you kill yourself on the street to make your dream. You have your home, you have your car, and you pay for them all your life.

"To have that dream here, you have to say, 'My old country is garbage.' It's like you could spend your money on your wife, nice clothes, send her to gym, make herself beautiful, but instead you spend on another woman. America is the other woman. Your country is your wife."

M-----wasn't in the square on New Year's Eve, 1999, and he won't be there this year, either. Every year, at two o'clock in the afternoon, he leaves for a church in Brooklyn and remains there in prayer. Who knows? Perhaps it was his devotions that got us through Y2K.

"How are you doing in the recession?" I asked.

"Worse worse worse worse worse," M-----said.

---

Outside T.G.I. Friday's this time was a man named Patrick, who had bought a fistful of tickets to a comedy club and who now was trying to sell them at a profit. This was fully legal; it was how dozens of people make their living every night in Times Square.

"Who wants to see a comedy show?" Patrick called out, with limited enthusiasm.

He was a computer expert, recently laid off by Deloitte Touche Tohmatsu, which was the thanks he got for helping the company not get stung by the Millennium Bug, in '99.

"The economy hit everybody," he said. "The department I was in, a lot of people got thrown into the cold."

I asked him where he was

on 9/11.

"Across the street," he said. "I saw them come down."

---

So there were reminders of a decade of tragedy and horror.

On March 6, 2008, a "large man" in a hooded grey sweatshirt riding a bicycle through Times Square dropped a box containing an improvised explosive device outside the U.S. Armed Forces Recruiting Center just below the New Year's crystal ball. Now, I walked down to look at the famous little building and, on its southern wall, found a bronze plaque that listed all the New York-born winners of the Congressional Medal of Honor, which is America's Victoria Cross.

Listed under "Global War on Terror" were two names:

Corporal Joseph L. Dunham USMC and Lieutenant Michael P. Murphy USN.

Later, I learned:

On April 14, 2004, three days after Easter Sunday, Cpl. Dunham was manning a checkpoint in Karabilah, Iraq, when an insurgent leapt from his car and began choking Cpl. Dunham. A scuffle ensued as two Marines approached to help. Suddenly, the insurgent dropped a grenade. Cpl. Dunham took off his Kevlar helmet, dropped to the ground and covered the explosive as best he could. The blast seriously wounded all three Marines. Eight days later, Cpl. Jason L. Dunham died at Bethesda Naval Hospital from wounds he received in the incident. He was 22.

And:

While continuing to be fired upon, Lt. Murphy made contact with the SOF Quick Reaction Force at Bagram Air Base and requested assistance. He calmly provided his unit's location and the size of the enemy force while requesting immediate support for his team. At one point he was shot in the back causing him to drop the transmitter. Lt. Murphy picked it back up, completed the call and continued firing at the enemy who was closing in. Severely wounded, Lt. Murphy returned to his cover position with his men and continued the battle.

These were two young men who would not see Y2K plus 10 next weekend, while the rest of us drank and cheered.

---

David M. Samuels and Rebecca H. Bilker were married on Nov. 25, 2000. They had waited out Y2K in Belize, which would have been a palatable place to be stranded, had everything gone haywire. David did propose on New Year's Eve, much to Sam's surprise, and dozens of their friends and relatives did learn the news from the miniscule advertisement on Page One of The New York Times.

Then the dot-com lunacy exploded -- "it was all smoke and mirrors" -- and David M. Samuels went from being, in his words, "the richest guy Sam knew to the poorest guy Sam knew."

He is telling me this at six in the morning in the office of the spotless Esposito's Finest Quality Sausage factory on West 38th Street, a short walk from Times Square. Mr. Samuels has been the proprietor of the firm since 2002, and if the presence of a not-especially-kosher-keeping Jew at the helm of a traditional Italian salsicceria strikes you as odd, well, remember, this is New York.

As representatives of the first decade of the 21st century, David and Samantha Samuels are perhaps typical: they were not killed on 9/11, they did not fight and die in the sands of Asia or win the Medal of Honor, they were not in the Big Easy during Hurricane Katrina, and the tsunami of 2004 didn't reach Westchester County, N.Y.

Yet they, like most of us, were brushed by calamity and preserved by chance. On Sept. 11, 2001, David was working in a building a few steps from the Twin Towers; he got off his bus to see papers whirling in the air and saw the second jet hit. His father, semi-retired, didn't come into his office in the World Trade Center that day. Samantha was uptown, working for a ladies' pajama company. There but for fortune.

A "cousin's best friend's younger brother" perished in Iraq. The sausage business suffered somewhat from the fears of the ignorant that swine flu can be contracted by eating swine, but margins have remained good and he hasn't had to lay off a single worker. Samantha helped him with the business and delivered children named Harry, Skyler and Casey.

"That would make the decade by itself," David says.

Like me, he keeps the front page of The Times framed on a wall, the better to remember that starry, star-crossed crossing of the centuries.

"I guess it sums up the state of the world," he says. "Not everyone is treated the same. Countless families were shattered by 9/11, soldiers lost in war, bankruptcies, lost jobs, lost homes. The world is not an equal or fair place."

"If anybody asked me about any specific event," Samantha says, "of course it was tragic. It was terrible. But except for losing tons of money that I never really had in reality, only had on paper, nothing happened."

"We are very lucky people," I suggest.

"Yes," says David M. Samuels. "It's been a great decade."

News
Seasonal flu or needle boost H1N1 shot efficacy; Research on ferrets suggests one flu vaccine may prime effectiveness of other
TOM SPEARS
Canwest News Service
522 words
24 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A10
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

Having a seasonal flu shot, or even a case of ordinary flu, appears to "prime" an H1N1 vaccination and make it more effective, new research from Italy shows.

The results are a surprise, because seasonal flu shots alone give no protection against H1N1.

But the finding provides evidence that a seasonal flu shot would be especially useful for children, or for people with weakened immune systems.

The study from Novartis, a major vaccine maker, is based on ferrets, not humans. Ferrets catch flu in a way that's closer to humans than any other animals.

A research team in the Italian city of Siena knew that vaccines were being rushed into production while many questions about H1N1 were still unanswered.

"Combating an ongoing epidemic is an inexact science," they write in today's issue of Science Translational Medicine, a research journal.

"Little is known about how previous seasonal flu vaccination affects H1N1 vaccines.

"Under extreme time pressure, public health officials have made their best guesses about the appropriate H1N1 vaccine formula and dosage to speed up manufacturing."

The experiment found that ferrets given a seasonal flu shot, followed several weeks later by an H1N1 shot, mounted a much more efficient immune response when exposed to the H1N1 virus.

"The seasonal vaccine by itself provides no protection against H1N1," study author Rino Rappuoli wrote in an email to Canwest News Service. "It only primes the immune system for a faster and better response to an H1N1 vaccine."

The ferrets with two flu shots created a greater number of antibodies to flu. This "higher concentration of antibodies ... corresponded directly to increased protection from H1N1 infection," the team reports.

Many Canadians have had two shots, but in the reverse order - H1N1 first. (A still-unpublished study from British Columbia suggested people who had the seasonal flu shot were more likely to get H1N1.)

Rappuoli said this should not be a problem.

"In the case of Canada, it is not important when they receive the seasonal vaccine in 2009, because people have already been primed by the vaccine of 2008 or by an influenza infection earlier in their life," he wrote in his email.

Earl Brown, a University of Ottawa virologist and flu researcher, said it's even possible that having a seasonal flu shot after the H1N1 shot might boost H1N1 resistance.

"The main point of this is that the similarity (between flu strains) is close enough that immunity to one can enhance immunity to the other," he said.

Both the seasonal and swine flus are in the H1 group of viruses, he noted.

The scientists in Siena said they don't know why the seasonal flu shot acts as a booster when it provides no protection on its own. They also said the next study needs to be done in humans.

If it works in humans, it could help people with weakened immune systems, who might not get enough protection from an H1N1 shot alone.

Ottawa Citizen

City & Region
Flu clinics closed until 2010; 500,000 H1N1 vaccines given to Calgarians
Jamie Komarnicki
For the Calgary Herald
461 words
24 December 2009
Calgary Herald
CALH
Final
B3
English
Copyright © 2009 Calgary Herald

Three Calgary H1N1 vaccination clinics are scheduled to reopen for at least six days in the new year as public health officials continue the push to immunize as many Albertans as possible.

Roughly 500,000 people in the Calgary region have been inoculated against the pandemic virus since the mass clinics opened in late October, said Dr. Judy MacDonald, deputy medical officer of health in Calgary.

The remaining public clinics in the city closed for the holidays on Wednesday.

MacDonald said Alberta Health Services officials decided to resume the service from Jan. 4 to Jan. 9 because of the continued level of interest in getting the vaccine.

After the first week, health officials will assess whether to extend the clinics' hours.

"We will have to take a look at what the demand is that week when we resume our mass clinics on Jan. 4," MacDonald said on Wednesday.

The H1N1 vaccine has been distributed to hundreds of family physicians and specially trained pharmacists in Alberta to give out the shots.

Further, the vaccine has been available to third-party providers since early December.

Private organizations can contract their services to get the shot.

One of those providers, the Victorian Order of Nurses, has given H1N1 shots to about 400 companies this month, said spokeswoman Kathy Cormier. The shots cost about $13 an injection, she said.

"They're literally small companies to big ones. It just has to do with whether there's an interest in putting together a contract for setting it up," said Cormier, noting the group will provide the service "until there's no longer any interest."

Public health officials, meanwhile, say even though a second wave of the virus has waned, it's still important to get vaccinated to ward off yet another spike in cases.

Higher travel volumes around the holiday season can spread the virus -- another reason to get the shot, MacDonald said.

"With all the travel that happens over the holiday season, that may lead us to a third wave sooner than we might expect," she noted.

So far, H1N1 has been linked to the deaths of 65 Albertans.

The three clinics that will reopen Jan. 4 are: Avenida Village, Brentwood Village Mall and Whitehorn EMS Station.

Their hours of operation are noon to 7 p.m., Monday to Thursday; and 8:30 a.m. to 3:30 p.m., Friday and Saturday.

Mass influenza vaccination clinics will close for the holidays beginning today and reopen in January with some changes.

"We're tailoring the campaign to the public need," said Noreen Remtulla, a spokeswoman for Alberta Health Services. "The times and locations will vary around Alberta, based on their demand in their community."

The clinic in Millbourne Mall at 38th Avenue and Millwoods Road will reopen Jan. 4 and will run Mondays and Tuesdays. The clinic at Westmount Mall will be closed and replaced with one at the East Edmonton Health Centre, 910 112th Ave. It will operate Wednesdays and Thursdays, beginning Jan. 6.

Both clinics will be open from 1 p.m. to 8 p.m. On Saturdays, they will both be open from 9 a.m. to 4 p.m.

Communities in the surrounding area, including Strathcona County, Fort Saskatchewan, St. Albert, Spruce Grove and Stony Plain, will continue to offer appointment-only shots at their local health centres.

Remtulla added that pharmacies and doctors now have the vaccine available. So if families want to be immunized for H1N1 over the holidays, they can give their local pharmacies and doctors a call for their availability. Those going for the vaccination should bring a piece of identification and their health card, and wear short sleeves if possible.

Alberta has been shipped more than 2.2 million doses of vaccine, but only one-third of the population has been immunized. Another 344,000 doses are being stored for the province and would be shipped immediately upon request, according to the Public Health Agency of Canada.

On Oct. 26, my husband was admitted to the University Hospital in severe condition from the H1N1 flu virus.

He had no predisposition nor underlying circumstances that would have given us concern that he would have been hit so hard by the flu virus. He spent more than six weeks in ICU.

On Dec. 11, David moved out of ICU to a regular ward, where he continues to recover from the damage inflicted by the virus to his body.

We would like to thank the doctors, nurses and support staff for the excellent care that David has received during this ordeal and for the support given to our family.

There were times when the doctors told us to pray for the best, but prepare for the worst. The doctors warned us that if David survived this attack, he would be spending months in the hospital in recovery.

However, thanks to the excellent care of hospital staff and David's determination, it appears that David maybe home for Christmas or before the New Year.

Truly, it is our Christmas Miracle.
