James Gordon
Sports
Pens put hurtin' on Sens; Ottawa captain Daniel Alfredsson leaves game after taking a hart hit; goalie Pascal Leclaire skates into an ambush in his return from injury. Penguins 8, Senators 2
James Gordon
The Ottawa Citizen
1150 words
24 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
B1 / FRONT
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

On a night when the Ottawa Senators were supposed to be celebrating the return of No. 1 goalie Pascal Leclaire, and energy wingers Chris Neil and Shean Donovan from injury, they found themselves coping with another potentially devastating loss.

Captain and leading scorer Daniel Alfredsson left and didn't return after taking a hard hit in the first period of a thoroughly embarrassing 8-2 loss to the Pittsburgh Penguins. As Alfredsson dragged himself straight to the dressing room, Milan Michalek doled out some immediate payback by jumping Pittsburgh right-winger Craig Adams.

That set the tone for a long, sad, scrappy night for the Senators. If the players weren't pushing and shoving, it meant the Penguins were probably scoring a goal.

A team spokesman said Alfredsson suffered a shoulder injury and coach Cory Clouston said after the game, "It doesn't look good."

Ottawa's captain had his arm in a sling after the game and said he would likely be out for at least a few weeks.

"I'll probably see the doctor (today) and get an X-ray, and see what the damage is, but I expect at least a few weeks," Alfredsson said.

He said he hadn't watched the hit delivered by Adams, but acknowledged he was in a vulnerable position reaching for the puck.

As for Leclaire, it seems absurd to suggest he was strong in his first game back after an errant puck flew into the bench and broke his cheekbone a month ago, but that was the case.

Of the six goals he gave up over two periods of work, precisely none were his fault. The defensive breakdowns were so atrocious, the Senators could have started a four-by-six slab of titanium and still allowed a few goals.

Brian Elliott came in for mop-up duty in the third, allowing two more.

"Those (games) happen once or twice a year and they're not fun, but all you can do is try and forget about it," Leclaire said.

Prior to the game, Penguins coach Dan Bylsma laid out his strategy against the returning Leclaire.

"Two approaches you can take," he said. "You can fire no shots at him for the first 20 (minutes) and put him in a situation where he doesn't get any work when he probably wants to feel the puck.

"We're not going to take that one."

No, they didn't.

Leclaire had to make about four or five highlight stops in the first 10 minutes and Ottawa was lucky to escape the first period tied 1-1. The dam broke in the second, when Bill Guerin (twice), Ruslan Fedotenko, Chris Kunitz and Evgeni Malkin scored.

The line of Malkin, Kunitz and Guerin toyed with the Senators, totalling 12 points.

"You can't blame (Leclaire) for the situation we were in going into the third period," Clouston said. "I thought we had four or five unbelievable chances in the first period, we didn's score on them and it just seemed to snowball from there. We collapsed partway through the second and just couldn't recover."

After Malkin completed his hat trick in the third period, fans littered the ice with headwear as the loudspeakers boomed, "Let it Snow."

Anton Volchenkov and Chris Kelly scored for Ottawa.

With the contest all but over, the Senators were left to do what most teams do when they're getting annihilated: They started yapping and throwing punches.

Chris Neil challenged and defeated Mike Rupp early in the third, and Matt Carkner came together briefly with Adams less than a minute later. Shean Donovan, whose knee was injured on a hit by Penguins agitator Matt Cooke the last time the two teams met, tried to exact revenge, but didn't get a response. Cooke skated away and the referees skated in to assess Donovan a minor for unsportsmanlike conduct and 10-minute misconduct.

It was an ugly game all around and it set the stage for a difficult new year.

The Senators face the prospect of starting 2010 with Alfredsson and centre Jason Spezza, who is out until after the Olympic break with a knee injury, and two others expected to help lead the offence are mired in slumps. Michalek has one goal in 12 games, while Alex Kovalev has three goals in the past 16, all on the same night.

Forget the latest gadgets. They're probably hoping Santa brings back their scoring touches. So much for a merry Christmas.

For more Senators news and notes, read James Gordon's Hockey Capital blog at ottawacitizen.com

Canada & World
A survival guide for the holiday season; How to invite people, how to respond, how to exit a party, and the all-important gift exchange
Booth Moore
Los Angeles Times
1561 words
24 December 2009
Vancouver Sun
VNCS
Final
B7
English
Copyright © 2009 Vancouver Sun

You're hosting cocktails for 100 of your nearest and dearest in a week's time. What do you say to the 75 who still haven't bothered to open the Evite?

You overhear a party guest announcing he's recovering from swine flu -and he's standing under the mistletoe. Do you have to pucker up?

You're newly unemployed and your best pal gives you a Goyard bag whose price tag approximates a car payment. How should you break it to her that you won't be reciprocating?

Every holiday season brings its own set of etiquette challenges, and this year is no different. Electronic correspondence, whether through Facebook, Evite, e-mail, Twitter or text message, has created the need for a new set of social skills. So has the economy, as people try to navigate the annual traditions of gifting and tipping while budgets are being squeezed.

And if that isn't enough to dampen spirits, there's the H1N1 virus, which has made the mini Purell hand sanitizer the holiday season's must-have accessory.

After having your parking space stolen out from under you at the mall, and being tackled for the last Zhu Zhu pet, it would be easy to think the holidays bring out the Grinch in people. But etiquette experts say that's not always true.

"When we're holding up the airport security line because we're on the phone telling people we are running late, we're at our worst because we are not aware of the people around us. We're stressed, rushed or running around trying to multi-task," said Anna Post, author and spokeswoman for the Emily Post Institute in Burlington, Vt. "But when we celebrate with our friends and family, we are at our best because we know we are supposed to bring our A-game."

That starts with sending a polite invitation. For all but very formal occasions (weddings), electronic invitations are acceptable, but experts disagree on the delivery system.

Fashion publicist-turned-etiquette enthusiast Cooper Ray, whose website Social Primer specializes in men's manners, considers communications that originate at Evite cheap and those from Paperless Post chic. (Both websites allow users to design electronic invitations that are sent via e-mail.) "I also send out a lot of e-mail invitations from my personal account," he said.

Thomas P. Farley, creator of the website What Manners Most and a columnist for Pingg who lives in New York, disagrees. "E-mail is the lazy way out. If you can't take the trouble to log on to a website and create something with flair that gathers all the tools together that you and your guests will need, then maybe you don't have the time to throw a party."

Whatever form the invitation comes in, it's a guest's responsibility to reply as soon as possible.

"You hate to say no, to be a downer, and you think you should hold off until you're sure," Farley said. "But you should treat any electronic invite like you would a paper one and respond right away. You can tick the box that says 'maybe,' which at least gives the host a sense that you got the invite, that it didn't go to the wrong address."

Guests should know that many online invitation sites have features that allow a host to see who has opened an invitation-- and how many times. So don't be a party peeping Tom, lurking in cyberspace until the guest list is up to your standards. And once you've made a commitment to attend, keep it.

"Not to show up," Ray said," is a slap in the face."

If you can't make it, don't feel as if you have to explain in detail why. (Does anyone really want to hear about your hernia problems?)

"There is a whole thing in the Evite world of people one-upping with their declines," Post said. (I can't make it because I'll be rock climbing in Yosemite or adopting a baby in Malawi, that kind of thing.) "You can just say you're sorry and explain offline."

Guests are not required to take anything to a party, but it's a lovely gesture, Post said. "Some people bring a sweet item, a box of chocolates or a bottle of wine if you know your hosts drink," she said. (Ray recommends inexpensive sparkling wine.)

"Flowers in a vase are also nice, so your host or hostess doesn't have to interrupt party prep. Or, a cute trend I saw at the Adolphus Hotel in Dallas that could be applied to a holiday party is breakfast bread -- for your host to eat the next morning," Post said.

Once the party is in full swirl, palms are being pressed and cheeks kissed, don't immediately try to banish the biohazards; wait to pull out the Purell until you are in private.

And if a guest seems to be under the influence of swine and not wine? "A host should be kind and direct," Post said. "Something like, 'Sarah, you don't look so well. Maybe we should hang out another time.' "

Guests shouldn't be conversation hogs, and they should know when and how to leave. "When you arrive, make sure you make a beeline for the host to say hello. And when you leave, just go," Ray said. "I can't stand people who come up to the host at a rocking party and say, 'We're sooooo sorry we have to leave,' because the implication is how will this party go on without us? Don't disrupt the flow."

The morning after, Post recommends guests match their thank-yous to the invitation. "If you were invited on Evite, post a thank-you on the host's Facebook page. If they called you, call them back. And it's never wrong to send a written note."

Jacqueline Whitmore, a Palm Beach, Fla.-based author, etiquette coach and the founder of www.etiquetteexpert.com,encourages[http://www.etiquetteexpert.com,encourages] handwritten thankyous during the holidays, particularly if someone has had you over for dinner or given you a gift or a gift card. "E-mail is too impersonal for this time of year," she said.

And sometimes a handwritten note is gift enough, she says. In other words, you need to forget about parity when it comes to gift giving, particularly in this lean year. That all-expenses-paid Disneyland trip from Grandma, or the Louis Vuitton wallet from Big Sis -- accept them graciously and don't worry about reciprocating to the last cent.

"The gift was given in love; you should accept it with love, grace and thanks," Whitmore said. "If you want to reciprocate, do what you can do. Don't go overboard and get yourself into a deeper financial hole. You can give the gift of your time, a gift of food, a gift of volunteering. You don't have to go to the mall and buy another tchotchke."

Conversely, if you are the lavish gift giver, understand that circumstances for friends and family may have changed. "I'm a big believer in giving freely with no expectation in return," Ray said. "You could say something like, 'I thought of you when I saw this, and I know this has been a tough year, so please know that for me your presence is present enough.' "

Other people might want to reevaluate their gift-giving habits altogether. Secret Santa versus white elephant gift exchange? Best to start with a frank conversation.

"I wouldn't diminish anyone's dignity," Farley said. "I would just say, 'We're all watching our budgets this year. We've known each other long enough, our friendship can sustain a year without gift giving.' "

Post agrees. "This is a time to be direct and clear." If you have a sister who has been laid off, you might offer to change up your gift exchange.

"Present it to her since she's the one with the new situation. She may say she's been saving up, and it could be a point of pride. Or she may say, 'Thank goodness you said something; let's cook dinner together instead.'

"When budgets get trimmed, you can't pull money out of thin air," Post said. "If someone expects a gift, and they might be offended if they don't get one, it might be worth telling them that with the economy, you're going to have to skip gifts this season. Find another way to let them know you value them."

"It's going to be different this year," Whitmore said. "We all have to be sensitive and realize that not everybody is in the same boat."

It's important to greet the host when you arrive, and don't disrupt the flow by interrupting when it's time to leave -- just go. Officials probe case of child resistant to H1N1 treatment -
211 words
24 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A5
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

MANITOBA Health is investigating after a child suffering from H1N1 was found to be resistant to antiviral treatment.

The child -- identified only as under the age of 18 with an underlying medical condition -- did not need to be hospitalized, Manitoba Health reported Wednesday.

The provincial government is investigating because this is the first time a Manitoban has shown resistance to Tamiflu, which is used to treat those with H1N1.

It's the eighth such case in Canada, and other cases are being reported around the world.

It "appears this is an isolated case," with no evidence to suggest that a resistant strain of H1N1 flu has emerged, the government said.

The provincial government is not recommending any changes to its clinical-care guidelines.

This case was found as part of routine testing of influenza specimens for antiviral resistance at the Winnipeg-based National Microbiology Lab.

Antiviral resistance can occur when there is a shift or mutation of the flu virus into a resistant strain.

It can also result when people don't finish their prescribed medication or don't follow the directions for their use; or when prolonged treatment with antivirals has been prescribed, particularly for people with weakened immune systems.

-- Staff

Economist blames red ink on recession -
By Bruce Owen
545 words
24 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A11
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

A looming provincial budget deficit of about $600 million is more a symptom of the global recession than a government that can't control its spending, a Toronto economist says.

CIBC senior economist Warren Lovely said Manitoba's projected $592-million deficit for the 2009/10 budget year is in keeping with what other Canadian province's are experiencing with their pocketbooks. The province released the anticipated deficit on Tuesday in its second-quarter financial report. It's a $640-million negative adjustment to the $48-million summary surplus the province projected in the March budget.

"What Manitoba is going through is not dissimilar to what other provinces are going through in the rest of country," Lovely said. "I think it's safe to say that back in March the full extent of the weakness in the economy wasn't appreciated by anybody."

Lovely said the revenue shortfalls the Manitoba government cites are due in part to the recession in the United States, particularly with weaker industrial demand for electricity from Manitoba Hydro. Hydro's revised forecasted profit this year of $120 million is $145 million less than budgeted.

"These mid-year reports hopefully represent the worst," Lovely said, adding there are signs the economy is slowly improving. "There is reason for cautious optimism for the remaining quarters, but there is still some hard work to be done."

Besides the economy, Finance Minister Rosann Wowchuk has noted spring flooding and the campaign against the H1N1 flu hit the province's bottom line this year to the tune of $150 million. Plus, corporate income taxes are now forecast to come in $93 million lower than first predicted.

Despite the projected deficit, the government said Manitoba is still on track to average a $221-million surplus for the four-year period ending 2009-10, fulfilling its commitment under the Balanced Budget, Fiscal Management and Taxpayer Accountability Act. Under the act, budgeting is done over a four-year period and allows for a deficit to be posted in one of those years.

Critics say the deficit turn-about is more a sign of careless spending than the economy.

Opposition Progressive Conservative finance critic Rick Borotsik has said Premier Greg Selinger, who was finance minister for 10 years before taking over the premier's job in October, did not put away enough money in the fiscal stabilization fund to help offset bad budget years and also failed to pay down the province's debt.

Canadian Taxpayers Federation's Manitoba spokesman Colin Craig said the province should copy what New Brunswick is doing to right its financial ship. It placed a two-year wage freeze on public sector unions in last March's provincial budget to control expenses. Craig also said the province should freeze discretionary capital spending, such as work on the new Upper Fort Garry park on Main Street.

He said it also doesn't make sense for the province to commit $30 million towards a new polar bear exhibit at Assiniboine Park Zoo at this time, nor was it worth the estimated $35,000 it cost taxpayers to fly Selinger and other provincial officials to the recent world climate conference in Copenhagen.

bruce.owen@freepress.mb.ca

Comment Column
Good thoughts on this most optimistic of days
LAWRENCE MARTIN
748 words
24 December 2009
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
A19
English
2009 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

lmartin@globeandmail.com

On Christmas Eve, a day of bliss before the big one, we push aside the dreary thoughts and think of the good things.

From the year gone by, some come to mind.

This was the year when many feared the economy would fall off a cliff and we'd be in the dungeon of depression. It didn't happen. Capitalism survived. Keynesian economics – great, big government – came to the rescue. Wall Street teetered, but didn't topple. Auto companies were saved in bailouts. Consumers receded, but are back at the counters, grinningly loading up.

Over the perilous stretch, some learning was in evidence. Leaders learned not to repeat the mistakes of the early thirties that induced the Great Depression. They learned that when deregulation is dominant, when markets run free, grief follows. They knew from earlier experiences that, conversely, when the state gets too fat, the same can happen. The lesson for engraving is that compressed ideologies of the right and of the left are each to be avoided.

This was the year when swine flu, renamed H1N1, was supposed to swallow populations whole. Combine that with an economic collapse and you had the makings of an orgy of sorrow. But it didn't happen. The pandemic didn't pan out. H1N1 was not much worse than a normal flu season. While not quite in the class of WMDs, it was another hype job.

This was the year that threatened to put Canadians through the tedium of yet another federal election – the fourth in a five-year stretch. Smile, everyone. We avoided that as well.

Other good things transpired. Just when we thought our Prime Minister was becoming too lugubrious to bear, he shocked everyone by taking to the stage at one of those artsy galas he had flayed and did a rousing piano and song rendition of a Fab Four tune. Blood flowed through his veins. It was a sensation.

Past prime ministers did okay, too. Jean Chrétien received the exclusive Order of Merit from the Queen. It was a blessing for him because an overabundance of attention had been paid to the downside of his decade in power – the sponsorship imbroglio. There were other things the little guy from Shawinigan did. Hence the big prize.

His antagonist, Paul Martin, had something to be proud of as well. The G20 came formally into being as one of the world's foremost deliberative bodies. Mr. Martin was a driving force behind its creation. He set up a G20 of finance ministers and then pushed to have the concept broadened to heads of state. Canada's international image as a progressive, honest broker took a lot of hits in 2009. The formation of the G20 was more in keeping with our history.

One of the best things of the year didn't happen here, but its impact is very much felt in this country. The regressive span the United States endured under the gruesome twosome of George W. Bush and Dick Cheney came mercifully to a close on Jan. 20. In his first year in power, Barack Obama wasn't all he was cracked up to be. He was more a compromiser than a reformer. In escalating the Afghan conflict, he failed to move America off its crippling post-9/11 terrorist paranoia. But his administration steadied the economy after its near-collapse and has now, barring some unforeseen development, given his country, after a century of failed attempts, a semblance of a national health-care plan.

More importantly, despite the rabid counter-pressures of the legions on the American right, now led by hillbilly Sarah Palin, he has restored American fair-mindedness and faith in its leadership around the world. The prejudice of his predecessors is replaced by a man of enlightenment, inspiration and global perspective. Barack Obama has put his country back on the high road. He embodies the spirit as expressed in the words of Thomas Paine: “My country is the world and my religion is to do good.”

On Christmas Eve, such noble thoughts as that arise. It is the year's greatest day of anticipation, a day when the glimmers of optimism reach beyond the radiant faces of children to all ages. In 2009, we moved past the big scares. It was a year of survival. In 2K10, more is promised. Recovery begins in earnest. New frontiers beckon.

POEM / A VISIT FROM PIGGY FLU
Globe Life
The fright before Christmas; André Picard reimagines a holiday classic in the time of the H1N1 pandemic
ANDRÉ PICARD
688 words
24 December 2009
The Globe and Mail
GLOB
L1
English
2009 CTVglobemedia Publishing Inc. All Rights Reserved.

'Twas the night before Christmas, and all through the house,

No swine flu virus was spreading, not even to a mouse.

The sanitizer was placed by the sink with much care,

In hopes the end of the threat would soon be declared.

The children were cowering from the bug in their beds,

While visions of Tamiflu danced in their heads.

Mamma sneezed in her kerchief, Papa coughed in his sleeve,

If we are all immunized then this disease would just leave.

From Mexico H1N1 arrived like a flash,

One wave, then two, it cost loads of cash.

In the news, on the Web, arose such a clatter,

They dubbed it pandemic and made much of the matter.

We soon feared pork, travellers, then each other,

The memories of SARS became such a bother.

WHO said all humanity must be engaged in this battle,

The imminent global threat left us all rattled.

Level three, level four, level six back to five,

It's a wonder than any of us are still alive.

The numbers were staggering, millions could die,

Public health mobilized and not on the sly.

From province to province they said flu would go,

Infecting all in its path and laying them low.

When, what to our wondering eyes should appear,

But a pandemic plan that left us quacking with fear.

With serious messages expressed in grave tones,

It could be none other than David Butler Jones.

On radio, TV and newspaper ads he proclaimed,

Leona Aglukkaq, she said much the same.

“Wash your hands, wash your hands,” they maintained,

“Stay home if you're sick,” we must all do the same.

H1N1 has most assuredly gone viral,

Out of hand we can not let it spiral.

“Don't panic, don't panic, don't panic,” they said,

“Vaccinate, vaccinate, vaccinate,” instead.

Kiddies, preggers and priority groups first of all,

Then we jab 'em, we jab 'em, we'll jab 'em all.

We have enough vaccine to immunize all by Christmas,

The campaign's necessary, on this you can trust us.

Rush to a clinic, there's one close to you,

And remember queue-jumping's not a thing to do.

The doubters they said: “Well, this will never fly,

This vaccine's not needed, you've been caught in a lie.”

The shortages, the adjuvant, fuelled conspiracy theories,

Experts were stumped, couldn't keep up with the queries.

Spanish flu, don't forget, killed more young than old,

It could happen again, or so we were told.

Thus we stood in long lines for hours for the shot,

And later we'd wonder: Was it all for naught?

With nary a word, public health did its work,

The waits they all vanished, as did the irk.

This flu turned out to not be one for the ages,

Leaving us to wonder why we flew into rages.

The fear has since faded, the headlines have vanished,

The pandemic has peaked, the foe has been vanquished.

But no, we're still told, you could still get this bug,

'Cause during the holidays we kiss and we hug.

Giving the flu to granny is not a gift that will amuse,

Besides we have 20 million doses we still need to use.

Such a huge surplus is costly but ultimately pleasant,

To those more in need we can now send a nice present.

Was swine flu for real, a good old-fashioned threat?

Did we overreact, would it have been a better bet,

To respond with a wink of the eye and a twist of the head,

And make like we know of no disease we should dread?

Soon we can do our postmortem and be most unpleasant,

Second guess all the action, pretend we resent it.

But first let's exclaim, as H1N1 fades from sight,

“Happy Christmas to all, it was quite a good fright!”

*With apologies to Clement Clarke Moore, author of the iconic poem A Visit from St. Nicholas.

Strike, H1N1 hampered inspections

This week's discovery of rodents at a Spadina Ave. pizza shop and a bakery outlet at a subway station has put the spotlight on Toronto's restaurant inspection program.

The pass-fail card system, in which a red card closes the eatery until problems are corrected, was set back by last summer's 39-day civic workers' strike and the fight against the H1N1 flu pandemic.

Inspectors have since been working hard to catch up.

"It's been a rough year," said Councillor John Filion, chair of Toronto's board of health.

Nearly every week in Toronto, an establishment is closed down temporarily for food safety infractions. There were 41 closures this year and 46 in 2008.

Those statistics indicate the city, which has some 16,000 restaurants, food stores and bakeries, is staying on top of the serious cases, said associate medical officer of health Dr. Howard Shapiro, who notes they inspect "probably a few hundred places a day."

News
H1N1 easing but 'third wave' still a concern
Stuart Hunter
The Province
269 words
24 December 2009
Vancouver Province
VANPRO
Final
A24
English
Copyright © 2009 Vancouver Province

The number of new cases of H1N1 flu in B.C. is petering out but a health official says vigilance is needed to protect against a possible third wave.

The numbers from the latest weekly H1N1 update are encouraging, showing five new severe cases of the disease--three on Vancouver Island and one each in the Interior and Fraser Health regions -- as well as one new death in Vancouver Coastal Health, Deputy Provincial Health officer Dr. Eric Young said Wednesday.

"It is slowing down quite a lot," Young said. "We are at the tail-end of the outbreak."

Since April, the B.C. Centre for Disease Control has confirmed 1,037 H1N1 cases. There have been 359 cases in Fraser Health, including 17 deaths; 234 in Interior Health, including 13 deaths; 44 in Northern Health, including two deaths; 258 in Vancouver Coastal Health, including 10 deaths; and 142 on Vancouver Island, including 10 deaths.

There have been 52 deaths due to H1N1 this year, with 45 of them involving underlying medical conditions. Two cases are still being investigated.

Young attributed the decline in new cases to the impact of the vaccine-- with just over 40 per cent of British Columbians having been vaccinated against H1N1 -- and the ability to quickly get anti-virals to people with underlying medical conditions.

Young encouraged people to still get vaccinated.

"We are still a bit concerned about a third wave coming," he said. "Historically, when we have large pandemics like this, we see a third wave."

Murray Mandryk
Viewpoints
A Mouseland Christmas Tale
Murray Mandryk
The Leader-Post
733 words
24 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
B7
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

A Mouseland Gift of the magi. Based on the original fable by T.C. Douglas and updated to reflect Saskatchewan politics in December, 2009

As falling snowflakes danced on the steps of Mouseland's great Marble Palace, the two mortal enemies stepped out of the shadows to exchange their gifts.

There was nobody around to see Brad Cat and Dwain Mouse this Christmas embark on their wondrous gesture of sharing and kindness -- a quite remarkable event even for this metaphoric, fictitious political world we now visit but once a year.

Sadly, sharing and kindness had become rather scarce commodities in Mouseland -- now a misnomer of a place if there ever was one.

Rather than overrun with the vermin, those declaring themselves common mice had dwindled to a mere 26.4 per cent. Mouseland now was mostly inhabited by many other critters like rabbits and turkeys and pigs (now under creditor protection after being accused of starting H1N1) and gophers and chickens and coyotes (who were also in hiding, after ol' Bob Cat issued his five-dollar-a-paw bounty).

And there were cats, although it seemed all of Mouseland's cats had become as black as Christmas coal.

This constituted a major shift from how Mouseland politics used to operate. Traditionally, it was the mice that ran Mouseland because a little ol' churchmouse named Tommy -- as kind as he was shrewd -- discovered long ago that if the white cats and black cats were always fighting among themselves, they would never be able to get together to form a majority government.

But more than a decade ago, the white cats started clawing themselves into virtual extinction -- spelling big trouble for the mice.

Slowly but surely, the cats, including a few that escaped the hoosegow in the 1990s, formed one party. And now under Brad Cat's popular leadership, Mouseland seemed more united under a cat government than ever.

Except that it really wasn't.

The emergence of a strong cat government had made the mice madder and feistier than ever -- especially after Rod Cat started estimating Mouseland's wealth while imbibing in a bit too much catnip.

So the feisty mice chose as their leader the biggest, baddest, mouse they could find -- Dwain Mouse.

Whether this had been a wise selection was becoming increasingly questionable. Right from the very first day when Dwain Mouse's leadership team signed up a whole bunch of other critters who weren't even mice, trouble seemed follow the mice's big cheese. Some could ignore the gray creeping into Dwain Mouse's whiskers, but few could ignore his plummeting in the polls.

But one thing about Dwain Mouse: He sure could get under the cat's fur. And Brad Cat -- a bit of hot-head, despite his fun-loving side -- was every bit as eager to claw, bite and scratch.

Somehow, Dwain Mouse and Brad Cat had combined to make Mouseland even more raucous than ever, although that didn't seem to be what either really wanted.

You see, Brad Cat had never been all that interested in being a capitalist cat. He was better at the charitable spending side of politics (he especially loved spending). And Dwain Mouse was about the oddest mouse you'll ever meet in that he had little time for a mouse's partialness to charity. He fancied himself a captain of industry.

So after many secret phone calls that the two swore they would never again bring up on the floor of the Marble Palace, the foes agreed to meet on the steps of the Marble Palace on Christmas Eve to give each other the gift each had always wanted.

They nervously stepped into the light, exchanged their neatly wrapped gifts and scurried off to open them in seclusion.

The snow fell silently this Christmas eve ... until the silence was pierced by two simultaneous agonizing screams!

Not really acquainted with the concept of charitable providence, Dwain Mouse's gift to Brad Cat was but an empty box. And not really sure how to achieve capitalistic prosperity, Brad Cat could offer Dwain Mouse nothing more than an empty vessel.

So ended another year in Mouseland, with each failing to deliver what they promised.

One can only wonder what the year to come holds.

- Mandryk is the Leader-Post's political columnist.

NEWS
Missing health records probed by privacy office
Pat Hewitt
The Canadian Press
368 words
23 December 2009
Waterloo Region Record
TKWR
Final
A6
English
Copyright (c) 2009 Kitchener-Waterloo Record.

TORONTO

Ontario's information and privacy commissioner has launched an investigation after the health information of more than 83,000 people who attended H1N1 flu clinics in Durham Region, east of Toronto, was apparently lost.

A health department nurse was taking a USB key containing the records to her car in Whitby to take it to a remote clinic site on Dec. 15 when the device was lost. A search failed to turn it up.

"We believe it was lost on regional property. We have some video surveillance tape data to indicate that was the case," said Dr. Robert Kyle, chief medical officer of health for Durham Region.

The privacy commission office was advised Monday by the Durham Region health department that the device was missing, said spokesperson Bob Spence.

The USB key contained the names, addresses, phone numbers, dates of birth and health card numbers of patients who attended H1N1 flu vaccination clinics in the region between Oct. 23 and Dec. 15.

The device was not encrypted, even though Ontario Privacy Commissioner Ann Cavoukian had issued guidance to all health information custodians in March 2007 that where personal health data is stored on mobile devices in identifiable form, it must be encrypted.

That guidance was issued in a health order made to Toronto's Hospital for Sick Children after a laptop computer containing the personal health information of 2,900 patients was stolen from a doctor's minivan in downtown Toronto on Jan. 4, 2007.

"There is no excuse for unauthorized access to personal health information (PHI) due to the theft or loss of a mobile computing device - any PHI contained therein must be encrypted," Cavoukian said at the time.

When asked if the lack of encryption was a lapse, Kyle said that would be part of Durham Region's investigation.

The focus of the privacy commissioner's investigation will be on what happened, how it happened and what steps can be taken to prevent a similar incident from occurring in the future, Spence said.

The probe will work in co-operation with, but be separate from Durham Region's own probe, he added.

News
Menacing pandemic or typical flu?; A top story 2009: The H1N1 Flu
AARON DERFEL
The Gazette
774 words
23 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A4
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

H1N1 was arguably the biggest news story of 2009.

It could also be argued that H1N1 was the biggest non-story of the year.

As a health reporter who has been on the beat for more than a decade, I've written my share of stories about superbugs like C. difficile, the SARS outbreak in Toronto in 2003 and the Asian flu scare of 2006.

So in late April, when reports first circulated of a mysterious new swine flu virus killing scores of mostly young Mexicans, I felt a sense of déjà vu. But at the same time, I was puzzled and anxious, especially after the World Health Organization warned that "all of humanity (was) under threat."

Those conflicting feelings - that the danger was being exaggerated and yet our lives were all under threat - would never leave me, and made reporting on this issue especially difficult.

Certainly, the lack of consensus among experts didn't help. Was the WHO correct in even declaring a pandemic in the first place, since it appeared the H1N1 strain was not nearly as virulent as initially feared? Richard Schabas, the medical officer of health for Hastings and Prince Edward Counties in Ontario, went so far as to predict H1N1 would be no more lethal than the seasonal flu.

In fact, by year's end it's projected that H1N1 will have killed slightly more than 400 Canadians. By comparison, the seasonal flu is estimated to cause up to 2,500 deaths annually, although no one has ever kept highly accurate statistics until H1N1.

What's more, no one will ever know just how deadly H1N1 could be because of the unprecedented mass vaccination campaign that unfolded. And there were still frightening cases of young, previously healthy individuals dying from H1N1, like 13-year-old Evan Frustaglio of Toronto.

Here in Quebec, confusion and panic quickly set in, often fuelled by some in the news media and by the very government that had pledged to protect us. Public health officials devised a highly controversial list of priority groups, with the elderly among the last to receive their shots.

In retrospect, that decision turned out to be questionable, as the greatest percentage of all deaths, by far, occurred in those over the age of 60.

There were other questionable calls, as well as conflicting information between the federal and provincial governments. First was the decision by the Public Health Agency of Canada to use an adjuvant in the vaccine, delaying its delivery and raising concerns about its safety, since pregnant women under 20 weeks would be given shots without the adjuvant.

An official with the agency even advised The Gazette not to describe the immunity-boosting adjuvant as a chemical compound, even though it is just that. In the end, there were few reports of severe reactions to the vaccine.

Equally challenging was trying to sort out all the contradictory pieces of information for readers. In the pages of The Gazette, Ottawa ran full-page ads about high-priority groups that didn't match up with other ads by the provincial government.

At the start of the vaccination campaign, both Ottawa and Quebec urged that children from the ages of 6 months to 5 years get a second booster shot, but weeks later they determined that a single inoculation was sufficient despite a lack of extensive research on the subject.

Like other journalists, I waited in line for a flu shot - actually, I had to go twice: first, to accompany my nearly 3-year-old son, Daniel, to receive his and then for myself. Yet just five days after getting the shot - during the peak of the second wave of infections - I, too, got the flu.

Ironically, I felt the quick onset of symptoms as I was filing an H1N1 story on deadline: a burning chest cough as I had never experienced as well as a fever, chills, muscle aches and a sudden weakness.

After the story was written, I lay down in bed for five days, wheezing, sweating and running a fever as high as 39 degrees Celsius. Did I catch H1N1? I was never tested, but the Info-Santé nurse I spoke to over the phone said that in all likelihood I did contract the pandemic flu strain.

I was fortunate not to have to go to the hospital, but I never could answer that nagging question: Was H1N1 a truly menacing pandemic strain or something merely akin to the typical flu?

aderfel@thegazette.canwest.com

News
Leader Swann taking heat for Liberals' tepid showing
Renata D'Aliesio, with files from Jason Fekete, Calgary Herald
Calgary Herald
583 words
23 December 2009
Calgary Herald
CALH
Final
A4
English
Copyright © 2009 Calgary Herald

When David Swann took over the reins of the Alberta Liberals last year, the party was rebuilding again.

Facing a rookie Conservative leader, the Grits expected to improve their standing in the March 2008 provincial election. Instead, the Tories won a stronger majority and the Liberals were reduced to nine seats in the legislature.

Fast-forward to today: The long-ruling Conservatives are sinking in public opinion polls but the Liberals have made only modest gains.

While the scenario has some Grit members and political observers contending the party and its new boss are showing momentum, it has others questioning Swann's leadership.

Longtime Calgary Grit organizer Donn Lovett and Mount Royal University political scientist Keith Brownsey point to the surge of the Wildrose Alliance, led by Danielle Smith, as evidence that Albertans are frustrated with the Tory government.

"Danielle Smith has assumed the role of official Opposition because nobody else is assuming that role," Lovett suggested.

Brownsey added: "I put the blame squarely on the leader.

"He's the anti-politician. He doesn't know how to be a politician."

In some respects, it was Swann's anti-politician persona that vaulted him to the helm of the Liberal party last December, defeating Calgary MLA Dave Taylor and former Edmonton MLA Mo Elsalhy.

A Calgary MLA since 2004, Swann was a medical officer of health and a family physician before entering politics. He's thoughtful and mild-mannered and was well known for promoting environmental and social democracy causes.

One of his first acts as leader was to limit Grit MLAs from heckling Conservative government members during question period because he wanted to raise the level of decorum and respect in the legislature.

Swann concedes he and his party have to do a better job of connecting with Albertans and of communicating what the Grits stand for.

He doesn't view the Wildrose rise as a death knell for the Liberals. Swann notes the new right-wing party has created a wedge among conservatives -- one that could benefit the Grits.

"There's a tremendous opportunity there as the Tories eat themselves from within and split the vote," he said. "It does open up the centre, the sensible centre, where we think Albertans mostly sit."

A year into the job, Swann said he feels he's coming into his own as party leader and his team is growing stronger.

Elsalhy agrees, but adds it's time for the Grits to seize the agenda.

"David is a lot stronger today and a lot more convincing. He's grown," Elsalhy said. "In 2010, they have to market him and the party's platform much more aggressively."

Grit deputy leader Laurie Blakeman argues criticism of Swann's leadership is premature.

"For someone coming from I think what many would call a kind of lefty point of view, he's worked very hard to understand the important issues in the Calgary energy sector, in the financial sector in Alberta," Blakeman said. She noted that media coverage of Swann and the Liberals increased during the fall legislature session, highlighting the Conservative government's troubled rollout of the H1N1 flu vaccination campaign.

The Liberal party's financial picture has strengthened, too. Party president Tony Sansotta said the Grits are on track to retire the party's debt in the spring.

City
Public flu clinics see holiday rush; More than half of residents have received H1N1 vaccine
Marta Samuel

23 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
C2
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

Crowds lined up behind a row of black curtains Tuesday afternoon to wait for their H1N1 and seasonal flu shots on the last day before clinics closed down for the holidays.

At the "roving" clinic set up at Ottawa City Hall on Monday, nurses saw about 800 people come in for their vaccinations and were anticipating the same or more for Tuesday, according to Maureen Lachance, supervisor of the immunization clinic.

"I suspect we are seeing (this many) people because it's the last day before Christmas," Lachance said. "It's been pretty constant, though. The numbers are there and people are interested."

More than half of Ottawa's residents have received H1N1 vaccinations, which surpasses the 40-per-cent target expected by Ottawa Public Health. Lachance said the immunizations have been well received and that the few people who have had bad reactions are usually those who have anxiety about getting the shot in the first place.

By mid-December, Ottawa Public Health had administered nearly 419,000 doses of the H1N1 vaccine since October. Compared to other cities, Ottawa appears to have a higher-than-average vaccination rate against swine flu.

Chris Stoney, waiting with his family at the City Hall clinic, said he hoped the line would move quickly. The family of four believe they all had the swine flu before the vaccinations were made available to the public and Tuesday was the first time they were all able to get the shots -- for H1N1 and the seasonal flu at the same time. But Stoney was uncertain about receiving both shots at once.

"I've heard mixed messages about getting the two shots and whether or not it is safe to get them both at once," Stoney said. He hoped there would not be any side-effects from the shots because he wanted his family to be healthy for the holidays.

Lachance says it is in fact safe to get the vaccinations at the same time, and she still encourages people to get them. "People will get very good coverage if they get both shots."

Richard Kalonji, on the other hand, waited until his children were out of school for the holidays before taking them to get their vaccinations. After waiting about half an hour, his four children had all received both H1N1 and seasonal flu immunizations. "I didn't want to have to take them out of school (if they had a bad reaction). Now if something does go wrong, I will be able to take care of them at home," Kalonji said.

While Tuesday was the last official day to receive the vaccinations at the clinics, Lachance says that come January, Ottawa residents will still have the chance to receive their immunizations. Clinics are still being booked, but, according to a news release from the city, they begin the afternoon of Jan. 6 at Sacred Heart High School, Jean-Robert Gauthier Catholic elementary school and Brookfield High School.

Overall, Lachance said the clinics have been running well. "I can't speak on behalf of all of the nurses but it's been a very good experience," she said. "I think all of them put their best foot forward in a massive exercise of collaborating. We are pleased and happy with the results."

Grieving father fears 'Evan effect' is fading; The death of 13-year-old Evan Frustaglio alerted Canadians to the threat of the H1N1 virus, but his father, Paul, fears we've stopped listening. Meagan Fitzpatrick reports.
Meagan Fitzpatrick
Canwest News Service
731 words
23 December 2009
Ottawa Citizen
OTCT
Final
A5
English
Copyright © 2009 Ottawa Citizen

The day after Christmas will mark two months since 13-year-old Evan Frustaglio died suddenly from the H1N1 flu virus and his story grabbed the nation's attention. Going to the grocery store without Evan is an emotional event, let alone facing the holidays, his father says.

"It is a debilitating heart-ache," Paul Frustaglio said in an interview as he described the waves of intense grief that come over him. "You can't walk, you can't breathe and it feels like your head is going to explode."

It was the morning of Monday, Oct. 26, when Frustaglio found his son on the bathroom floor of their Toronto-area home, gasping for air. The father picked up his son, who collapsed in his arms and fell unconscious. He never woke up. The paramedics arrived, but their efforts to revive the young hockey player were unsuccessful.

"They couldn't revive his heart," Frustaglio said. "They tried and tried. His little heart wouldn't start."

The Grade 8 student had fallen ill over the weekend and his mother brought him to a walk-in clinic on Sunday. He was sent home with instructions to take medication to treat his flu symptoms.

Less than 24 hours later, Evan's parents and younger brother were reeling from the shock that the H1N1 virus had claimed the teen's life.

"We're going down a really dark road in our life and we don't see any light, and we don't know when we will," Frustaglio said. "We were a strong family the four of us. So it's been difficult not having our other quarter of our whole there. That's the easiest way I can say it: A quarter of us is missing."

Evan Frustaglio was not the first child to die from the H1N1 flu, but his death was the most highly publicized of all its victims in Canada. He was a young and healthy athlete who died quickly and on the same day H1N1 vaccine clinics opened.

"I was not going to let my son's death be a number, there was just no way," Frustaglio said.

When Evan's story was told by media outlets across the country, it caused concern among some parents, who, until then, hadn't paid much attention to the H1N1 pandemic. Worried parents who earlier had little enthusiasm for immunizing their children took them to the jam-packed clinics and waited for hours.

Canada's health minister, Leona Aglukkaq, and chief public health officer Dr. David Butler-Jones were asked recently if they thought vaccine uptake would have been lower that first week of immunization programs if the Frustaglio's story wasn't so publicized.

"Absolutely," Aglukkaq said. "Totally," Butler-Jones said, also referring to what he said was the family's courage for sharing their story.

About 40 per cent of Canadians have been immunized against H1N1, and, while he didn't give precise numbers, Butler-Jones said he had heard from various jurisdictions that rates among children were higher than expected.

Evan Frustaglio would have been immunized had the shot been available before his death, his father says. He's encouraging Canadians to get the vaccine.

"I can't express how much pain I'm living through and if I can save one life or 20 or 100, then (Evan) didn't die in vain," Frustaglio said.

Frustaglio doesn't want Canadians to become complacent about the pandemic, but he feels the "Evan effect," as he calls it, is fading away.

"My son's face, that innocent boy, that was the Evan effect. It kicked everything in the butt to get it going," he said of the attention his son's death drew to H1N1 and the vaccine for it. The public and the media's attention, however, has since drifted away.

"It's just not a hot topic any more ... the problem is people are dying still every week," Frustaglio said. "Everybody should realize that it's real."

Colour Photo: Frustaglio Family Photo / When the otherwise healthy Evan Frustaglio died from H1N1, Canadian families flocked to get vaccinated.

Canada & World
Veterinarians find first case of H1N1 influenza in dog; Canine infection said to be a rare occurrence that does not present a danger to humans
Thomas H. Maugh Ii
Los Angeles Times
222 words
23 December 2009
Vancouver Sun
VNCS
Final
B8
English
Copyright © 2009 Vancouver Sun

Veterinarians in White Plains, N.Y., have identified the first known case of pandemic H1N1 influenza in a dog -- a 13-year-old mixed-breed male who is now recovering. The dog was tested because his owner previously had swine flu.

The virus has been found before in other pets, including at least three ferrets, several cats and pigs, and a cheetah named Gijima at a wildlife preserve in Santa Rosa, Calif.

A couple of the cats died, but most of the animals recovered.

In each case, the virus is thought to have been transmitted to the animal by its owner or handler, and there is no evidence of the virus being passed back to a human.

These incidents "are not a reason to be concerned," said Dr. Anne Schuchat, director of the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention's National Center for Immunization and Respiratory Diseases.

"A rare occurrence in other species is not a problem."

And if you are wondering, the symptoms of flu in pets are the same as they are in humans: fever, lethargy, runny nose, lack of appetite, coughing and possibly sneezing.

Columns
Pucker up... Santa's here - Airport travellers help spread holiday cheer, not germs
Doug Speirs / In the Doug House
835 words
23 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A2
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

I can't speak for Tiger Woods, but I know my wife, as a general rule of thumb, doesn't allow me to approach complete strangers in public places and ask them for hugs and kisses.

She's funny that way. Fortunately, my wife is a little more forgiving about me kissing strangers if it takes place during the Christmas season and I'm dressed as Santa Claus.

I should point out here I do not make a habit of putting on a red velvet suit and asking people to get up close and personal in exchange for candy canes.

But I agreed to give it a try after Free Press videographer Tyler Walsh sent me a news story stating that, according to British etiquette experts, even with fears of an H1N1 pandemic it's more hygienic to exchange kisses on the cheek under the mistletoe than to shake hands.

Tyler felt we should test this theory by having me dress up as everyone's favourite fat man and offer strangers a chance to (a) kiss Santa on the cheek; (b) give him a big bear hug; (c) shake his hand; or (d) bump fists or whatever bodily part they felt would best convey the true spirit of the holiday season.

We conducted this experiment at James Richardson International Airport because, at the most hectic travel time of the year, what sane person, while running for an airplane, does not want to take a few minutes to hug and/or kiss a middle-aged newspaper columnist who is sweating profusely under a rented Santa suit.

So there Santa was Monday afternoon, microphone in one hand, bag of candy canes in the other, stalking hectic travellers to explain why it was safe to plant a holiday smooch on his cheek.

Before confronting the general public, Santa warmed up on the nice folks in the human resources office at the Winnipeg Airports Authority.

Santa first explained all the options to Andrea Wolters, but she quickly rejected them. "Um, none of the above," is what she told Santa.

Then, seeing Santa's sad face, she chirped: "How about a pat on the back?" So Santa did not get off to a hot start, but things improved when he approached HR co-ordinator Taina Williams.

"I can shake hands because I have a bottle of hand sanitizer," Taina declared.

"Is there any way Santa's getting a kiss?"

Taina pondered this. "I can go for a hug," she said. So things were looking up.

It needs to be recorded here that Santa got his first hygienic kisses from HR manager Dianna Richardson and Christine Alongi, the airports authority's public relations director. In fact, Christine kissed both of Santa's rosy cheeks. "Well, you see, I'm French," she explained.

Santa then got a Tiger-style fist bump from Barry Rempel, CEO of the airports authority, who also gave Santa a swell tuque and scarf, which was nice.

His "kiss me or hug me" speech honed to perfection, Santa staggered into the bustling terminal in search of holiday affection from the travelling public.

Despite being a veteran elf, Santa got a little misty-eyed when he received spontaneous hugs from Lauren Hartley, 3, and Christa Hartley, 6. After Santa explained his mission to their mom, Lisa, she turned to the kids and asked: "What should we do? A hug or a kiss for Santa?"

With wide-eyed delight, they shrieked: "A KISS!"

"What will daddy say?" mom wondered.

"We won't tell dad," Santa promised. "It will be our secret."

With that, Lisa agreed "Santa deserves a kiss," and planted a welcome smooch on his sweaty cheeky. There isn't room to describe every dramatic encounter, but Santa would be remiss if he didn't point out that, after exchanging hugs and fist bumps with the Thomas family, Santa received a festive "come-hither" shout from a lovely woman named Bonnie, who was checking in for a holiday in Mexico. As hundreds of bemused travellers looked on, Bonnie was persuaded to plant a smooch in the middle of Santa's phoney beard.

"Can you tell the boys and girls why it's OK to kiss Santa?" I asked later.

"Because Santa doesn't have any germs!" Bonnie squealed in delight.

"Will you tell that to Mrs. Claus?" Santa begged. All in all, Santa made merry with about 40 frazzled travellers, pilots and airport staff, most of whom offered hugs and handshakes, but at least six of whom were willing to pucker up.

So Santa spread cheer. He spread mirth. But, hopefully, he didn't spread any germs. Before jumping into his sleigh, he wants to wish everyone a very merry Christmas.

And he has a special message for everyone who didn't give him a kiss: Remember, Santa knows when you're asleep!

doug.speirs@freepress.mb.ca

NDP pursue tuberculosis strategy - Vow to end suffering on First Nations
By Jen Skerritt
748 words
23 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A6
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

Two Manitoba MPs will push for a new tuberculosis strategy to eliminate what they call Canada's "national embarrassment."

Winnipeg North MP Wasylycia-Leis, Churchill MP Niki Ashton and the federal NDP's aboriginal affairs critic Jean Crowder have struck a committee to develop a national action plan to eliminate TB in First Nations communities. They plan to introduce their strategy as a private member's bill when Parliament resumes in late January, in addition to calling an emergency debate on the issue.

Wasylycia-Leis said the move is a reaction to a recent Free Press series that revealed some Manitoba First Nations have recorded some of the highest rates of TB in the world since the mid-1970s. Some northern Manitoba communities have recorded more than 600 cases of TB per 100,000 -- more than 100 times what their rate should be.

By comparison, Canada's national rate is five cases per 100,000. In developing countries such as Bangladesh, the rate is slightly below 400 cases per 100,000.

Wasylycia-Leis said she wants to see the Harper government make TB a top priority and work with aboriginal communities to end a curable scourge.

"I think the federal government can play a key role by saying this is a national priority," she said. "(It's) a national embarrassment. Here we are heading into 2010 and a curable disease is taking a toll on our First Nations communities."

A spokeswoman said federal Health Minister Leona Aglukkaq was unavailable for comment.

TB is an infectious disease that experts say is a symptom of poverty, and a byproduct of overcrowded homes, malnutrition and poor overall health. The airborne disease is rampant in many northern Manitoba reserves where more than 10 people are crammed into a small house and access to clean running water and affordable healthy food is limited.

Manitoba Keewatinowi Okimakanak (MKO) Grand Chief David Harper said he thinks developing a national strategy is a great idea. It should include better housing, access to running water and infrastructure improvements, he added.

While a major TB outbreak in Harper's home community of Garden Hill in 2006 did little to prompt a response from the federal government, he is optimistic that things will be different in the wake of the H1N1 crisis.

The same deadly mix that let H1N1 flourish in remote aboriginal communities last spring has allowed TB to persist on Manitoba reserves for a century.

Half of the 500 overcrowded houses in Garden Hill aren't hooked up to the main water line, making handwashing -- one of the basic keys to prevent the spread of disease -- more difficult.

"This is really a good time to bring about that kind of change," Harper said. "This is one of the issues we'd want to be 100 per cent involved in."

Ashton said better housing and increased support for healthy food initiatives will be included in the new strategy.

She said the federal government needs to take a targeted approach to address the living conditions and the root causes of TB in order to stop the spread.

"The fact that Canada in 2009 has tuberculosis rates on par with a Third World country is shameful," Ashton said.

Manitoba Health Minister Theresa Oswald called the idea of a national strategy "hugely encouraging" and said she'd like to see more work done to address the health disparities between aboriginals and non-aboriginals. She said the source of much of Manitoba's TB problem stems from poor, overcrowded housing in some communities, and poor health.

She said Ottawa has spent more on health recently. She hopes it devotes more resources to close the gaps. "I will be cautiously optimistic," she said.

jen.skerritt@freepress.mb.ca

What is TB?

TB spreads like the common cold. A person can become infected with TB if they inhale airborne droplets from an infected person who is coughing, sneezing, spitting or even talking. TB germs usually spread through prolonged, close contact with an infected person.

TB can be cured with nine months of drug treatment. If TB is left untreated, a person with active disease will infect an average of 10 to 15 people a year.

Visit the interactive TB website at winnipegfreepress.com/tb to read the Free Press' six-part TB series and watch videos, see slide shows and scroll through 100 years of TB history in Manitoba.

1most important works of the decade No. 8: 'I Dreamed a Dream'
John Sakamoto
Toronto Star
579 words
23 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
E01
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

We asked our critics and our readers to vote for the 10 most important works of the decade. We count down to No. 1 as we approach Dec. 31.

"I'm not saying Susan Boyle caused swine flu," one Sion Simon joked via that most brutally efficient of social media outlets, Twitter. "I am just saying that nobody had swine flu, she sang on TV, people got swine flu."

Simon happens to be a British Labour MP, a vocation that perhaps partially accounts for his tone deafness to the nuances of a well-told joke. At the time - late April, a couple of weeks after Boyle's galvanizing appearance on the U.K. reality show Britain's Got Talent - he also held the post of minister for further education, which seems entirely appropriate, since he was about to get one.

"Earlier I repeated a joke that was in poor taste, which I now regret," he grovelled a couple of days later, again on Twitter. "I apologise (sic) wholeheartedly for any distress or embarrassment caused."

The only distress or embarrassment it likely caused was to the MP himself, who had to fend off the vitriolic reaction of passionate music fans (though not, tellingly, of voters outraged that he would kid about a disease that has claimed thousands of lives).

Which brings us to that now-celebrated seven-minute clip that has been viewed online more than 200 million times.

It is, above all, a potent demonstration of the power of confluence: old and new media, reality and verisimilitude, lifelong anonymity and instant fame.

Forget the cultural resonance of American Idol. A television show that most of what is now Boyle's constituency could not even view in its original airing has managed to telescope the years-long arc of a musical career into the time it takes to down a cup of coffee.

It renders geographical boundaries irrelevant, simultaneously reinforces the importance of talent and consigns it to a supporting role, and upends the deeply ingrained requirement that our pop stars adhere to an excruciatingly circumscribed notion of acceptable appearance.

On the downside, it also virtually guarantees that anyone who finds fame in this manner has peaked upon arrival.

After all, Boyle's irresistible narrative - an ungainly, unemployed, small-town wallflower comes out of nowhere and takes the world by storm - assisted as it was by masterful editing (check out the skilful medley of crowd and judges' reaction shots) can, by definition, happen only once in a career. Even a Disney teen star like Miley Cyrus is bound to experience a more dynamic professional trajectory.

One final thing: watch the end of Boyle's performance. As soon as she's finished belting out the song's last, crowning note, she promptly turns on her heel and begins strolling, obliviously andapparently contentedly toward the wings.

Granted, her oddball actions may simply have been a case of her nerves getting the better of her. Then again, there's another, considerably more appealing inference: that she'd succeeded on her own terms, accomplished what she set out to do, and didn't feel the slightest compunction to stick around for the validation of a bunch of obscenely compensated, obsessively coiffed, self-appointed arbiters of popular taste.

The joke, we'd like to think, is on them.

GTA
Identity theft feared as data lost; Durham Region's loss of flu clinic information on 83,524 people called 'disturbing'
Carola Vyhnak
Toronto Star
587 words
23 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
GT04
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

Tens of thousands of people who attended flu clinics in Durham Region may be at risk of identity theft following the disappearance of a USB key containing their personal information.

Health card numbers included in the data could allow thieves to gather more information needed to use someone else's identity, said Tamir Israel, staff lawyer for the Canadian Internet Policy and Public Interest Clinic at the University of Ottawa.

"The risk for identity theft with this information is very high," he said on Tuesday after Durham's health department issued a news release announcing the loss of the portable storage device.

The key contained data for 83,524 people who attended the department's H1N1 and seasonal flu vaccination clinics between Oct. 23 and Dec. 15. It was used to store information from eight clinics being taken to the computer server at regional headquarters at Rossland Rd. E. and Garden St. in Whitby.

A health department nurse dropped it on Dec. 16 between the building and parking garage, said Dr. Robert Kyle, commissioner and medical officer of health for the region. "We've spent many days mobilizing quite a number of staff to search high and low for this thing, and come up dry. It's extremely regrettable and unfortunate. Any time there's a privacy breach, people should be concerned."

The information in the "huge file" was not encrypted, but to read it you would need to know which file to open and what application to use, Kyle said. Once open, you'd see "meaningless symbols" among which names, addresses, phone numbers and health card numbers could be deciphered.

Other personal health details that patients provided at the clinics could not be read, he said.

While the device's loss was reported immediately and a surveillance camera showed someone picking it up and putting it on a rock, searchers failed to find it later, Kyle said.

Ajax resident Brian Johnson, whose wife and 3-year-old son received flu shots at clinics in Pickering, was appalled at what he called a lack of care and organization.

"I'm disturbed on a number of levels. Why is such sensitive data on a device that could be so easily lost?"

Johnson, a management consultant, found it "pretty damned disturbing" that he learned about the loss through the media and not the municipality. He was concerned about personal data such as his health card number and "anything that might compromise my identity" being released.

Israel agreed personal information should be encrypted to prevent identity theft, which is "growing phenomenally," he said. "It depends on whose hands it ends up in. There's some luck involved."

Worried about the public's loss of confidence in Durham's health department, Kyle put the loss down to "human error." Using the device to transfer data to a server is a common procedure the department has followed for years, he said.

The incident comes as the province is in the midst of a problem-plagued attempt to computerize all medical records across Ontario.

The province's information and privacy commissioner is investigating and the department is reviewing its practices, he said, adding they hope to send letters this week to everyone affected.

Anyone with concerns or questions is advised to contact Durham Health Connection Line at 905-666-6241 or 1-800-841-2729.

Ontario's privacy commissioner has launched an investigation after a USB drive containing the personal health information of more than 80,000 people who went to flu clinics in Durham Region -- just north of Toronto -- went missing.

News
Volunteers vital to clinic
Jodi Gillich
The Leader-Post
660 words
23 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
A5
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

We might not notice them, but they're always there. They greet us, guide us into the queue, direct us to the washroom, listen to our concerns and chat with us.

They amount to a huge workforce that is never paid.

"Without the volunteers, this wouldn't work," says Harry Secuur, a 76 year old volunteer at the H1N1 Mass Immunization Clinic located in the former Robert Usher Collegiate.

Pam Larsen, public health nurse consultant with Population and Health Services -- who co-ordinates the volunteers -- agrees.

"We couldn't run without them," she says, with a grateful smile to Secuur and Jan Ayer, another volunteer.

Both Secuur and Ayer have been there since the beginning -- late October -- when the clinic opened its doors to health-care workers.

At the beginning of December, volunteers had worked 684 shifts -- each typically lasting four hours -- at the Usher clinic and the clinic in Mosaic Stadium's Green and White Room, says Larsen.

Retirees Secuur and Ayer plan to volunteer at the clinic until the doors close for good. They say they enjoy volunteering because it helps them to get out and interact with people.

Secuur -- retired for 11 years -- has even submitted his name for next fall's seasonal flu clinic.

"What do I do besides sitting at home, watching the television set or being behind a computer? I like to do this," says Secuur. "I think when you're retired, you get sort of into a routine -- you do this, you do that -- and this gives me a break."

"I did some of the morning shifts, which start at 8:30, and I'm like 'OK, I'm getting up, taking a shower. It's just like going to work!' This is so good!" says Ayer, who retired three years ago.

"You get to get out and meet people. It's surprising -- being in Regina -- I see people I know."

Secuur usually starts his shift at 12:30 p.m., but always comes in at noon to make sure he gets his favourite job -- guiding people waiting in the queue to the next available nurse.

"I'm sort of the host, because I bring them to the tables," says Secuur, with a laugh.

It's Ayer's favourite job, too, because she gets a chance to talk with people standing in line.

That helps them relax and keep their mind off what's going to happen, says Secuur -- "although it's not a big thing, but to some people it is," he adds.

Secuur has reached the point where he can tell whether a person is comfortable at the clinic or not. If someone seems really anxious, he asks the head nurse to watch them in case they pass out.

"You can't just walk up to a person (and say) 'Go over there.' I don't think that's the right thing to do. So I try to make it as pleasant as possible," says Secuur.

Ayer constantly receives compliments about how organized the clinic is.

Without the volunteers, Larsen says, the clinic would not be so organized or even operational.

"I feel very, very lucky that we've had such a large group, and people have been very generous with their time," says Larsen.

She adds she wants to say "thank you, thank you, thank you" to all the volunteers -- who often don't get breaks, if the clinic is busy -- as well as health services and community organizations that provide volunteers.

On Dec. 4, health minister Don McMorris acknowledged the efforts of volunteers.

"This is the biggest immunization that Canada has ever seen -- that Saskatchewan has ever seen -- and I think it takes a lot of hands on deck, but it's worked out very well," says McMorris.

Unneeded diabetes tests could be cut: new study

A new study says Ontario could save tens and even hundreds of millions of dollars by cutting back on the number of unnecessary glucose tests people with Type 2 diabetes perform weekly.

The study authors, including a McMaster University researcher in family medicine, suggest the amount the province will have to spend on glucose test strips over the next five years could be cut by between $26 million and $300 million, depending on how often people are told to test their levels.

The changes, if implemented, would not apply to people with Type 1 diabetes or people who control Type 2 diabetes with insulin.

Tara Gomes, author of the paper appearing in the Canadian Medical Association Journal, says studies have shown the multiple-times-a-day glucose testing recommended for people with Type 2 diabetes doesn't actually improve their glucose control and can trigger anxiety and depression.

She says cutting the number of tests back to twice a week or something in that range would not increase the risk to the individuals but would save the Ontario government large sums.

The authors, including Mac's Mike Paterson, say if the policy on testing doesn't change, Ontario will spend $500 million in the next five years paying for glucose test strips for people 65 and older.

Light box, exercise beat the Christmas blues

The holiday season is here, and for some that means additional emotional stress and depression.

But there are ways to cope.

Listen to Dr. Laura Miller, the director of women's mental health in the psychiatry department at Brigham and Women's Hospital, Boston.

If seasonal depression worsens, Miller suggests a special therapeutic light box might help in the morning. Exercise can also make you feel better.

If you're busy and stressed out, she suggests making time for activities such as walking, meditating, exercising and hanging out with friends.

Also consider buying prepared foods instead of trying to make everything yourself.

Poll shows many think swine flu risk overblown

A poll suggests Canadians are generally satisfied by the way governments handled the H1N1 outbreak.

The Canadian Press Harris- Decima survey also suggests Canadians think the risk of swine flu was exaggerated.

Now many clinics have closed and the rate of new infections seems to be dropping.

That appears to be reflected in a receding level of concern among those surveyed.

Plastic sex toys spark fears over chemicals

Concern about products containing bisphenol A and phthalates is spreading.

Managers at some Edmonton stores that sell plastic adult sex toys say they have already removed items containing the controversial chemical.

Toronto MP Carolyn Bennett is also calling on the federal health minister to regulate the industry.

Bisphenol A and phthalates are used to make plastic toys soft and flexible. Studies have shown the chemicals can cause hormonal complications at a certain level of exposure.

More nurses working, total hits 341,431

A new study finds that the number of nurses working in Canada rose by 8.3 per cent between 2004 and last year.

The report by the Canadian Institute for Health Information says the number of employed nurses reached 341,431.

It indicates the rate of growth for registered nurses and licensed practical nurses exceeded the rate of population growth over the past five years.

An official with the Canadian Nurses Association says they're encouraged to see the rise in the number of RNs.

One positive development is an increase in community health nurses and a rise in those working in telehealth.
