Comment
How deep will cuts be and other queries
Dave Obee
Times Colonist
795 words
22 December 2009
Victoria Times Colonist
VTC
Final
A12
English
Copyright © 2009 Victoria Times Colonist

Questions, questions, I have questions. Most of them are aimed at the provincial government, so I don't expect answers. The government's Orwellian definition of its "open, honest" commitment doesn't allow that kind of thing.

- If British Columbia is the Best Place on Earth, as the government keeps repeating, why do so many politicians head to Hawaii for their vacations?

Why would they settle for what they believe to be second-best? Have they never seen Fernie in winter? Or Smithers? No beachfront bars, I admit, but certainly pretty with all that snow and with great skiing just up the hill.

If this is the Best Place on Earth, why are our leaders in such a rush to get out?

- Where will the government cuts come? The September budget update was nasty, but it carried the promise that much worse was to come in the next budget on March 2, 2010.

Arts, sports and community groups are likely to suffer more than they already have. Looking for more help for abused women or kids? That's not a priority, so forget it.

At a certain point, though, cuts to discretionary grants will not satisfy the bean counters. If the goal is to govern by the numbers rather than the needs, you have to look at cutting employees.

To have a real impact on the next fiscal year, which starts on April 1, staff cuts will have to start early in the new year. So, in January, how many layoff notices will we see?

- When will the government make a serious effort to sell us on the merits of the harmonized sales tax? Its efforts to date have been abysmal -- "You are going to drink that medicine, kid, and you are going to like it."

Telling us that the tax will be good for the economy isn't enough, especially when the tax increase is clear and lower costs are theoretical. What will the tax mean to individuals? Why can't we have some examples of prices that are likely to go down when the tax arrives on July 1?

I can understand that the government is charging ahead and ignoring anyone with concerns. But we have yet to see a credible message and that is a management failure. Has the government already laid off the people who would know how to sell this stuff to the public?

- Will we have labour peace in 2010? Hmm. A bunch of government contracts are expiring just after the Olympics. The government says it won't consider higher salaries.

Yet raises have been given to registered nurses and they are being given by local governments. Beyond that, there is a perception that the Liberals are governing for the rich and not the rest of us. That leaves plenty of room for jealousy, bitterness and determination to get a fair share. Really think the unions will go quietly?

- How should employers balance H1N1 and sick time? Times are tight, so supervisors are being told to ensure nobody is slacking off. We are also being told that if there is the slightest risk of H1N1, we should stay away so we don't infect everyone else.

Can't have it both ways. Which would you prefer?

- Why do so many people still believe that a government has to be run like a business? Setting aside the obvious -- that many businesses are run badly -- only a fool would fail to see that there are fundamental differences. A business is supposed to make a profit, a government is supposed to set good public policy and provide important services.

Yes, government can learn from business. But does that mean it has to be run that way?

- If money is such a concern within government, why have ministries not embraced open-source software? Why do they continue to pay for expensive commercial licences when they could get the open-source equivalent for free? The government in the United Kingdom has gone the free route, as has the city of Vancouver. Are they smarter than the province?

How many arts groups will lose their funding so the government can keep paying Bill Gates? How many employees will lose their jobs? Who makes these decisions, anyway?

- Once the decision was made to replace career bureaucrats with people with backgrounds in the corporate world, should we have been surprised that the government's values changed dramatically?

Sure, it's nice that things can be measured on a spreadsheet. But what about the intangibles, like issues, sustainability, the future, viability and, um, people?

- In 2009, what have we done to make this a better place?

Just wondering.

dobee@tc.canwest.com

News
Health region expecting large deficit
Pamela Cowan
Leader-Post
400 words
22 December 2009
Regina Leader Post
RLP
Final
A10
English
Copyright © 2009 Regina Leader Post

The Sunrise Health Region is projecting a larger-than-anticipated deficit this year.

"We budgeted for $179 million in expenditures this year, but we're currently forecasting expenditures of $181 million," said Sharon Tropin, the region's director of communications.

The region had budgeted for a year-end $1.5-million deficit, but H1N1-related expenditures contributed an additional $500,000 to the bottom line.

"The H1N1 costs may be offset by the province, but we're still awaiting notification," Tropin said.

The region's H1N1-related costs cover supplies, additional staffing, advertising and rental space for some of the immunization clinics.

"We used a combination of donated space, health region facilities and schools, as well as some rental space," Tropin said.

Adding to the health region's financial challenges are the province's belt-tightening measures.

In response to lower-than-expected potash revenue, the provincial government announced $8.1 million in program reductions, including $5 million trimmed from the health regions' non-clinical programs.

The Sunrise Health Region's portion of the $5-million cuts translated to a base funding reduction of $305,000 for 2009-10. To cope with the reduction, the region will not be filling a variety of vacant positions, Tropin said.

"The vacant positions are in the areas of administration, the autism program and nurse practitioner services," she said. "No positions are being cut. The positions were either filled later in the year than budgeted or are not yet filled as some are for difficult-to-recruit positions."

Until the region can fill the positions, it has adjusted existing resources to continue these program areas.

"The region will continue to actively recruit for these positions," Tropin said.

The Regina Qu'Appelle Health Region's portion of the $5-million program reductions is $1.358 million, said Mark Torjusen, spokesman for the RQHR.

"The region will meet our requirement by not filling vacant administration positions and as a result of being unable to fill hard-to-recruit clinical positions, which accounts for $1.2 million," Torjusen said. "Another $200,000 in savings will be through restraint on travel and restraints in other areas, such as supplies."

That said, the RQHR isn't feeling the same financial pinch as the Sunrise Health Region.

"We're still working towards a balanced budget for this year," Torjusen said.

Personal Finance
Extreme change both challenge, opportunity; Economic recovery will see a number of shifts in society: speakers
Ray Turchansky
For Canwest News Service
935 words
21 December 2009
Saskatoon Star Phoenix
SSP
Final
C13
English
Copyright © 2009 Saskatoon Star Phoenix

The common thread among the half-dozen speakers at the recent Economics Society of Northern Alberta conference on recovery was that change is at hand in almost every aspect of our lives.

It may range from farmers raising bison and sheep as significant revenue sources, to cuts in government spending and increases in consumption taxes, to new technologies causing us to embrace solar and nuclear energy plus natural gas as primary power sources.

"We are in a period of extreme change, and it's not going away," said Peter Tertzakian, chief economist of ARC Financial, and author of books A Thousand Barrels a Second and The End of Energy Obesity. "There's always opportunity in change."

Therein lies the warning, and challenge, for investors. Those who seek and recognize future changes in how we live and work can prosper from them.

Tertzakian showed how energy conservation has evolved from an automatic candle snuffer that put out the flame after a candle burned down a certain length, to a box by Cisco Systems that continually adjusts energy needs for lighting, heating and powering machines throughout a building, cutting electricity consumption by 10 to 15 per cent.

During that stretch of history, we used ice blocks for refrigeration until machines were developed to do the job. We burned whale oil for lighting, and when whale stocks depleted we moved the processing "factories" from land onto the whaling ships to reduce oil deterioration. And we evolved from heating with coal gas to the use of stranded "nature's gas" when the innovation of pipeline welding allowed it to be transported great distances.

"The technologies that change the game are going to come from outside the energy complex, much as welding affected the fortunes of coal," said Tertzakian. "Google, Cisco and IBM are largely in the business of saving energy."

He noted that it was correctly predicted in 1966 that within five years more than 10 million colour TV sets would be sold annually, but there have been fewer than a half million hybrid cars sold in a single year since the Toyota Prius emerged in 1997.

"If (Apple founder) Steve Jobs was running that, he would have had some people fired."

Tertzakian said the problem is that two-thirds of all barrels of oil produced go to transportation, and it isn't until "around $85 a barrel when you start to get substitution, you start to get behavioural change, when people seek alternatives.

"And the free market is the most powerful thing to sort things out, within boundaries. The role of government is to set the boundaries. The most powerful changes have nothing to do with government incentives."

That dovetails with the message from Stewart Hall, an economist with HSBC Securities Canada.

"We need to hand off the public sector spending to the private sector at some point in time," said Hall.

He said there are huge profits to be made from other Canadian resources.

"India is set to import rice for the first time in a long period of time (20 years)," said Hall. "There are areas that are not water-rich, and I'm not talking lakes and rivers, I'm talking water in the ground, aquifers.

"Canada is very well-positioned to take advantage of this, because you can set up a virtual trade of water by exporting grain and rices to areas that are water-deficient. Some argue that the next Microsoft is going to be agricultural engineering."

Don Brown, the director of economics and competitiveness with Alberta Agriculture, noted that the amount of total Canadian household spending that is on food and nonalcoholic beverages has dropped from 26 per cent in 1945 to 8.8 per cent currently.

He said Canada's share of world wheat trade is declining, beef is facing "one calamity after another," pork is suffering from high feed costs and the H1N1 flu, while Canadian consumption of poultry isn't rising as fast as in the United States.

The good news is that there's a growing demand for bison, as well as sheep breeding stock and market lambs.

"Never waste a crisis, never waste a disaster," Brown said. "That is a key requirement for attitude change."

Indeed, there is opportunity in diversification.

"We are limiting ourselves if we don't think of our provincial economy as something more than agriculture and conventional energy," said Todd Hirsch, senior economist with Alberta's ATB Financial.

"China's growth rate slipped from 12 per cent to six per cent in 2009; we did not lose China. It was a similar thing in India. A very interesting theme has developed, and that is the recovery is being led by developing nations, and we haven't really seen this in other global downturns. This time the U.S. is going to lag, and there's going to be a new leadership by China and India, and other developing economies, Indonesia and South Korea. They will lead the global recovery in 2010."

The legacy of this Great Recession, as some are calling it, will be change.

(EDMONTON JOURNAL)

Canada's water supply be possibly sold to countries with a limited supply

Sunday Reader
Great right hope; The Wildrose Alliance Party sees a future premier in Danielle Smith. That's a tall order
Sheila Pratt
Edmonton Journal
3104 words
20 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
E1
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

On a cool spring day in 2008, a curious Danielle Smith headed to a downtown restaurant in Calgary for a meeting with John Murdoch, an oilpatch veteran who runs a small oil-and-gas firm. Over lunch at the Wild Ginger, the two discussed the provincial election of a few weeks earlier, in which the Stelmach Tories took a massive 72-seat majority and defeated the only Wildrose MLA, but suffered losses to the Liberals in Calgary.

Smith had intended to run for the Tories in that March 2 vote in Calgary North Hill. She reminded Murdoch that she intended to do exactly that in 2012. Ambitious, articulate, and an up-and-comer in conservative circles, Smith was ready to go to the big show after working for a decade as a champion of conservative causes.

Murdoch had a different idea. The Stelmach Tories were in trouble, especially in downtown Calgary, he argued. The Wildrose Alliance had raised a stunning $513,000 in that 28-day campaign, almost as much as the Tories.

It was time for a change, said Murdoch, and Danielle Smith could be the next premier with a renewed party from the right.

It wasn't an instant sell. It took several lunches over many months before Smith was ready to make the biggest decision of her life. In late 2008, she rolled the dice. She'd take on the 40-

year Tory dynasty rather than work within it -- a mission decidedly not for the faint

of heart. "It was a long process to leave the Tory party," she explains in a recent interview in Edmonton. "But I realized what would happen if there was not a strong Wildrose party. Next time, Ed Stelmach would get 60 to 70 seats and pat himself on the back and carry on. So if we can offer a strong opposition, that would be a good outcome."

The political right has bigger ambitions for Danielle Smith than just a stronger opposition. Young, articulate and urban, she dispels the party's image as a rural rump of grumpy ideologues and social conservatives. She's from Calgary where the anger at Stelmach is still simmering, anger that gave the Wildrose its big break this fall -- an upset victory in the Calgary Glenmore byelection.

"She could be the first premier schooled in the neo-conservative school," says an excited Link Byfield, former publisher of Alberta Report, a now defunct weekly right-of-centre news magazine.

"She is also a real democrat and believes in the common sense of the common person," he says. "It's been 40 years (of Tory rule), and it's been too long."

It was only last year, in 2008, that Danielle Smith was running the six-person, local office of the Canadian Federal of Independent Business. The question now is whether she has the depth, political skills and acumen to be a giant killer. It's no small task to parlay a party with local organizations in only 40 of 83 constituencies and about seven per cent of the popular vote in 2008 into a government-in-waiting. But the Wildrose has cash in hand and another trump card at this point -- a wave of unrelenting frustration with the Stelmach government.

In many ways, Smith's grooming for the Wildrose leadership has been underway since her university days. After Prime Minister Stephen Harper, she's the second, proud graduate of the "Calgary school" -- a group of economists and political scientists at the University of Calgary devoted to free markets, small government, privatization, low taxes, the oil-and-gas sector and doubts about global warming.

Smith counts longtime Harper adviser Tom Flanagan as a major influence. Flanagan helped get her into Vancouver's Fraser Institute, a right-wing think-tank, in 1996. There she met her heroes, former British prime minister Margaret Thatcher and Michael Walker, then the institute's executive director. She studied under U of C economist Fred Atkins, Harper's thesis adviser, who describes himself as on the far right on economic policy.

There are of course many politicians from the "Calgary school" inside the famous Tory big tent; there always have been. Sustainable Resources Minister Ted Morton, also one of Smith's key mentors, comes to mind.

But, says Smith, Stelmach made two wrong moves that persuaded her it was time to leave.

With a ready smile and brimming with confidence, she plunks down a big handbag full of books and papers and settles in for an interview in a south-side Edmonton eatery. For the next 90 minutes, she talks almost nonstop about issues near and dear to her -- the plight of small oil companies, the new royalty rates ( "excessive and punitive"), decentralized democracy ( "great faith in local school boards"), carbon sequestration ( "sounds like a boondoggle"), Stelmach's spending ( "out of control"), and plans for new electricity-transmission lines ( "digging an irrigation canal to water the backyard") and converting cars to run on natural gas.

A quick study, Smith, 38, is a bit of a policy wonk. Quite a bit, actually. She absorbs facts and figures with ease and relays them with a conservative edge that astutely avoids an overtly ideological hue.

She's brims with ideas about energy conservation, new technology, and a new natural gas strategy, after reading The End of Energy Obesity, a look at the future by Calgary energy economist Peter Tertzakian.

Engaging and friendly, Smith also has a dogged side. She doesn't like to give up an argument.

So what were the Two Big Mistakes that persuaded her to leave the usual safety of Calgary's Tory club, with its almost certain path to power and influence?

First, the premier raised royalty rates. Then he raised spending 17 per cent in his first budget in April 2008.

"I was shocked when he raised spending like that," she says. "I was willing to give him the benefit of the doubt."

Smith firmly rejects the notion that Albertans were not getting their fair share of natural resource revenue before Stelmach's royalty review, which was released in October 2007.

"I don't hear people saying, 'I want my fair share.' I hear farmers complaining about not getting land access fees. Everyone is impacted. We were sold a bill of goods (in the royalty review). It was a fundamental mistake and we have to fix it."

"Punitive" royalty rates must go down, especially for junior oil and gas companies, among her biggest supporters. "They live and work in this community, and it's really a tragedy that this sector was hardest hit.

"You need to have incentives for drilling, and that's profit.

Smith is heavy on economic policy. The province's future lies in a vigorous oil-and-gas sector and a vigorous agriculture sector. Staking a future on nanotechnology or the knowledge economy is "silly" -- except when new technology can help the oil and gas industry, she says. On global warming, she's a skeptic, sort of, but says an energy-efficiency strategy is just basically a good idea.

On social policy, individuals are responsible for themselves. Government has a limited role.

"Individuals and non-profit groups are the first line of support for underprivileged Albertans," Smith says.

"We need to do more to ensure private charities and non-profits have the resources they need to continue to do their good work. Government's role is to make sure services are delivered, but it is not necessarily their job to deliver them."

Health care? The money follows the patient, like school vouchers. (As an editorial writer, she supported Klein's third-way, private-parallel system.)

Environment policy? Here Smith is vague, calling for "sustainable oil development" and new technology.

Smith was born in Calgary in 1971, the year Peter Lougheed's Tories chased the Social Credit dynasty from power. She cut her teeth politically in Preston Manning's Reform movement in the 1990s and worked for former MP Lee Richardson in Calgary. She also supported Rick Orman's bid in 1992 for the leadership of the provincial Tories, the race that Klein won.

In 1997, she returned from the Fraser Institute to set up the (now defunct) Canadian Property Rights Research Institute, an advocacy group for ranchers, farmers and other rural land owners -- key elements of the old and limited base of the Social Credit party. Later, in 2006, she helped launched the Alberta Property Rights Initiative, an organization with a similar cause.

But Albertas's right wing, it seems, is trying to reinvent itself these days. Smith leads a party that aims to be less blatantly ideological, less shrill and "more sensible" to the average voter.

In the 1990s, government was the problem and the less of it, the better. The mantra of the right was, "let the market prevail."

Now, says Byfield, taxpayers deserve "smart government" or "high-performance government" and "practical free enterprise." In fact, the new right isn't even necessarily anti-union, he says.

"Most people don't see government as the problem, but it's bad value for money," Byfield says. "The question is, what do you want to get done?

"Danielle is quite firm on this stuff and stays right on track without being inappropriately ideological about it."

As for social conservatism, that too is getting a revise. Social conservatives should not necessarily look to the state for moral underpinning for their causes, Byfield says. That only increases the power of the state over individuals, he says.

Smith, the second of five children, grew up in a boom-and-bust economy. In the 1980s, her

parents lost a home when oil prices collapsed. Her dad worked in a warehouse for years before getting into the oilpatch. She doesn't dwell on personal history.

After an English degree at the U of C, Smith went to Vancouver for a year to figure out her life. She worked in a restaurant and ran the local office of Encyclopaedia Britannica. She came back to Alberta to do an economics degree. In 1998, she ran for the Calgary public school board as a champion of charter schools. But that political venture was short-lived. The board was so politically divided it became dysfunctional, and education minister Lyle Oberg shut it down after 10 months.

In 1999, Smith went to work for the Calgary Herald editorial board, and became a television and talk-radio celebrity. She left journalism in 2006 to work for the CFIB. She's divorced and remarried.

Her big break came in September 2006, when Link Byfield asked her to chair the Calgary Congress, a forum of conservative thinkers, supported by the Manning Institute, that was looking to renew federalism and promote an elected Senate.

That's where she caught attention of John Murdoch.

"She's articulate and intelligent and she erased a lot of unfair branding of the party" as angry Albertans and social conservatives, he says. "She's a fiscal conservative and social moderate and that's what Albertans want."

That tension between the social conservatives and libertarians -- Smith considers herself part of the latter group -- is one of her biggest challenges. So far she mostly refuses to discuss "issues that divide," such as gay rights and abortion.

Murdoch himself is a disillusioned Tory. Originally a Klein supporter, he was briefly elected to the Calgary regional health board until Klein shut down that exercise in local democracy. In 2004, the day he got the phone call firing the elected board, he went to the founding convention of the Alliance Party, looking, he says, for a party that took democracy seriously, including referendum and recall.

There's plenty of upset at Calgary Tories in the oilpatch, especially among smaller oil producers, he adds, charging that new royalty rates drove investment to Saskatchewan.

"This place runs on Other People's Money and I think Stelmach has lost sight of that," Murdoch says.

"Most of us are working stiffs trying to put away money for retirement," says Murdoch, whose company consists these days of three people and wells producing 50 to 60 barrels a day.

Being "on-side" with the oilpatch is critical to the Wildrose Alliance or anyone who wants to lead Alberta because that's where Alberta's wealth comes from, says U of C economist Frank Atkins, also one of Smith's mentors.

Stelmach's biggest mistake was alienating the oilpatch, says Atkins.

"Stelmach did not understand the need to keep oil and gas on-side. If you anger oil and gas, you suffer economic wrath," he says.

While in principle he's not against raising royalty rates, Atkin says there's no point transferring private-sector wealth to the public sector if government just continues to spend wastefully.

Atkins describes himself as being on the far right on economic policy. He advised Harper last fall against getting into a stimulus package when the recession hit, and says there's little you can do about an overheated economy as well.

"If you're going to let the market work, you will have winners and losers, and you just have to put up with it," he says. "People just have to adjust. "

He's certain Smith shares that view.

Calgary political analyst Dave Taras agrees there is a "lot of anger in downtown Calgary looking for a place to go." The city is still simmering from the rise in royalty rates, the loss of Calgary's candidate in the Tory leadership race, the mishandling of the H1N1 flu vaccine.

Taras wonders if Smith is "mainly a Calgary play," though there's plenty of unhappiness in Edmonton, too, a recent poll indicates. In both cities, the Tories slipped to third place in an early December Angus Reid poll and the Wildrose is ahead of the Liberals.

In many ways, the oilpatch took over the empty shell of the Wildrose party as a vehicle, says Taras, who teaches communications at the University of Calgary.

"She fits an image of change," he says. "She's very poised and has an excellent grasp of policy."

But Smith has to take Wildrose from a protest movement to a government-in-waiting and that won't be easy. The battleground with Stelmach will be over credibility and competence, he says.

People now seem to be deciding that Stelmach is a "nice guy but out of his depth," says Taras. That's the conclusion he draws from the December poll.

While there's plenty of buzz around Smith, she has yet to pass the credibility test herself. That will depend to a large extent on the team she attracts and whether she gets outside the narrow, right-wing circles of old Socreds and social conservatives.

"So the next move is critical: Does she get credible people around her?" says Taras. "Watch to see over the next six months if ambitious people move to her."

So far, she's got Dave Yager, a veteran oilpatch warrior, Link Byfield, a longtime advocate on the right, and David Gray, former executive director of the Alberta Utilities Consumer Advocate

Longtime Edmonton Tory Ken Chapman says change is in the air. But the Wildrose represents a return to "old school politics" at a time when disengaged, frustrated voters and non-voters are looking for something new, an alternative to Tory "command-and-control" party politics. Like Stelmach, Smith won't release her list of her donors, hardly advancing the cause of accountability and transparency. "That's just not good enough anymore," Chapman says.

Also, the Wildrose Alliance is mostly about an economic policy that's tied to conventional oil and gas, a sector that is declining, Chapman says. "She's smart. But we need to go some place else coming out of Copenhagen," said Chapman, adding that her party is light on environmental policy.

Alberta had one other highly successful premier who came from the ranks of journalism. But Ralph Klein ran the city of Calgary for six years before taking the leap into provincial politics. Smith has seven years in newspapers and television before running a local CFIB office. Experience? That's where her team comes in, she says.

John Murdoch also downplays Smith's lack of experience in running a big organization. "I don't think (Saskatchewan Premier) Brad Wall had much experience either," he says.

Byfield says the province is closer to the moment of change than at any time since Peter Lougheed defeated Social Credit. After the Wildrose Alliance won the Calgary byelection in September, "the attitude on the golf courses of Edmonton shifted overnight, " he adds.

Smith also acknowledges she may simply be dividing the provincial Conservatives, as the Reform did federally. But that doesn't bother her.

"We may be setting up the same pattern provincially. But that's OK," says Smith. "The PC party needs to be shaken up, but it can't be changed from within."

Key Dates

- - October, 2002: Alberta Alliance Party formed. Former Social Credit leader Randy Thornsteinson chosen as leader a few months later.

- - Nov. 19, 2005: Paul Hinman (MLA in Cardson-Taber-Warener) replaces Thornsteinson as leader.

- - June 23, 2007: Wildrose Party formed after the Alliance fails to make gains in byelections.

- - Oct. 24, 2007: Stelmach government releases royalty review.

- - January 19, 2008 -The two parties merged to form the Wildrose Alliance Party.

- - March 2, 2008: Provincial general election. The Wildrose Alliance loses its only seat, the one held by leader Paul Hinman, to the Stelmach Tories who take 72 seats.

- - April, 2008: Danielle Smith has lunch with John Murdoch of the Wildrose Alliance.

- - Sept. 14, 2009: Wildrose Alliance candidate Paul Hinman wins the Calgary-Glenmore byelection; the Tories come in third.

- - Nov. 7, 2009: Provincial Tories give Ed Stelmach a strong, 77-per-cent endorsement at a leadership review vote.

- - Oct. 17, 2009: Danielle Smith wins the Wildrose Alliance leadership against Mark Dyrholme.

Letters
H1N1 reminds us of the importance of immunization programs
Edmonton Journal
297 words
20 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
A15
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

Re: "H1N1 claims Calgary teen; Youth had no existing illness," The Journal, Dec. 17.

A 16-year-old was Alberta's 65th H1N1 death and it is a striking reminder of the importance of vaccines.

While more than 1.1 million Albertans have been vaccinated against H1N1, we should take a moment to remember the millions of parents who are forced by poverty to stand idle while their children die from easily preventable diseases for lack of access to basic and inexpensive immunizations.

Last month, the director general of the World Health Organization, Margaret Chen, made this comparison: "The H1N1 pandemic draws attention to the promise and dynamism of vaccine development today. Yet it also reminds us once again of the obstacles of bringing the benefits of science to people in the poorest nations."

According to a recent WHO report, vaccines are more readily available than ever and the impact has been profound. Measles deaths alone decreased by 74 per cent from 2000 to 2007. Moreover, the WHO reports that if all the vaccines now available against childhood diseases were widely adopted, an additional two million deaths a year could be prevented among children under five years by 2015.

The Canadian International Immunization Initiative (CIII) is a home grown Canadian initiative that has been a leader in ensuring the provision of basic immunizations to needy children in countries around the world. Right now, decisions are being made within CIDA that will determine whether or not CIII is renewed. It is critical that we act immediately to ensure this initiative continues to save lives. All parliamentarians and parties need to be aware of this program and provide support to ensure the CIII continues.

Jimmy Wang, Edmonton

Get vaccinated
Edmonton Journal
138 words
20 December 2009
Edmonton Journal

Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

I recently heard that there are approximately 1.5 million Albertans, including the premier, who have not received the H1N1 flu shot

Dr. Gerry Predy, senior medical officer of health with Alberta Health Services, has been seen many times on television asking people to get the shot. I don't think this flu has reached its peak yet. There are still a lot of winter months in which flu can break out at any time.

My wife and I received the seasonal and the H1N1 flu shots.

I urge all Albertans to get the shot to protect not only themselves but also for their loved ones. The province has said that the clinics will only be open for one more week. Come on, Albertans, let's get the H1N1 vaccination.

Jim Hawthorne, Edmonton

BILL BROWNSTEIN
Arts & Life
Deal or no deal, don't shake hands
BILL BROWNSTEIN
The Gazette
939 words
20 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
A18 / BREAK
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

Credit Howie Mandel for summing up his life in six words: Here's the Deal - Don't Touch Me. The first three words are a reference to his hit TV game show Deal or No Deal; the latter three, to his germaphobia.

Here's the Deal - Don't Touch Me (Bantam; $29.95) is also the title of Mandel's surprisingly revealing bio, written by him and Josh Young. The book focuses on the Toronto-born Mandel's lifelong battle with OCD (obsessive-compulsive disorder) and ADHD (attention deficit hyperactivity disorder), which go a long way toward explaining the man's manic comic style, both on the chuckles and the game-show circuit. The accent here is much more on dysfunction than funny.

Howie Hughes had little over Howie Mandel - on the germaphobia front. Which triggered both their OCD conditions. Mandel was a mess before the swine-flu crisis. Can't imagine how he is faring now. Perhaps exchanging high-fives with his feet.

Although Mandel has been plagued with OCD all his life, his condition was made public only a decade back. And not quite in the manner Mandel the control-freak - there's another issue - would have liked. He was on Howard Stern's radio show, and already some can see a recipe for disaster brewing.

Turns out Mandel was sharing the spotlight on Stern's program with a fellow who had a special talent. Evidently, the guy had been endowed with a penis the size of a redwood, and to prove the point, he whipped it out. Mandel's first reaction was one of envy. But that was swiftly followed by one of panic. Seems the fellow, after doing something akin to baton exercises, didn't wash his hands and opened the door to leave the studio.

Mandel was frozen with fear. He couldn't bring himself to touch that doorknob when it was his turn to exit. He stayed trapped in the studio. The ever-sensitive Stern was amused. Mandel's only way out was to announce his OCD on air with Stern - not quite the optimum venue to reveal a serious psychological disorder.

It's a wonder Mandel, 54, is not living in a bubble. At the age of 4, he was keeping many of Toronto's medical professionals in business. He was allergic to dairy products, suffered from "seeping" eczema and constant ear infections, and was a bed-wetter - the result of his ADHD. And, oh yeah, he was a maniacal attention-seeker, which may account for his choice of career.

By the time he entered school, teachers feared he was somewhat slow, because he didn't tie his shoelaces when they got untied. But this had nothing to do with his pre-existing conditions - from lactose intolerance to seeping eczema. Mandel simply refused to touch his shoelaces after they were dragged through an array of germs in the classroom and schoolyard.

Naturally, when his parents took him away on vacation to Miami, a colony of sand flies decided to change residence from the beach to his legs, leading to many trips to the dermatologist.

So perhaps now you understand why Mandel won't shake hands with anyone. Evidently, he was somehow able to overcome his germaphobia in order to make babies with his wife, Terry, but it's safe to say few would want to know his modus operandi in that department.

Not surprisingly, considering his quirks, Mandel got and lost many jobs as a teen and young man - everything from running rides at the Canadian National Exhibition to bingo-calling. Nor did it help that he never finished high school, let alone go to college. The best he could do was become a carpet salesman. Oh, and it should be mentioned that Mandel was colour-blind, too, which can negatively impact the task of selling carpets.

At an age when all his cronies were charting a path in life, Mandel wasn't charting and had no path. His only goal was to become a millionaire, and it didn't seem likely that the wonderful world of broadloom was going to bring him great riches.

Show-biz was the farthest thing from his mind as a young man. Mandel's idea of high comedy was Candid Camera. Really. Still, his friends thought he was funny, in spite of - or perhaps because of - his myriad disorders. They dragged him to a comedy club for an open mike night with amateurs who actually coveted a career in comedy.

And the rest is history. In Mandel's case, it was hysteria that had him leap into the limelight. This was not a subscriber to the Lenny Bruce/George Carlin school of comedy. Mandel was no one's notion of cerebral. He would get up on stage, often with a surgical - naturally - glove stretched out on his head or sporting a giant diaper, and enact his version of Tourette Syndrome in swearing a blue streak. Whatever. It obviously worked.

Mandel soon uprooted to Hollywood. He got major gigs in Vegas. He landed films and a starring role in the acclaimed TV drama St.

Elsewhere, playing - what else but - a doctor. He created the award-winning kiddie cartoon-series Bobby's World. He proved skeptics wrong again by striking gold in Deal or No Deal. And he's had to deal with a variety of maladies along the way.

It just may be that good health - mental and physical - is much over-rated. Purell, anyone?

bbrownst@thegazette.canwest.com

News
Cutback plan dropped overnight; Government says it will no longer cancel mentally ill patients' toiletries, snacks after Edmonton MLA contacts health minister
Archie McLean
Edmonton Journal
551 words
20 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
A1
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

A plan to save $70,000 a year by taking free toiletries and snacks away from mentally ill patients was overturned by the Alberta government Saturday in a hasty flurry of goodwill.

"Something like this only adds to the feelings of stress and uncertainty that both patients and families are feeling right now," Fred Horne, MLA for Edmonton-Rutherford, said about the new cost-saving measures at Alberta Hospital Edmonton.

"It will be rescinded immediately."

Horne first learned of the policy by reading Saturday's Journal. He then spoke by phone to Health Minister Ron Liepert, a member of the premier's office staff and finally Alberta Health Services president and CEO Stephen Duckett, who was asked to kill the plan.

The policy was supposed to save $70,000 for the government by eliminating free items such as toothbrushes, face soap, feminine hygiene products, razors and nail clippers, as well as coffee and snacks for patients who live at the hospital.

Critics blasted the idea as a petty and mean-spirited way to save money for the health department, which is facing a $1.2-billion deficit.

A spokeswoman for Alberta Health Services on Friday described the move as "part of aligning and standardizing our supplies across the province."

Horne is the premier's representative on a committee currently looking at how to implement the closure of 246 acute care beds at the hospital in northeast Edmonton. He expressed regret on behalf of the committee.

"We want patients and their families to know that they're at the centre of this process," Horne said.

NDP Leader Brian Mason said the flip-flop means the government is sensitive to recent polls showing their support falling across the province. He said the original policy was petty, small and unsurprising, given the government's approach to cutting spending.

The senior's ministry recently announced a $6-million clawback of funding to agencies that support people with developmental disabilities.

"They always turn on the most vulnerable among us," Mason said.

Liberal Leader David Swann said both the original policy and the hasty turnaround make the government look incompetent in the midst of major restricting of the health department.

"It's extremely embarrassing," Swann said. "The government was nickel-and-diming these people when they overspent for years."

Swann said the change of course raises questions about who is making policy, when a backbench MLA is able to reverse AHS policy with a few phone calls.

The Liberals have been calling all fall for Health Minister Ron Liepert to resign over his handling of the province's health reforms and the H1N1 vaccination program.

Austin Mardon, a prominent mental health patient activist who also has schizophrenia, applauded the government for acting so quickly to reverse the policy.

Mardon said little perks -- such as the toiletries and especially caffeine to counteract the sedative effects of some medication -- go a long way to help patients, many of whom are forced to live a the hospital.

"I know the government is under pressure to tighten its belt, but hopefully we can find another way," Mardon said.

amclean@thejournal.canwest.com

Sunday Sports
Give 3 points for win in regulation time
ELLIOTT PAP
Canwest News Service
848 words
20 December 2009
Montreal Gazette
MTLG
Final
B2
English
Copyright © 2009 Montreal Gazette

Forget the H1N1 virus, there is a bigger epidemic in the National Hockey League called the OTL.

The OTL, or overtime loss, is the official designation for a team that drops a game in the extra period or shootout.

You can't win in 60 minutes, you can't win in overtime and you can't win in a shootout, but you still get a "loser point" for not losing in 60 minutes.

It's wacky and, heading into last night's action, a whopping 138 "loser points" had already been awarded this season.

Dallas led the way with 11, followed by Tampa with nine.

Last season, 282 points were awarded to teams that couldn't win.

Now, we have no problem with the "loser point" if the NHL deep thinkers feel playing someone even for 60 minutes is worth a reward.

Our problem is that some games are worth two points - that's for the old-fashioned victory in 60 minutes - and others are worth three when it goes beyond 60.

The obvious solution is to award three points for a regulation victory and make all games of equal value.

Traditionalists may scoff at the three-point solution, but what kind of tradition is it when 138 points are given to teams that lost?

The loser point has skewed the standings and the only way to counter it is to reward three points for a regulation victory.

Funny, when this theory was put to two team captains last weekend, it received no support.

We polled a Canadian, an American, a Finn and a retired goalie and nobody was buying our seemingly sensible solution.

"I don't mind the way it is," Canucks captain Roberto Luongo said. "Obviously, it's not in our favour right now and it's a little frustrating but, you know, that's the way it goes. We have to find a way, when we're down a goal, to bring games to overtime and take it from there.

"It's not helping us right now, but that's not the system's fault. That's our fault."

Wild captain Mikko Koivu, the Finn in our little poll, admitted he doesn't spend much time sweating this sort of stuff.

"I don't know about three points for a win, I haven't really thought about it," he said.

"I know in Europe they have three points, but I like the way it is here. If you get to the shootout, I think for sure you should get one point. Maybe if you lose in overtime, you shouldn't get any."

(Fat chance that would ever come to pass, Mikko, because coaches would order their players to sit back and protect the point through the overtime.)

Ryan Kesler, a Canucks centre and impending U.S. Olympian, wasn't the least bit interested in a three-point regulation victory.

"No," he replied emphatically. "That wouldn't work. That's too much like European soccer. Pretty soon, you're going to have 10 points for a win, then 15 points for a loss. I think it works the way it is now. It's hard to win games in the NHL. You battle hard for that one point, and I think you deserve it if you tie a game through 60 minutes."

The three-point win would actually add drama to the standings, not lessen it. If teams desperately needed an extra point to finish first or make the playoffs, they would go all-out in regulation rather than letting it get to OT or a shootout.

Kesler wasn't buying that, either.

"I just like it the way it is," he said. "Some teams are going to play a lot of games where they lose in overtime and might make the playoffs because of it. But they worked hard for 60 minutes to get that point."

Retired goalie Kevin Weekes, now a rookie commentator on Hockey Night in Canada, also endorsed the current system, but conceded that a three-point regulation victory is at least worthy of discussion.

"I think it's a little bit of a novel concept," Weekes said. "I think it always takes a lot of people in the hockey world to get their heads around something when it comes to change.

"One proposal I heard the other night with regards to the OT situation is maybe go 4-on-4, 3-on-3, 2-on-2, then 1-on-1. I think that's pretty neat. I think that's exciting. Not to suggest that the shootout isn't exciting, but I think in this instance teams would play harder and it's a more natural way for teams to try to get that point."

We'll vote that one down and stick to our guns: three points for a regulation win, two points for an overtime or shootout win, one point for an OTL.

In our world, all games are created equal.

Vancouver Sun

News
Latest poll backs government handling of H1N1 vaccinations
Allison Jones
THE CANADIAN PRESS
376 words
20 December 2009
The Toronto Star
TOR
ONT
A13
English
Copyright (c) 2009 The Toronto Star

Canadians think governments at all three levels have done an okay job of handling the H1N1 pandemic, although many also think the risk was exaggerated, a new poll suggests.

The Canadian Press Harris-Decima poll suggests that, as concern wanes among Canadians, they generally are satisfied with how governments responded.

Nearly three-quarters (73 per cent) of respondents assessed Ottawa's performance as good or fair; numbers were similar at the provincial and municipal levels. That's up from 61 per cent last month, when there were distribution challenges with the vaccine and lineups at clinics were hours long.

"It struck me that some of the things that were most likely to be causing people a problem at the last time that we asked had been taken care of," said Doug Anderson, Harris-Decima senior vice-president.

Provincially, the biggest change was 41 per cent of respondents in Alberta, saying their government was handling H1N1 poorly, as opposed to 61 per cent one month ago, when controversy had erupted over Calgary Flames players and their families getting the vaccine when others found it difficult to get.

Now, many clinics have closed and the rate of new infections seems to be dropping, based on hospitalizations, with declines reported in all provinces and territories.

Among those surveyed, just 9 per cent said they were very concerned about H1N1, down from 11 per cent two weeks ago and 22 per cent three weeks before that.

The survey found most who responded either had had the inoculation already or had decided not to, leaving 12 per cent who said they still intend to be vaccinated.

The telephone survey of about 1,000 Canadians was conducted Dec. 10 to 13. It has a margin of error of 3.1 per cent, 19 times out of 20.

Days after the survey, Canada's chief public health officer, Dr. David Butler-Jones, warned against complacency, saying a third wave could come in the new year.

The survey suggests most Canadians have made up their minds about the vaccine; 40 per cent of respondents received the shot and 45 per cent said they would not get it.

7 Things you might have missed this week -
528 words
20 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
A7
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

It's official

Reality-TV stars Jon and Kate Gosselin, more famous this year for marital discord than the family harmony their show was supposedly about, were officially divorced. Aside from deciding who would look after the eight children, the consequences for their showbiz endeavours are sure to keep people talking.

Got the shot? Not so much

Despite a huge public awareness campaign and long lineups to get a shot of H1N1 vaccine, only 35 per cent of Manitobans have done so. This is on par with the percentage in other provinces, but Dr. Joel Kettner, Manitoba's chief provincial public health officer, would like to see everyone get the shot. "At this time of year, when people are indoors and meeting new people, it's even more important to get vaccinated."

He scores... a spot on the team

Travis Hamonic got a golden opportunity to show what he can do when he was the lone Manitoban picked for Team Canada at the World Junior Hockey Championship that begins Dec. 26 in Saskatoon. The 19-year-old defenceman was passed over for the spot last year, but caught the attention of Team Canada's head scout Al Murray: "Travis can do so many things. He can be a shutdown guy, a combination of an offensive-defensive guy and he's shown the ability to be one of our power-play guys." Could be one to watch.

A Gaiman island

As in Neil Gaiman, famed fantasist and acclaimed writer of books such as American Gods, the Sandman series, and Coraline. The British author, who now lives in St. Paul, Minn., spoke to fans and signed books at McNally Robinson Polo Park, which had won a North American-wide contest to attract him here. Six hundred people turned up, with no advertising for the event. Winnipeggers like to read -- who's next?

Nice ice, baby

Well we're always aiming to beat Ottawa with our longest skating rink in Canada, but this year river rink guru Paul Jordon plans to make the skating smoother. Using a technique for building winter roads up north, Jordan will use water pumps to deal with slushy frazil ice on the Assiniboine River. Lace up your skates!

Is there no decency?

Ah, the Internet: boon to society or instrument of evil? It depends. Aside from a survey that found many teens were searching for porn online, it seems the Liberal Party of Canada needs to keep a closer watch at what gets posted on its website. A third-party image showing Prim Minister Stephen Harper getting shot (his face in place of Lee Harvey Oswald's) did not leave the Torries amused.

Play ball

After the long ordeal that Gordon Bell High School went through to get a little green space added to its grounds, it seemed it was moot at best. The area they got would be too small for a sports field. But it came to light, thanks to University of WInnipeg cartographer Prof. Weldon Hiebert, that if you place the lines just right (and close a side street), a football field works just fine.

Comment
H1N1 and the global power of vaccines
Elizabeth Payne
Special to Times Colonist
696 words
20 December 2009
Victoria Times Colonist
VTC
Final
D2
English
Copyright © 2009 Victoria Times Colonist

You might be forgiven for thinking the vaccine was a new invention after witnessing the fuss over the H1N1 immunization campaign this fall.

People agonized over whether or not to get it, consulted friends, neighbours and the Internet and talked about it endlessly.

In the end, close to 47 per cent of Ottawa residents lined up to get jabbed, which is significantly more than get an annual flu shot. Fussing aside, the H1N1 vaccine campaign has been, and continues to be, a success.

That is good. What is better is that the campaign might have reminded those of us lucky enough to be able to take vaccines for granted what modern miracles they are. Vaccines have changed the world. And they continue to do so.

Vaccines are so much a part of the way we do things in Canada that most of us give them very little thought once our children are beyond the childhood immunization stage -- which might explain the out-of-proportion concerns about the H1N1 vaccine.

The H1N1 vaccine campaign is not over yet and federal chief public health officer Dr. David Butler-Jones is warning Canadians who are not yet immunized not to get complacent.

But all our recent focus on this particular vaccine might give Canadians a renewed appreciation for what vaccines have done for us and can do for us in the future.

And, more importantly, what they can do for people in parts of the world who don't have the luxury of being complacent about public health.

Just last week, World Health Organization officials began a campaign to immunize 2.8 million Afghan children under the age of five against polio, a disease that has largely disappeared in Canada but that continues to plague some of the poorest countries in the world.

The new vaccine is expected to be used on tens of millions of children in India and Nigeria by early next year. International health officials have been working since the late 1980s to eradicate polio, which a few countries have been unable to shake. The oral vaccine that immunizes against several strains of the disease makes that goal possible.

In Uganda, trials are about to begin on a new experimental tuberculosis vaccine -- the first new one to be developed in almost 100 years. The old one is not very effective anymore and tuberculosis is a growing international scourge that kills one person every 20 seconds.

And work continues, slowly, on the development of an AIDS vaccine. In September, there was excitement over the results of a vaccine trial that seemed to offer some protection against the disease, but that has since been tempered.

An effective vaccine is likely still years away.

There are many ways to prevent people from succumbing to deadly diseases, some of them simple.

Mosquito nets significantly reduce malaria deaths, condoms significantly reduce AIDS transmission, drugs successfully control many diseases.

But in poor and especially war-torn parts of the world, disease alleviation through drugs and other means is often difficult or impossible. And conditions make people more susceptible to illness.

That is where vaccines can have the biggest impact.

Which is why the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, among other organizations, has made vaccine development and access in the developing world a priority.

Almost three years ago, Prime Minister Stephen Harper held a press conference with Bill Gates to announce funding support for the global effort to develop an AIDS vaccine. It is good to see the federal support for the initiative.

But Canada can do more. Even as health officials mull over what should be done with the excess doses of H1N1 vaccine stockpiled around the country, Canada has yet to commit to giving extra vaccine to developing countries.

The government now says a decision will be made early in the new year.

Canada's seeming reluctance to share its excess flu vaccine, as other countries have done, is depressing, especially at a time when Canadians have been reminded of the extraordinary power of vaccines.

Elizabeth Payne is a member of the Ottawa Citizen's editorial board.

Four for Mother Earth Best thing about these resolutions they're keepable -
Mary Agnes Welch
1343 words
19 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
H16
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

You've trained yourself to

remember the cloth bags when

you get groceries. You caulked

all your windows. All your light

bulbs are compact fluorescents. You

at least feel a twinge of guilt about the

carbon emissions from your flight to

Cancun.

But without a binding

agreement in Copenhagen

and Canada's

green reputation in

tatters, it's time to take

the next few baby steps.

In the build-up to the

United Nations Climate

Change Conference,

there was chatter that, for all our noble

intentions, individual actions will never

be enough to forestall catastrophic

global warming. What's needed is a

coordinated government crackdown on

polluters.

But, according to Statistics Canada,

direct and indirect household emissions

account for 46 per cent of Canada's total

greenhouse gas output, a fact noted by

proponents of individual action.

"We need both streams complimenting

each other," said Adria Vasil, author of

Ecoholic Home and a green columnist

for Toronto's Now Magazine. "We need

government clamping down on big emitters,

but we also need to clamp down on

ourselves."

If lobbying for a new wind farm or a

continent-wide cap and trade system is

beyond your ken, and you're not quite

ready to spend $15,000 installing a

geothermal heat system, here are four

simple things you can do in 2010 that

don't require endless trips to Rona, a

weekend of work or a bajillion dollars.

UNPLUG EVERYTHING

Your cable set-top box can suck

up more power than your fridge,

even when you're not watching

television. Startling, eh?

It's called phantom load

-- the power drawn by

household gadgets even

when you think they're

turned off. That's

pretty much everything

that can be

turned on with a remote

or that has an

internal clock. And

it's your pile of cell

phone chargers,

Ipod chargers,

battery chargers

and laptop cords

that leach power

constantly if they're

plugged in. It's also

video game consoles

like the Wii and

Xbox. Some of those

consume 185 watts of

power when they're

just junked up under

the TV.

Phantom load accounts

for between five and 10 per

cent of all the power used by the

average household, says Tracy Moroz,

Manitoba Hydro's residential programs marketing

specialist.

Granted, Manitoba's power is pretty green

since it's produced by water not coal or natural

gas. But because the province's power rates

are so cheap, Manitobans aren't great at conservation.

The more power Manitobans save,

the more green power is available for export,

which offsets the need for coal or natural gas

plants in the United States.

Moroz has a list of easy to-dos that will

actually save you money.

. Walk around the house and unplug every

charger, appliance and electronic gadget that's

not in frequent use.

. Next time you buy a new appliance, don't

just look for the Energy Star rating but also

ask yourself whether you need all the bells and

whistles like timers and remote controls. Most

kitchens suffer from digital clock overkill --

there's one on the stove, the microwave and the

coffee maker. Keeping appliances simple saves

phantom power.

. Get yourself some power bars. It's annoying

to rustle around behind the television looking

for the dusty plug every time you want to

watch Entertainment Tonight, so the best thing

to do is plug all your TV gadgets into a power

bar that's easy to switch on and off. Same with

computers and printers that don't get used all

the time.

Set top boxes are a lingering puzzle. Unplugging

them usually means losing all your channel

preferences. Manitoba Hydro and other

power companies are working with manufacturers

and cable companies on set top boxes

that could one day use just one watt of power

instead of the 45 watts some of them suck right

now.

BE A SEMI-VEGETARIAN

If the 100-mile diet is out because you just

can't get through a Winnipeg winter without

coffee, chocolate and wine, there are still tons

of ways to eat more sustainably.

A vegetarian diet is ideal, but Vasil says even

reducing the amount of meat you eat -- think

Meatless Mondays and Tofu Tuesdays -- can

make a difference.

Eating less beef is a start. Manitoba's hog

farmers, often criticized for huge factory

farms and stinky sewage lagoons, will be

glad to hear that pork has about a quarter the

carbon footprint of beef, largely because of the

methane produced by cow farts and the fertilizer

needed for grazing.

Even better is free-range chicken, and even

better than that is supporting small, local

organic producers, many of whom sell their

products at local independent butchers.

"We just ordered a quarter of a bison and a

large local turkey for our Christmas dinner,"

said Kreesta Doucette, executive director of

Food Matters Manitoba. "If you have the time,

connect with a local farmer."

Same with vegetables. Try to buy what's in

season locally instead of vegetables trucked in

from afar.

"See if it's made in Manitoba and if it doesn't

say, ask," said Doucette.

But Vasil points out one exception to the

"buy local" mantra. Greenhouses suck up

incredible amounts of energy, so the organic

tomato trucked up from Mexico might actually

be a greener bet than one grown in a greenhouse.

TAKE THE BUS ONCE A WEEK

Winnipeggers love to complain

about the transit system. The buses are

old, the routes into far-flung suburbs are

spotty, there's no rapid transit system and

someone with H1N1 might cough on you.

But experts say taking public transit or

biking to work is the single best thing you

can do for the environment.

If you suck it up, put on your longjohns,

pop on your Ipod and take the bus just one

day a week, you could be saving at least 123

kilograms of greenhouse gas emissions a

year.

According to Statistics Canada, the average

commute in Winnipeg is 6.1 kilometres.

Even if you left your very fuel efficient

2009 Toyota Corolla at home once a week,

you would still save about 2.45 kilograms of

CO2 emissions a day, according to the U.S.

Federal Transit Administration's carbon

calculator.

That would also save you about $35 a year

in gas, which would help offset the cost of

bus fare.

PURGE THE PACKAGING

Everyone who has tried to break into a

new batch of razors or batteries has cursed

the layers and layers of hard plastic packaging.

Vasil suggests making a grand statement

by leaving the bulky packaging at the

grocery store till when you check out or

mailing it to politicians. If you're too shy to

do that, try this:

. Steer-clear of prewashed veggies like the

tubs of lettuce or the bags of salad. Veggies

from the shelf are greener and cheaper, especially

if you can get away without using

one of those flimsy plastic bags.

. Do it Costco-style, if you can. Buy a huge

tub of peanut butter or one mega-box of

cereal instead of lots of smaller ones. But

make sure the bulk versions really do have

less packaging. There's nothing worse

than realizing each of the soaps in your

four-pack comes in its own box, wrapped

together in paper and then plastic shrinkwrapped.

And, when buying big plastic containers,

make sure they have the recycling

triangle stamped on the bottom so they can

go in the Blue Box.

. Next time you go to Rae and Jerry's, bring

your own Tupperware for the leftover onion

rings instead of taking the disposable containers.

As an experiment, Vasil once sent

an intern to all the big fast-food joints to

test whether they'd use the interns plastic

containers for take-out.

"None of them flinched," said Vasil.

"They just said 'OK' and filled it up."

maryagnes.welch@freepress.mb.ca

Four for Mother Earth -
By Mary Agnes Welch
1337 words
19 December 2009
Winnipeg Free Press
WFP
H16
English
All material copyright Winnipeg Free Press, a division of FP Canadian Newspapers Limited Partnership. All rights reserved.

It's called phantom load -- the power drawn by household gadgets even when you think they're turned off. That's pretty much everything that can be turned on with a remote or that has an internal clock. And it's your pile of cell phone chargers, Ipod chargers, battery chargers and laptop cords that leach power constantly if they're plugged in. It's also video game consoles like the Wii and Xbox. Some of those consume 185 watts of power when they're just junked up under the TV.

Phantom load accounts for between five and 10 per cent of all the power used by the average household, says Tracy Moroz, Manitoba Hydro's residential programs marketing specialist.

Granted, Manitoba's power is pretty green since it's produced by water not coal or natural gas. But because the province's power rates are so cheap, Manitobans aren't great at conservation. The more power Manitobans save, the more green power is available for export, which offsets the need for coal or natural gas plants in the United States.

Moroz has a list of easy to-dos that will actually save you money.

Walk around the house and unplug every charger, appliance and electronic gadget that's not in frequent use.

Next time you buy a new appliance, don't just look for the Energy Star rating but also ask yourself whether you need all the bells and whistles like timers and remote controls. Most kitchens suffer from digital clock overkill -- there's one on the stove, the microwave and the coffee maker. Keeping appliances simple saves phantom power.

Get yourself some power bars. It's annoying to rustle around behind the television looking for the dusty plug every time you want to watch Entertainment Tonight, so the best thing to do is plug all your TV gadgets into a power bar that's easy to switch on and off. Same with computers and printers that don't get used all the time.

Set top boxes are a lingering puzzle. Unplugging them usually means losing all your channel preferences. Manitoba Hydro and other power companies are working with manufacturers and cable companies on set top boxes that could one day use just one watt of power instead of the 45 watts some of them suck right now.

Be a semi-vegetarian

If the 100-mile diet is out because you just can't get through a Winnipeg winter without coffee, chocolate and wine, there are still tons of ways to eat more sustainably.

A vegetarian diet is ideal, but Vasil says even reducing the amount of meat you eat -- think Meatless Mondays and Tofu Tuesdays -- can make a difference.

Eating less beef is a start. Manitoba's hog farmers, often criticized for huge factory farms and stinky sewage lagoons, will be glad to hear that pork has about a quarter the carbon footprint of beef, largely because of the methane produced by cow farts and the fertilizer needed for grazing.

Even better is free-range chicken, and even better than that is supporting small, local organic producers, many of whom sell their products at local independent butchers.

"We just ordered a quarter of a bison and a large local turkey for our Christmas dinner," said Kreesta Doucette, executive director of Food Matters Manitoba. "If you have the time, connect with a local farmer."

Same with vegetables. Try to buy what's in season locally instead of vegetables trucked in from afar.

"See if it's made in Manitoba and if it doesn't say, ask," said Doucette.

But Vasil points out one exception to the "buy local" mantra. Greenhouses suck up incredible amounts of energy, so the organic tomato trucked up from Mexico might actually be a greener bet than one grown in a greenhouse.

Take the bus once a week

Winnipeggers love to complain about the transit system. The buses are old, the routes into far-flung suburbs are spotty, there's no rapid transit system and someone with H1N1 might cough on you.

But experts say taking public transit or biking to work is the single best thing you can do for the environment.

If you suck it up, put on your longjohns, pop on your Ipod and take the bus just one day a week, you could be saving at least 123 kilograms of greenhouse gas emissions a year.

According to Statistics Canada, the average commute in Winnipeg is 6.1 kilometres. Even if you left your very fuel efficient 2009 Toyota Corolla at home once a week, you would still save about 2.45 kilograms of CO2 emissions a day, according to the U.S. Federal Transit Administration's carbon calculator.

That would also save you about $35 a year in gas, which would help offset the cost of bus fare.

Purge the packaging

Everyone who has tried to break into a new batch of razors or batteries has cursed the layers and layers of hard plastic packaging.

Vasil suggests making a grand statement by leaving the bulky packaging at the grocery store till when you check out or mailing it to politicians. If you're too shy to do that, try this:

Steer-clear of prewashed veggies like the tubs of lettuce or the bags of salad. Veggies from the shelf are greener and cheaper, especially if you can get away without using one of those flimsy plastic bags.

Do it Costco-style, if you can. Buy a huge tub of peanut butter or one mega-box of cereal instead of lots of smaller ones. But make sure the bulk versions really do have less packaging. There's nothing worse than realizing each of the soaps in your four-pack comes in its own box, wrapped together in paper and then plastic shrink-wrapped. And, when buying big plastic containers, make sure they have the recycling triangle stamped on the bottom so they can go in the Blue Box.

Next time you go to Rae and Jerry's, bring your own Tupperware for the leftover onion rings instead of taking the disposable containers. As an experiment, Vasil once sent an intern to all the big fast-food joints to test whether they'd use the interns plastic containers for take-out.

"None of them flinched," said Vasil. "They just said 'OK' and filled it up."

DOLPHINS AT TITANS

Line: Titans by three

Tennessee has won six of seven to get in the playoff chase and hopes to have QB Vince Young back from a hamstring injury he suffered last week. If he can't go, it will be Kerry Collins handing off to NFL rushing leader Chris Johnson, who operates vs. the league's 13th-ranked run defence. Miami QB Chad Henne has completed 61.7 per cent of his passes in victories over New England and Jacksonville the last two games.

Titans 26 Dolphins 16

PATRIOTS AT BILLS

Line: Patriots by 7-1/2

New England has been sputtering but gets its favourite foe here, as the Patriots have beaten Buffalo 12 straight times. Look for Pats QB Tom Brady to target Randy Moss, who has been criticized by foes as a quitter, early in this one, then try to establish a ground game vs. the NFL's worst run defence. Also look for Bills QB Ryan Fitzpatrick to try to open up the passing game, because New England's pass defence has been woeful on the road, a good reason the team is 1-5 away from home.

Patriots 27 Bills 23

CARDINALS AT LIONS

Line: Cardinals by 12-1/2

Arizona tries to bounce back from its atrocious, seven-turnover outing Monday in San Francisco and clinches the NFC West title if it wins and the 49ers fall in Philadelphia. The Cards expect to have leading WR Larry Fitzgerald back from leg injuries he suffered last week to face the NFL's worst pass defence. Even if he's out, look for QB Kurt Warner to exploit that unit. Detroit is minus top RB Kevin Smith, who suffered a season-ending knee injury last week, and probably will be without QB Matthew Stafford (shoulder), meaning Daunte Culpepper would start again.

Cardinals 27 Lions 13.

Line: Eagles by 8-1/2

Philadelphia goes for its fifth straight victory as it's on the verge of a playoff slot and WR DeSean Jackson is the biggest threat, with eight TDs of 50 yards or more. Fellow WR Jeremy Maclin (foot) is out, but fellow WR Kevin Curtis might be back from a knee ailment that has sidelined him most of the year. RB Brian Westbrook (concussion) also is out. The 49ers have lost five in a row on the road. RB Frank Gore is coming off a big game but faces the NFL's 10th-rated run defence.

Eagles 26 49ers 20

FALCONS AT JETS

NY expects to have QB Mark Sanchez back from a knee injury that sidelined him last week. The key will be how RB Thomas Jones (11 TDs, 4.4 per carry) fares vs. a defence that is 23rd vs. the run but has allowed only about 90 yards a game in its last four outings.

Jets 23 Falcons 20

BROWNS AT CHIEFS

Yuk. Two of the worst teams in the NFL meet. Kansas City gets back WR Dwayne Bowe from a four-game suspension. Chiefs RB Jamaal Charles, coming off a 143-yard outing, faces the No. 29 run defence. Cleveland upset Pittsburgh in its last outing but is 1-6 on the road and its offence is woeful. Flip a coin.

Chiefs 17 Browns 13

TEXANS AT RAMS

The Rams were battling swine flu late in the week (insert punchline here for a one-win team). Matt Schaub, who has thrown for a team-record 3,814 yards, faces a defence allowing 27 points per game, 30th in the league. It's hard to see the depleted Rams, who manage just 11.2 ppg (worst in the NFL) keeping pace.

Texans 30 Rams 13

BENGALS AT CHARGERS

Cincinnati will try to focus just three days after WR Chris Henry died, and the players will wear patches with his number (15). The Bengals will try to wrap up the AFC North title but face a team that's won eight in a row and clinches a playoff spot if it wins. San Diego is fifth in passing but Cincy is decent (12th) in pass defence. The Bengals will pound RB Cedric Benson at the 21st-rated run defence.

Chargers 24 Bengals 20

RAIDERS AT BRONCOS

Line: Broncos by 14

Denver tries to rebound from a loss at Indy, and with its RB corps banged up, expect more work for WR Brandon Marshall, who recorded 21 catches last week (NFL record), as Oakland is in the middle of the pack (17th) vs. the pass. The Raiders turn to journeyman QB Charlie Frye because Bruce Gradkowski is out (knee ailments) and former No. 1 pick JaMarcus Russell has been a huge bust.

Broncos 23 Raiders 10

BUCCANEERS AT SEAHAWKS

Line: Seahawks by 6-1/2

Seattle was blown out at Houston last week but now comes home to face a foe that's 0-6 on the road, has lost 16 of its last 17 outings overall, the offence has a grand total of nine points in its last two contests, and rookie QB Josh Freeman is struggling. Seattle hasn't protected QB Matt Hasselbeck, and Tampa despite its woes is seventh vs. the pass. Seahawks 23 Buccaneers 13

Roethlisberger might be under the gun again because Chris Kemoeatu is injured and out. The Steelers' pass defence isn't the same without safety Troy Polamalu, who is out again (knee), although it is No. 1 vs. the run. Green Bay has the NFL's second-ranked run defence and QB Aaron Rodgers is clicking.

Packers 23 Steelers 20

BEARS AT RAVENS

Line: Ravens by 11

Ravens 24 Bears 16

Vikings 23 Panthers 16

Redskins 24 Giants 23
