Julie Blais
Lifestyle
Holiday etiquette; Regift, buy in bulk, skip the hug for a fistbump. Welcome to recessionary, germfree manners
Julie Blais
Canwest News Service; Ottawa Citizen
1280 words
19 December 2009
Edmonton Journal
EDJR
Final
G2
English
Copyright © 2009 Edmonton Journal

Before you start making that list, checking it twice, signing your cards and buying gifts for your children's teachers, take etiquette expert Julie Blais Comeau's holiday quiz. Give your gut responses for each of the scenarios below, then read on to learn what to do for appropriate seasonal celebrating.

1. You exchange gifts with your best friend during a holiday drink. She presents you with a DVD of Julie & Julia. One of your colleagues gave you a copy the week before. You know your sister-in-law loved the movie. You simply change the tag and re-gift it. Is re-gifting acceptable?

2. Your son's hockey coach gives you a holiday-cheer invitation for Saturday night after your son's game. The invitation has yours and your spouse's names on it. Do you assume your children are also invited?

3. January marks the one-year anniversary in your current position. Your boss is amazing, makes work fun, and always has time for you. He is a wine connoisseur and an avid golfer. On the last day of work before your holiday break, you show your gratitude by offering a 2007 Mike Weir Pinot Noir. You feel good about your choice: You are encouraging Canadian wine-makers and proceeds from the sale of the wine will go to the Mike Weir Foundation. Is your gift appropriate?

4. It is Dec. 23 and, after a long last day before your vacation, you click your garage-door opener and pull up the driveway as your neighbour walks up to your car with gift in hand. She informs you that she and her family are leaving to spend the holidays on a beach in Cuba. You need not worry about their home, as her brother and his girlfriend will be there to care for their dog and plants. She hands you the gift bag and offers you her best wishes. You thank her gratefully, wish her a wonderful trip, season's greeting and send her a thank-you note without reciprocating a gift. Is this a good-neighbour policy?

5. Financially, it has been a difficult year and you can't afford to give even a token gift to a service provider. Can you forgo gifts this year?

6. Your gift list for your children's teachers, day-care workers and coaches could make up a baker's dozen. This year, you decide to be efficient and make the trip on your own to buy, in bulk, one gender-neutral gift in a variety of colours and styles. Your children will make personalized gift cards. Is this a good strategy?

7. You kick off the holiday season by attending a performance of The Nutcracker with your parents, aunt and uncle. Everyone meets at your home for a potluck meal. As planned, your aunt and cousins bring an appetizer. Cousin Rachel brings a gelatin cream-cheese mould in the shape of a wreath with kiwis on the surface and, for a festive touch, crushed candy-cane sprinkles. It gets put in the back of the fridge. Should you have served the dish?

8. You are a first-year university student without any family in town, and have been invited to a fellow student's house for Christmas dinner. You are a vegetarian but don't want to burden the host. Should you let your eating habits be known?

9. Friends are hosting an open house. They and their daughters have received the H1N1 vaccine. To ensure a safe celebration, the hosts have decided to have a no-handshake party and greet guests with fist bumps and hugs without kisses. Is this greeting acceptable?

10. Your parents live miles away and visit during the holidays, when they spoil your children. You graciously thank your parents, but know there is no way your kids will wear all the cute outfits. Once they leave, you give some of the toys to charity and exchange the outfits for more practical wear. Should you have kept the gifts?

AND THE ETIQUETTE EXPERT SAYS ...

1. Re-gifting used to be a complete no-no, but is generally acceptable, given the economic situation. Recycling a gift is also OK if it's store-bought with originally packaging and tags and not made especially for you. Remember, as Oprah says, "doubt means don't." If you're afraid of hurting someone's feelings, or there's a slim chance that your worlds will collide, don't.

2. Call a sitter on this one. Without the children's names on the invitation, they're not invited. The same rule applies to the office party. Generally, all invitees are listed on the invitation. If you receive a work invitation with only your name on it, go solo, especially during office hours. When in doubt, simply ask the host.

3. Although a good choice for this person, gifts to the boss are discouraged. They may be perceived as seeking personal favours and, for that reason, it's best to offer a group gift or to express sentiments in a card. Alcoholic gifts are discouraged, especially in the workplace.

4. This is appropriate if you don't want to reciprocate gifts annually with your neighbour, especially since you wrote a thank-you note. The same would apply to holiday cards. Should you receive a greeting card and not wish to include the good-wisher on your annual list, thank her the next time you speak to her. If you wish to add her to your list, reciprocate by sending your holiday card. You can always have a couple of "just in case" gifts wrapped and ready to go. Hostess gifts with a holiday theme such as centrepieces, ornaments, hand towels, or a box of chocolates are ideal.

5. Due to your current budgetary restrictions, it's OK to forgo this tradition. The main point of this gesture is the recognition of good service throughout the year. Express your appreciation in a holiday card, with or without an explanation for the absence of tip or gift. Your card could be accompanied by baked goods or a homemade craft.

6. This strategy works. Not every gift has to be different. It especially works since your children will be involved in the process and will learn the tradition of gift-giving in recognition of the efforts and affection of those around them. Holiday music CDs, ornaments, plants, or gift cards to a bookstore, coffee shop or movie theatre are good options.

7. Although it may not appeal to you and your other guests, as a hostess, you graciously accept the contribution and serve it. If you receive a box of chocolates as a hostess gift, you would serve it at dessert. On the other hand, if you receive a bottle of wine, you have the choice of saving it for later or serving it, if it pairs well with your meal.

8. It's best to inform your host and volunteer to bring a vegetarian dish. If a guest has allergies, it's imperative to advise the host.

9. Yes, greeting alternatives are acceptable. No matter what your choice is, greet everyone in the same way without discriminating.

10. Because gift receipts are often enclosed, it's easy and acceptable to take something back to the store, exchange it or offer it to charity, if you won't use or don't like it, except when it's one-of-a-kind or made especially for you.
